London-specific talent dynamics: the pool of senior L/S equity PMs at Lansdowne, Marshall Wace, Egerton, Pelham has been systematically mined by Millennium, Citadel and Point72 from 2018 onwards. A senior Lansdowne analyst moving to Citadel can expect a 2-3x pay increase plus 2-year guarantee. Many of the traditional long-biased fundamental London funds have struggled partly because the platforms poach their top talent before they become PM-grade. Career progression at a platform is more accelerated (analyst → junior PM in 3-4 years possible) versus traditional fundamental funds (analyst → PM in 7-10 years typical).

## SECTION XV: PLATFORM CAPACITY & LP ACCESS
The top platforms (Citadel, Millennium, Point72, DE Shaw) have been closed to new capital for most of 2018-2024 except for top-tier institutional LPs willing to commit large amounts with long lock-ups. Minimum investment: typically $100M+ at Citadel, $25-50M+ at Millennium, $10-25M+ at Balyasny and Schonfeld. Lock-ups: Citadel Wellington is quarterly redemption with 90-day notice, 25% quarterly gate; Millennium is quarterly with 90-day notice; both have "investor-level gates" that automatically trigger if more than X% of the fund requests redemption in a period. Allocations to new LPs are rationed heavily — allocators report waiting years to get into Citadel, often only via side pocket or slice products rather than the main fund. The classic LP allocation to platforms from a pension fund or sovereign wealth fund portfolio is 3-8% of total portfolio or 25-40% of alternatives bucket. Top global LP allocators to platforms: ADIA (Abu Dhabi, ~$15B across platforms), GIC (Singapore, ~$10B), CPPIB (Canada), CalPERS/CalSTRS, Future Fund (Australia), UK DB pension funds via consultants (Willis Towers Watson, Mercer, Cambridge Associates, Albourne), UK endowments (not large), Swiss private banks, Gulf family offices.

## SECTION XVI: POD SHOP DRAWDOWN MANAGEMENT & 2018, 2023 CASE STUDIES
Two instructive platform drawdown episodes: (1) 2018 — Balyasny had a challenging year flat to negative, triggering a 20% headcount reduction and restructuring; Citadel had a weaker year with ~8% return (vs usual 15-20%); Millennium held steady at 7%. Lesson: platform returns can compress in low-vol/low-dispersion years as pod strategies converge. (2) 2023 — Pod shops generally had a mediocre year compared to 2022 record highs; Citadel Wellington +15% (strong), Millennium +10%, Balyasny +2.4% (poor), Schonfeld +3% (poor), ExodusPoint +6% (moderate). The 2023 dispersion underscored that not all platforms deliver equally; execution and risk discipline separate leaders from laggards. Drawdown at the individual pod level is constant — a typical Millennium year sees perhaps 30% of pods with negative P&L, many of whom are terminated. The platform model distributes drawdown risk to individual PMs (who lose their jobs) rather than LPs (who see muted but positive net returns).

## SECTION XVII: PLATFORM CULTURE & WORK ENVIRONMENT
Platform culture is intensely performance-driven. PMs are expected to work 12-14 hour days minimum, with 7am start, 9pm end common in London offices. No formal corporate culture — each pod operates like a small business. Aggressive meritocracy — PMs are ranked quarterly by risk-adjusted P&L and consistently bottom-quartile performers are managed out. Inter-pod collaboration is rare and sometimes penalised (can confuse attribution). The work is intellectually demanding but repetitive — same markets, same sectors, same models refined over years. PM burnout is a significant issue; average PM career length is 15-20 years before moving to family office or retirement. Compensation compensates for the brutality — a senior PM who survives 10 years at a platform can make $30-100M lifetime earnings. Diversity is poor — the PM population is heavily male (~90%), heavily white/Asian, heavily Oxford/Cambridge/Ivy League educated. Efforts to increase diversity have been modest.

## SECTION XVIII: THE PLATFORM FEE MODEL VS TRADITIONAL HF FEE MODEL
Traditional 2-and-20: simple, predictable, GP bears operating cost risk (if fund is small, GP can be unprofitable). Pass-through: LP bears operating cost risk, GP's income scales with performance. Math comparison on $5B fund with 10% gross return: Traditional 2-and-20: $5B × 2% = $100M mgmt fee + 20% × ($500M - $100M) = $80M perf = $180M to GP; LP gets $500M - $180M = $320M net = 6.4% net. Pass-through (10% cost + 20% perf): $500M × 10% = $500M costs + 20% × $500M - $500M = 0 perf = $500M to LP; wait that's wrong. Let me redo: Pass-through has LP pay actual costs (not a flat 10%, but say 8% of AUM = $400M) + 20% performance on gross return = 20% × $500M = $100M; LP gets $500M - $400M - $100M = $0 net. Hmm that's zero. In reality the math depends on pass-through costs being lower than gross return. The pass-through is GP-favourable when gross returns are high (e.g., 25%) and LP-neutral when returns are moderate (15%) and LP-unfavourable when returns are low (single digits). The 2022 Citadel year at 38% gross: costs of ~$2B = 20% × $10B AUM = actually ~10%, then performance 20% × (38% - 10%) = 5.6%, so LP net = 38% - 10% - 5.6% = 22.4%. At 8% gross: costs 8% = -2%, so LP net is negative. That's the risk transfer.

## SECTION XIX: PLATFORM REGULATION & COMPLIANCE
Platforms operate under heavy regulatory scrutiny given their size and concentration. US: SEC Registered Investment Advisor, Form ADV annually, Form PF confidential reporting to SEC/FSOC (required for large funds), CFTC reporting for commodities. UK: FCA Authorized AIFM under AIFMD since 2014, SMCR senior manager regime with named individuals carrying personal liability. EU: AIFMD marketing passport (pre-Brexit), now need national private placement for each EU country, Ireland or Luxembourg parallel UCITS or AIF for EU marketing. Position reporting: Form 13F for US large fund equity positions (quarterly, 45-day lag), Short Position Notifications in EU/UK (≥0.2% of issued shares notified to issuer/regulator, ≥0.5% publicly disclosed), position limits on commodities (CFTC, ICE/CME, EU MAR). Compliance infrastructure: chief compliance officer (CCO) personally SEC-registered, comprehensive trading surveillance (all trades reviewed for insider trading, market manipulation, best execution), material non-public information walls between pods and across asset classes, expert network vetting (post-SAC, platforms have 5-10 compliance staff devoted solely to vetting expert network calls), recorded phone lines for all traders, mandatory training.

## SECTION XX: WHERE IT'S GOING — THE PLATFORM INDUSTRY 2025-2030
The platform model has consolidated industry AUM — top 8 platforms now manage ~$350B of the $4.2 trillion HF industry total, roughly 8%, up from 4% in 2015. Forward pressures: (1) capacity saturation — every additional dollar of capital into the same strategies reduces Sharpe; Citadel and Millennium have been selectively returning capital; (2) talent cost spiral — signing bonuses have risen 3x in 5 years, squeezing GP economics; (3) LP pushback on pass-through fees — allocators are demanding cost transparency and caps; (4) regulatory scrutiny — SEC 2023 proposed rules on private fund adviser conduct, AIFMD II in EU, FCA enhanced reporting requirements; (5) AI/ML substitution — some believe ML can replace fundamental PM pods, threatening the headcount-based model; (6) market concentration risk — when platforms all hold similar positions, an unwind can cascade across all of them simultaneously (risk flagged by Bank of England FSR 2023). Forward strategy shifts: platforms are moving into less-liquid strategies (privates, structured credit) to escape crowding, expanding into crypto (cautiously), and increasing geographic diversification (opening offices in Dubai, Tokyo, Sydney).

---

# CHAPTER 35 — THE LONG/SHORT EQUITY FUNDAMENTAL SPECIAL

## SECTION 0: THE ORIGINAL HEDGE FUND STRATEGY
Long/short equity is the oldest and largest hedge fund strategy — the one Alfred Winslow Jones pioneered in 1949 when he founded the first "hedged fund" using leverage and short sales to deliver market-neutral returns. In London specifically, the fundamental long/short equity space peaked around 2015 at roughly $200-250B across ~150 dedicated managers, and has since contracted to ~$150B as capital has migrated to platforms and passive. The strategy's core thesis: deep fundamental equity research plus the ability to short produces superior risk-adjusted returns versus long-only funds by (i) avoiding or betting against overvalued stocks, (ii) hedging market beta to isolate alpha, (iii) using leverage to amplify the alpha component. The reality post-2009: most long/short equity managers have delivered ~5-7% net CAGR versus S&P 500 at ~13%, causing massive LP disillusionment. The survivors are those who have either adapted (lower fees, higher net exposure, more concentrated) or specialised (deep sector expertise, unique sourcing edge). London's specific long/short equity scene is distinctive because European equity markets are less efficient than US (more inefficiency to exploit), regional fragmentation creates cross-border arbitrage opportunities, and concentrated ownership structures in European companies create activist and catalyst-driven situations.

## SECTION I: LONDON LONG/SHORT EQUITY AUM & FIRM MAP
The tier-1 London L/S equity managers by AUM (2024-2025): TCI Fund Management (Chris Hohn, $55B AUM) — though technically activist/concentrated long-bias rather than traditional L/S, it's the largest London equity HF. Lansdowne Partners ($12B, founded 1998 by Paul Ruddock and Steven Heinz, Berkeley Square, long/short equity including Lansdowne Developed Markets Fund and Lansdowne Energy Dynamics). Marshall Wace LLP ($65B total, of which ~$25B in traditional L/S equity funds, founded 1997 by Paul Marshall and Ian Wace, Sloane Square HQ, globally dispersed now with Hong Kong and NY offices). Egerton Capital ($18B, founded 1994 by John Armitage ex-Morgan Grenfell, Buckingham Gate, long-biased global equity with short book). Pelham Capital ($3B, founded 2006 by Ross Turner ex-Lansdowne, Knightsbridge, European fundamental L/S). Sloane Robinson LLP ($2B, founded 1993 by Hugh Sloane and George Robinson, emerging markets L/S, Mayfair). Sand Grove Capital Management ($3B, founded 2014 by Simon Davies ex-Lansdowne, Mayfair, event-driven and L/S). Theleme Partners ($4B, founded 2012 by Patrick Degorce ex-TCI co-founder, long-biased concentrated). Pentwater Capital Management (US HQ but significant London presence, $7B, event-driven L/S). Odey Asset Management ($2B at peak, but catastrophic decline after founder Crispin Odey's misconduct allegations 2023; AUM collapsed and funds closed 2023-2024, now a cautionary tale). Pharmakon Advisors (life sciences specialist). Lombard Odier Eolia (energy transition L/S). Majedie Asset Management (acquired by Liontrust 2022). TT International ($4B, multi-strategy L/S). CQS (credit-focused but includes L/S equity). Caius Capital ($2B, London credit and equity). Glendower Capital. MAN GLG (Man Group's discretionary long/short division, $14B).

## SECTION II: TCI / CHILDREN'S INVESTMENT FUND — THE LONDON GIANT
The Children's Investment Fund Management UK LLP (TCI) was founded 2003 by Chris Hohn and Patrick Degorce (Degorce subsequently left in 2012 to found Theleme Partners). TCI's founding thesis was to combine extreme fundamental research with activist engagement on a concentrated portfolio of 10-20 positions. The "Children's" name refers to Hohn's charitable commitment: a fixed percentage of TCI's gross returns (historically ~0.5% of AUM annually, equivalent to tens of millions annually) is donated to the Children's Investment Fund Foundation (CIFF), making Hohn one of the largest living philanthropists. TCI has delivered exceptional returns — net CAGR of ~18% since inception 2003 through 2024 — placing it among the best-performing hedge funds of the 21st century. Famous positions include Deutsche Börse (opposed the 2005 proposed takeover of LSE, forced strategic review), ABN AMRO (activist campaign that led to 2007 RBS-Fortis-Santander takeover, $100B transaction — the trade printed $1B+ for TCI before AMR went bust), Canadian National Railway, CSX Corporation, Alphabet, Microsoft, Visa, Moody's, Ferrovial, Safran, Airbus, Aena, Thales, Airbus, Aeroports de Paris. TCI's approach is long-biased concentrated (10-15 core positions, typically 80%+ of NAV long, 0-20% short as hedge), with holding periods 3-7 years. The fund charges 1.5% management fee and 20% performance fee above 8% hurdle rate. Chris Hohn is personally one of the wealthiest individual in the UK HF industry with a reported net worth of ~$9B. TCI structure: UK AIFM partnership, Cayman flagship fund, London HQ. The firm is notable for having the highest AUM-to-headcount ratio in London ($55B across ~60 employees = ~$900M/employee), because of the concentration of capital in few positions managed by senior analysts rather than a wide pod structure.

## SECTION III: LANSDOWNE PARTNERS — THE POST-LION ERA
Lansdowne Partners was founded 1998 by Paul Ruddock and Steven Heinz (both ex-Schroders). The firm became the poster child for fundamental L/S equity during the 2000s boom, growing to $22B at peak. Lansdowne's most famous position was the 2007 short of Barclays and other UK banks ahead of the GFC, which delivered 15-20% returns for clients while most peers lost money. The Lansdowne Developed Markets Fund (LDMF) was the flagship. Post-2008, Lansdowne struggled — multiple flat to negative years, partner departures, fund closures. Paul Ruddock retired 2013. In 2020 Lansdowne announced the closure of LDMF after 15 years (AUM had fallen from $8B to $2.5B), returning capital to LPs — a landmark event in the "fundamental L/S is dead" narrative. The firm continues with sector-focused funds: Lansdowne Energy Dynamics Fund (energy and resources, long-biased with tactical shorts, $2-3B), Lansdowne European Special Situations (value-oriented event-driven, $1-2B), Lansdowne Asia (Asia long-biased). Former Lansdowne analysts populate many of the successful follow-on firms: Ross Turner (Pelham), Tim Davis (Maven), Peter Hargreaves' son. The Lansdowne story illustrates the challenge of scaling fundamental L/S: at peak size, the strategy becomes difficult to implement because the best ideas are capacity-constrained and diversification dilutes alpha.

## SECTION IV: MARSHALL WACE — THE L/S PLATFORM HYBRID
Marshall Wace LLP was founded 1997 by Paul Marshall (ex-Mercury Asset Management) and Ian Wace (ex-Deutsche Morgan Grenfell). Headquartered at Sloane Square, London. AUM ~$65B total. Marshall Wace is distinctive because it runs multiple strategies under one roof: (1) TOPS (Trade Optimised Portfolio System) — a systematic-informed fundamental L/S equity strategy using "trade ideas" contributed by an external network of sell-side analysts and hedge fund managers, who are compensated on performance of their ideas ($15B+); (2) Eureka Fund — fundamental discretionary L/S equity ($8B); (3) Systematic Strategies — quant equity L/S ($5B); (4) MW Global Opportunities — multi-strategy ($4B); (5) Marshall Wace Alpha Plus — quant overlay on beta exposure. TOPS is the most innovative contribution: it aggregates research insights from a subscribed network of banks and independent analysts, scoring their historical accuracy and weighting trades accordingly. The approach creates a kind of meta-alpha capture. Paul Marshall (politically prominent — bought GB News, backed Leave in Brexit 2016, various UK political activism) has donated substantially to political causes. In 2015 Marshall Wace sold a 25% stake to KKR for ~$500M valuation, making it one of the first major London HFs to take external private equity investment, setting precedent for institutional GP stakes deals. Marshall Wace employs ~500 globally with ~250 investment professionals in London.

## SECTION V: EGERTON CAPITAL — THE ARMITAGE MACHINE
Egerton Capital was founded 1994 by John Armitage, previously head of European equities at Morgan Grenfell. Headquartered Buckingham Gate London. AUM ~$18B. Armitage is one of the most respected fundamental equity managers in London, known for a disciplined long-biased approach with shorts used primarily as hedges. The Egerton Capital Fund has delivered ~14% net CAGR since inception (2024 nominal), with Sharpe ~1.0, placing it among the best-performing European L/S equity funds. Egerton's process is deep fundamental bottom-up research with 30-50 long positions and 20-40 short positions, moderate gross leverage (250-350%), market-neutral to moderately net-long positioning. Typical holding period 2-4 years for longs, 1-2 years for shorts. Armitage personally controls ~20% of the fund and serves as sole CIO — succession has been a topic among LPs for years. The firm is known for low employee turnover, conservative growth (has not chased AUM), and a clubby Oxford-Cambridge educated culture. Performance has cooled since 2020 (flat to low single digits), reflecting the broader L/S equity struggle.

## SECTION VI: PELHAM CAPITAL — THE LANSDOWNE ALUMNI FIRM
Pelham Capital was founded 2006 by Ross Turner (ex-Lansdowne, also a Balliol College Oxford graduate). Based Knightsbridge, London. AUM ~$3B. Strategy: European fundamental long/short equity, concentrated 20-30 positions, gross 200-300%, modest net exposure. Performance has been strong — ~12% net CAGR since inception, Sharpe ~1.1. Pelham is notable for running smaller AUM than peers, deliberately capping capacity to preserve alpha. Fee structure 1.5/20. Known for intensive sell-side relationships and deep channel research. 2022 and 2023 were challenging years as broader L/S equity struggled but Pelham held up better than peers.

## SECTION VII: SAND GROVE CAPITAL — THE DAVIES ALUMNI FIRM
Sand Grove Capital Management was founded 2014 by Simon Davies, previously co-head of European equities at Lansdowne Partners. AUM ~$3B. Strategy: event-driven and fundamental L/S, with a significant hard-catalyst component (mergers, spinoffs, restructurings). The firm was a successful Lansdowne alumni launch — Davies brought the Lansdowne playbook of rigorous fundamental research combined with event-driven positioning. Sand Grove has delivered solid returns with particular strength in European deal arb and event situations. London office 2 Charles II Street.

## SECTION VIII: THELEME PARTNERS — THE TCI ALUMNI FIRM
Theleme Partners was founded 2012 by Patrick Degorce, TCI's co-founder who left after 9 years. AUM ~$4B. Strategy: long-biased concentrated equity, similar to TCI but without the activist overlay. The Theleme Fund has delivered strong returns, with particular strength in European and US industrial/tech longs. Degorce is personally wealthy from his TCI years and runs Theleme with a small team focused on deep fundamental research. The firm is notable for radical transparency with LPs, publishing quarterly investor letters detailing positions and thesis — rare in the hedge fund world.

## SECTION IX: MAN GLG — THE DISCRETIONARY DIVISION
Man GLG is the discretionary long/short division of Man Group plc. GLG Partners was founded 1995 by Noam Gottesman, Pierre Lagrange, and Jonathan Green (ex-Lehman/Goldman traders). GLG was acquired by Man Group in 2010 for $1.6B. Current Man GLG AUM ~$14B across multiple strategies: Man GLG European Long/Short, Man GLG Japan CoreAlpha, Man GLG High Yield Opportunities, Man GLG Strategic Bond, Man GLG Absolute Value. The integration with Man was turbulent initially with departures of senior PMs (Greg Coffey left 2008, Gottesman and Lagrange left 2019 and 2018 respectively). Current leadership has stabilised the business. The flagship Man GLG European L/S is run by Rory Powe (ex-Invesco Perpetual), delivering mid-single-digit CAGR. Man GLG is Europe's largest active long-short equity boutique by AUM under an institutional parent.

## SECTION X: ODEY ASSET MANAGEMENT — THE CAUTIONARY TALE
Odey Asset Management was founded 1991 by Crispin Odey, previously at Barings Securities. The firm grew to ~$13B AUM at peak, with Odey personally one of the highest-profile hedge fund managers in London, known for aggressive short-biased positions and political conservatism. Flagship: Odey European Inc, which delivered spectacular returns in some years (+53% 2018 from Brexit shorts, +113% 2022 from inflation/rates shorts) and catastrophic losses in others (-50% 2009, -30% 2020). In June 2023, the Financial Times published allegations that Odey had engaged in sexual misconduct against multiple women over years; following the allegations, Crispin Odey was forced out of the firm, prime brokers (Morgan Stanley, Goldman) severed relationships, and assets were frozen/liquidated. The firm effectively wound down by end of 2023, with remaining funds either closed or transferred to other managers (Ruffer, Waverton, Redwheel). The Odey collapse became the case study of operational and conduct risk in hedge funds — a single person's actions can destroy a $13B firm in weeks. The lesson for LPs: key-person risk is not just about investment skill but also conduct and governance.

## SECTION XI: L/S EQUITY STRATEGY MECHANICS
The canonical L/S equity book: gross exposure 200-400% (long 150-250%, short 50-150%), net exposure 30-80% long (classic "1.5 long for every 0.5 short"), beta-adjusted net exposure 20-60%. Position sizing: typical long 2-5% of NAV, short 0.5-2% of NAV (shorts smaller due to unlimited downside and borrow cost). Portfolio concentration: concentrated managers hold 20-40 names; diversified managers 60-150 names; fundamental research produces conviction in a limited number of "high-conviction" ideas plus smaller trading positions. Stock selection process: thesis development (business model, competitive position, growth/margin trajectory, management quality, valuation), primary research (management meetings, industry expert calls, supply chain checks, customer channel checks), financial modeling (3-5 year earnings forecast, DCF or SOP valuation, scenario analysis), catalyst identification (earnings inflection, M&A, spinoff, regulatory change, product cycle), position sizing based on conviction and risk/reward, stop-loss discipline (typically 20% loss from entry triggers review, 30% forces reduction). Holding periods: longs typically 12-36 months, shorts 6-18 months (shorts are unwound sooner because borrow cost is continuous drag).

## SECTION XII: SHORT SELLING — THE HARDEST PART OF L/S EQUITY
Shorting is where fundamental L/S equity managers distinguish themselves (or fail). Long-only active managers can avoid shorts entirely; HFs are defined by their ability to short. The challenges: (1) asymmetric payoff — max gain 100%, max loss unlimited; (2) borrow cost — "general collateral" stocks cost 5-25bps/year, "special" borrow costs 50-2000bps/year, some names are uneconomic to short; (3) recall risk — lender can recall shares with 1-3 days notice, forcing buy-in; (4) short squeeze risk — when short interest is high and the stock rallies, forced covering amplifies the move (see GameStop Jan 2021, Volkswagen October 2008 +400% in 2 days); (5) regulatory risk — regulators periodically ban short selling (2008 US/UK bank shorts, 2020 European short ban on stocks during COVID); (6) tax inefficiency — short positions don't qualify for long-term capital gains; (7) psychological difficulty — being short in a bull market is lonely. Best practices: (i) thorough borrow availability check before sizing, (ii) diversification across 20+ shorts rather than concentrated bets, (iii) hard-catalyst shorts preferred (fraud allegation, earnings miss, refinancing risk) over pure valuation shorts, (iv) options-based bearish expression (long puts, bear put spreads) for high-conviction bets, (v) avoid shorting high short-interest names without a clear catalyst, (vi) careful attention to borrow cost creep in squeeze environments. Famous London shorts: Lansdowne's UK bank shorts 2007-08, TCI's Porsche/VW 2008 call (they were long Porsche via options which got squeezed), Odey's Metro Bank short 2019, Plural Investments short of Diageo 2024 (rumoured), Bridgewater's Tencent short 2022.

## SECTION XIII: SECTOR-SPECIALIST FUNDS IN LONDON
Rather than generalists, many of the most successful London L/S equity funds are sector specialists: (1) TMT — Egerton has heavy TMT exposure; Discovery Capital Management (Rob Citrone, US HQ but London office); Tremblant Capital (NY HQ with London); Whale Rock Capital (London PM for Europe). (2) Healthcare — Polar Capital Healthcare (David Pinniger), Bellevue Asset Management (Swiss with London), Venrock Healthcare Capital Partners (NY with London). (3) Financials — Kirkoswald financials sleeve, Numis (specialist), Capital Research (generalist with heavy financials). (4) Energy — Westbeck Capital (London oil specialist), Andurand Capital (Pierre Andurand oil specialist, $1.5B — more macro than equity), Maven Energy. (5) Consumer — Pelham has significant consumer, Eminence Capital (NY with London). (6) Industrials — Glenview Capital (NY with London). (7) Mining/Metals — Tribeca Investment Partners London, Pala Investments (Swiss with London), Clapham House, Orion Mine Finance (project finance crossing into HF). Sector specialists typically outperform generalists in their niche because the depth of industry expertise creates genuine alpha from channel checks, management relationships, and understanding of industry-specific KPIs. The trade-off is diversification — a pure healthcare fund lives or dies with healthcare sentiment.

## SECTION XIV: SOURCING ALPHA IN L/S EQUITY — THE RESEARCH EDGE
Fundamental L/S equity managers justify their fees on the claim of superior research. The sources of informational edge in 2024-2026: (1) management access — senior analysts at tier-1 funds can get 1-on-1 CFO meetings that mass-market investors cannot; (2) industry expert networks (GLG, Third Bridge, AlphaSights, Guidepoint, Dialectica) providing access to industry practitioners at $1000-3000/hour — used by all fundamental HFs but carefully vetted for insider-trading risk; (3) primary research — channel checks via surveys, mystery shopping, trade show coverage, patent filings, job posting scraping; (4) alternative data — credit card spending (Second Measure, Yodlee), web traffic (SimilarWeb, SEMrush), app usage (Apptopia, App Annie), satellite imagery (for retail footfall, agricultural yields, oil storage, shipping); (5) regulatory filings deep-dive — reading every 10-K footnote, 10-Q reconciliation, proxy filing, insider transaction, 13F; (6) macro-fundamental integration — understanding how rates, FX, commodities affect specific company earnings; (7) network effect — having the same analyst covering the same sector for 10+ years creates relationships and pattern recognition irreplaceable by new entrants. The challenge: every piece of "informational" edge is being commoditised by the combination of (i) SEC/FCA crackdowns on expert network abuse post-SAC, (ii) alternative data becoming available to all funds, (iii) AI/ML making pattern recognition less unique, (iv) sell-side research unbundling under MiFID II creating more standard research distribution.

## SECTION XV: L/S EQUITY PORTFOLIO CONSTRUCTION & RISK
Beta-neutral market-neutral L/S equity construction: target market beta 0 ± 0.1 using beta-adjusted sizing and/or index hedges (S&P 500 futures short, SX5E futures short, FTSE 100 futures short). Factor-neutral construction: additionally neutralise size (SMB), value (HML), momentum (UMD), quality (QMJ) factor exposures using Barra risk model. This reduces "factor beta" P&L contamination so that residual returns are genuine stock selection alpha. Risk management: position stops (auto-reduce at -20% from entry), sector limits (<25% of gross), liquidity limits (all positions exitable in 5 days at <15% ADV), single-name concentration (<5% NAV), borrow concentration limits (no name with <2x daily volume of fund short position). Gross leverage management: de-gross in volatility spikes (VIX > 25 triggers 20% reduction, VIX > 35 triggers 40% reduction). Counterparty exposure limits per prime broker. Stress testing: apply 1987 crash, 2008 GFC, 2015 China devaluation, 2020 COVID crash, 2022 tech crash scenarios weekly.

## SECTION XVI: LONG/SHORT EQUITY FEE PRESSURE & CONSOLIDATION
The fundamental L/S equity space has faced unprecedented fee pressure since 2015. Traditional 2-and-20 is widely negotiated down to: 1.5 and 20, 1 and 20, 1 and 15, or even management-fee-only funds. Performance fees increasingly subject to hurdle rates (MSCI World or S&P 500 minimum benchmark before GP gets performance). Some institutional mandates demand founders' share class pricing at 0.5-0.75% and 10% performance. The underperformance gap has been brutal: HFRI Fund-Weighted Equity Hedge Index delivered ~5.5% net CAGR 2014-2024 vs S&P 500 ~12% CAGR — a 650bps annual gap — leading to persistent outflows. LP psychology has shifted: "why pay 2/20 for underperformance when I can buy SPY for 3bps?" The survivors are funds that can either deliver clear alpha (rare) or offer differentiated exposure (sector specialists, activists, regional specialists). Many mid-tier London L/S equity funds have closed or converted to family offices since 2015: Odey (closed 2023), Moore Capital (returned capital 2019), Lansdowne flagship (closed 2020), Tosca Fund (closed 2019), Centaurus Capital (closed 2018), BlueMountain London (closed 2020), JANA Partners (closed 2019), Viking Global Europe (closed 2018). The consolidation is brutal and ongoing.

## SECTION XVII: INSIDER TRADING & EXPERT NETWORK CAUTION
Post the 2012 Galleon Group (Raj Rajaratnam) and 2013 SAC Capital (Mathew Martoma / Elan Pharmaceuticals) insider trading convictions, the L/S equity industry has been forced into extreme compliance posture on expert networks and primary research. Galleon's Rajaratnam was sentenced to 11 years for trading on tips from corporate insiders including McKinsey partner Anil Kumar and Goldman board member Rajat Gupta — the latter the highest-profile corporate figure ever convicted of insider trading. SAC's Mathew Martoma received 9 years for Elan Pharmaceuticals bapineuzumab trial tips. The industry response: every expert network call now requires pre-call compliance review with named topic and expert background, recorded calls, post-call documentation, prohibition of calls with specific individuals (company employees of covered stocks, recently-departed employees within 6 months), mandatory training for all analysts on MNPI (material non-public information). In the UK/EU: UK Criminal Justice Act 1993 (insider dealing) and MAR (Market Abuse Regulation 2016) create parallel civil and criminal liability. FCA/ESMA enforcement has been less aggressive than SEC but still meaningful (FCA fined GLG's Philippe Jabre £750k in 2006 for insider dealing, one of the largest individual fines in UK HF history). Modern expert network calls are heavily circumscribed: questions about "industry trends" rather than "specific company performance," avoidance of any "uniquely knowledgeable" source on a covered name, bans on "tipping"-adjacent topics.

## SECTION XVIII: TCI'S ACTIVIST INTEGRATION & CONCENTRATED APPROACH
Although activist strategies are covered in detail in Ch31, TCI's integration of fundamental L/S equity with activist engagement is worth special mention. TCI differs from "pure" activists (Elliott, Pershing Square, Starboard) in that activism is the exception rather than the rule — most TCI positions are pure long bets without engagement. When TCI does engage activistically, it tends to be on governance issues (opposing bad M&A, challenging management strategy, nominating directors) rather than aggressive financial engineering (dividend recaps, spinoffs, sale of the company). The most famous TCI engagement was 2005 Deutsche Börse: Hohn opposed its proposed bid for LSE, arguing the combined entity would destroy value; TCI garnered support from other large shareholders and Werner Seifert (CEO) was forced out. In 2007 TCI was instrumental in the ABN AMRO bidding war, pushing management to consider the RBS-led consortium offer over Barclays. More recently TCI has been vocal on ESG and climate topics: in 2020 Hohn launched the "Say on Climate" campaign requiring companies to publish climate transition plans and put them to annual shareholder votes. The TCI model shows that fundamental equity research and activist engagement can coexist when done selectively on high-conviction positions.

## SECTION XIX: THE L/S EQUITY CAREER PATH
The traditional career path: MBA or investment banking (2-3 years) → sell-side equity research (2-4 years) → buy-side analyst at long-only (e.g., Capital Group, Fidelity, Wellington) or hedge fund analyst → senior analyst → junior PM → PM → senior PM. At tier-1 London L/S funds (Lansdowne, Egerton, Marshall Wace, Pelham, Sand Grove, Theleme), analyst pay is £200-500k for juniors, £500k-1.5M for senior analysts, £1-5M+ for junior PMs, £3-15M+ for senior PMs. Analysts typically cover 30-80 stocks in a sector, present investment theses at weekly team meetings, build detailed financial models, maintain management/industry relationships, and graduate to PM status after proving consistent alpha generation over 5-7 years. Post-MBA direct entry to hedge fund buy-side is less common than for sell-side; most analysts join HFs after 3-5 years of research experience. Post-Brexit, Irish and EU27 passports have become more valuable for London HF analysts covering European stocks, because UK-only work permits limit EU company access. London HF analyst demographics: ~85% male, ~60% UK/Irish nationals, ~25% EU, 15% other; heavy Oxbridge/LSE/INSEAD/Harvard/Wharton representation.

## SECTION XX: L/S EQUITY INTERVIEW PREP & PITCH FRAMEWORK
Typical London L/S equity interview process: (1) initial phone screen with HR and a senior analyst (30-45 min); (2) in-person interview with 3-5 analysts and a senior PM covering technical (modeling, accounting, valuation) and behavioural (team fit, motivation, stock pitches); (3) stock pitch round — candidate pitches a long and a short, typically 20 minutes each plus Q&A; (4) modelling case study (90-180 minute take-home or in-office); (5) final interview with CIO/founding partner; (6) offer and reference checks. The stock pitch is the single most important element. Expected structure: (i) thesis in one sentence ("Long X because Y will drive EBITDA upgrade from $Z to $W over 18 months"); (ii) business model overview (2-3 sentences); (iii) competitive position and industry structure; (iv) key financial drivers (revenue growth components, margin drivers, capital intensity); (v) valuation with at least two methodologies (DCF, comps EV/EBITDA or P/E); (vi) catalyst and timeline; (vii) risks and how you'd hedge them; (viii) stop-loss level. Example: "Long Ferrovial at €33 / target €45 / 36% upside. Ferrovial owns 43% of 407 ETR toll road Toronto (most profitable toll road globally, $200M/year cash to Ferrovial), 25% of Heathrow, builds airports and infrastructure. Sum-of-parts: 407 ETR worth €15 per share, Heathrow €6, LATAM airports €5, construction business €4, net cash €3 = €33 base + hidden value of 407 ETR growth capex €12 = €45. Catalyst: 407 ETR tariff increase 2025, Heathrow expansion approval, construction backlog growth. Risk: sterling weakness hurts Heathrow valuation (hedge via GBPUSD short or S&P 500 long). Stop: €28 (-15%)." Key formulas to know: EV = market cap + net debt + minorities - investments; EV/EBITDA multiples 6-12x typical, higher for growth, lower for cyclicals; DCF WACC = equity cost × equity weight + (1-tax) × debt cost × debt weight; Gordon growth terminal value = FCF × (1+g) / (WACC - g); DuPont ROE = margin × asset turnover × leverage; PEG = P/E / earnings growth rate.

---

# CHAPTER 36 — THE CREDIT, DISTRESSED & FIXED INCOME RELATIVE VALUE SPECIAL

## SECTION 0: THE CREDIT HEDGE FUND TAXONOMY
Credit hedge funds are the third-largest category after equity L/S and macro, with ~$850B global AUM. The category splits into five sub-strategies with distinct risk/return profiles: (1) Credit Long/Short — directional investment-grade and high-yield bond/CDS positions, Sharpe 0.5-0.9, holding periods 6-18 months; (2) Distressed Debt — buy bonds and loans of companies in or near bankruptcy at discounts, work through restructuring, target 15-25% IRR with holding periods 1-3 years; (3) Credit Relative Value — capital structure arbitrage, cross-currency basis, CDS-cash basis, tranche trades, Sharpe 1.0-1.8 but vulnerable to liquidity shocks; (4) Structured Credit — CLOs, CMBS, RMBS, ABS, relative value and directional positions; (5) Fixed Income Relative Value — government bond basis trades, swap spreads, curve trades, Sharpe 1.2-2.0 but requires leverage 10-30x to produce meaningful returns and vulnerable to repo/funding squeezes. London is particularly strong in European credit (HY, IG, loans), distressed (post-EU sovereign debt crisis 2010-2015), and FIRV (sovereign bond trading, euro rates basis). This chapter covers all five as London's credit ecosystem is tightly interconnected — the same PMs often rotate across sub-strategies as opportunities shift.

## SECTION I: LONDON CREDIT FUND LANDSCAPE & AUM
Elliott Investment Management (Paul Singer, NY HQ, ~$70B firm AUM, $25-30B of which is distressed/special situations): London office at Aviation House Kingsway, ~80 London investment professionals, covers European sovereign distressed (Argentina 15-year litigation, Puerto Rico), European corporate distressed, activist positions. Silver Point Capital (Greenwich HQ, $25B, founded 2002 by Edward Mulé and Robert O'Shea ex-Goldman Sachs): London office ~30 staff, focused on European leveraged finance, middle-market distressed, structured credit. Anchorage Capital Group (NY HQ, $23B at peak, returned substantial capital 2022-2023 and wound down significantly): London office historically large (50+), now reduced. Davidson Kempner Capital Management (NY HQ, $38B firm AUM): London office ~40 staff, multi-strategy including distressed, event-driven, credit RV. Oaktree Capital Management (LA HQ, $193B firm AUM post-Brookfield acquisition 2019): London office large (~100), covers European distressed, global credit opportunities. CQS (London HQ, founded 2000 by Michael Hintze ex-CSFB, $13B AUM): flagship Directional Opportunities Fund (multi-strat including credit), ABS Fund (structured credit), Convertible and Quantitative Strategies Fund, Diversified Fund. Cheyne Capital Management (London HQ, founded 2000 by Jonathan Lourie and Stuart Fiertz ex-Morgan Stanley, $11B AUM): European credit specialist including impact credit, real estate debt, structured credit. BlueBay Asset Management (London HQ, part of Royal Bank of Canada from 2010, £54B AUM but includes long-only; HF AUM ~$10B): multi-strategy credit with particular strength in EM debt and event-driven corporate. Alcentra (London HQ, acquired by Franklin Templeton 2022, $45B AUM): leveraged loans and private credit. M&G Investments (Prudential owned, £370B but includes huge insurance AUM; HF component ~$5B): UK credit specialist. Apollo Global Management, Ares Management, KKR, TPG all have London credit teams but primarily private credit rather than hedge funds. Capula Investment Management ($30B, London): fixed income relative value specialist (distinct from macro despite overlap). LMR Partners ($8B London): macro-plus-rates-RV. Kriya Capital, Polygon Investment Partners, Trian Partners (London credit arm), Farringdon Capital, GoldenTree Asset Management London, Muzinich & Co, King Street Capital Management London.

## SECTION II: CREDIT LONG/SHORT STRATEGY MECHANICS
Credit long/short exploits mispricings in individual corporate bonds and CDS. The instrument set: (i) investment-grade cash bonds (trading at G-spread or asset swap spread, e.g., JP Morgan 10Y +85bps over Treasury); (ii) high-yield cash bonds (B to BBB- ratings, spread 300-800bps over Treasury typical, wider in stress); (iii) CDS on specific reference entities (standardised contracts on iTraxx CDX indices or single-name); (iv) index CDS (CDX IG, CDX HY, iTraxx Main, iTraxx Crossover) for beta hedging. Trade types: (1) directional long credit (buy undervalued bonds expecting spread tightening); (2) directional short credit (buy CDS expecting spread widening); (3) pair trades (long one name, short another in same industry or curve); (4) capital structure arbitrage (long bonds, short equity or vice versa when the debt/equity valuations imply inconsistent default probabilities). Sizing: typically 3-8% of NAV per position for directional, 1-3% for pair trades. Gross leverage 2-4x. Hedge ratio: beta-weighted exposure to index CDS, e.g., a 5% long BBB bond position gets hedged by ~3% short CDX IG. Holding periods 3-18 months. Credit L/S returns have been compressed post-2009 by central bank QE which suppressed spreads — Sharpe has fallen from 1.0+ in 1990s-2000s to 0.5-0.8 in 2010s-2020s.

## SECTION III: DISTRESSED DEBT — THE LONDON POST-CRISIS GOLD RUSH
Distressed debt is the art of buying bonds and loans of companies in or near bankruptcy at discounts to face, then capturing value through the restructuring process. The mechanics: (1) identify a stressed/distressed credit (defined loosely as anything trading below 70% of par, or with spread >1000bps over Treasury); (2) analyse the capital structure, recovery scenarios, fulcrum security (the tranche where residual equity value sits), and timeline to restructuring; (3) build a position — typically starting in the fulcrum security, layering out through senior secured for protection; (4) engage with other creditors, the company, and legal advisors to drive the restructuring outcome; (5) exit via (a) cash repayment in a solvent restructuring, (b) debt-to-equity conversion in a Chapter 11, (c) sale of the new equity in a post-emergence IPO or private sale. Typical IRR targets 15-25% with holding periods 18-36 months. London distressed has been dominated by European corporate situations post-2010 Eurozone crisis: Abengoa (Spanish renewable energy, 2015-2018 restructuring, €9B debt), Codere (Spanish gaming, multiple restructurings), Vivarte (French retail), Paprec (French waste), Steinhoff (South African retail with European listings, €12B debt, 2017 accounting fraud), Nyrstar (Belgian metals, 2019 restructuring), Europcar (French car rental, 2020 COVID restructuring), Leonardo (Italian defence), Atos (French IT services, 2024 restructuring), Casino (French supermarkets, 2023 restructuring), and numerous UK situations including Tata Steel UK, Carillion (2018 collapse), Debenhams (2020), Homebase, BrightHouse, Flybe. US distressed has been bigger by capital deployed (energy 2015-2016, retail 2017-2020, COVID 2020, regional banks 2023, CMBS 2023-2025) but London houses like Oaktree, Elliott, Davidson Kempner, Silver Point trade both.

## SECTION IV: ELLIOTT MANAGEMENT — THE DISTRESSED KING
Elliott Investment Management (formerly Elliott Associates) was founded 1977 by Paul Singer, originally with $1.3M as a convertible arbitrage fund. Today managing ~$70B with a distressed/special situations focus. Elliott is legendary for its willingness to litigate aggressively and maintain patient capital. Famous positions: (1) Argentina 2001-2016 — Elliott bought Argentine sovereign bonds at 20c on the dollar during the 2001 default, rejected the 2005 restructuring that other creditors accepted, and litigated for 15 years through US courts, ultimately winning full recovery plus interest of ~$2.4B when Argentina settled in 2016. Elliott's tactics included seizing the Argentine navy vessel "ARA Libertad" in Ghana in 2012 (released after 10 weeks of diplomatic wrangling); (2) Peru 1996-2000 — similar distressed sovereign strategy, recovered 4x investment; (3) Republic of Congo 2003-2008 — "vulture fund" label applied pejoratively; (4) US corporate distressed — Samsung Electronics activism, AT&T activism, Twitter activism 2017, Commscope, BMC Software; (5) European activism — Hyundai Group, SoftBank, Bayer, Thyssenkrupp, Telecom Italia, GSK consumer spinoff. Elliott's London office opened 1994 and now houses ~80 investment professionals led by Gordon Singer (Paul's son). The London team covers European sovereign and corporate distressed plus activist campaigns. Elliott is notable for: (i) extreme patience (10-15 year timelines acceptable), (ii) willingness to litigate where peers settle, (iii) large team of in-house lawyers and restructuring specialists, (iv) multiple funds including Elliott International (offshore) and Elliott Associates (US), (v) Paul Singer's personal fortune estimated at $6B+. Performance: ~12% net CAGR since 1977 inception — the longest-running successful HF track record alongside Renaissance.

## SECTION V: OAKTREE CAPITAL — THE HOWARD MARKS SCHOOL
Oaktree Capital Management was founded 1995 by Howard Marks and Bruce Karsh, ex-TCW distressed debt specialists. Headquartered Los Angeles with major London office. AUM ~$193B (after 2019 Brookfield acquisition of 62% stake at $4.7B valuation). Oaktree's distressed focus: the firm runs a series of "Opportunities" funds that pool committed capital to deploy when distressed opportunities appear, similar to PE fund structures. Oaktree XII was $16B, XI was $16B, X was $11B. This allows Oaktree to raise capital in good times and deploy in bad times without LP redemption pressure. Marks is famous for his quarterly "Memos to Clients" which have become widely-read industry commentary — topics include risk, cycles, contrarianism, forecasting. Notable Marks memos: "You Can't Predict. You Can Prepare" (2001), "The Most Important Thing" (2003, book version 2011), "Risk Revisited" (2014), "Sea Change" (2022), "Further Thoughts on Sea Change" (2023). The Oaktree investment philosophy: "second-level thinking" (understanding not just what's cheap but why the market thinks it's cheap and whether that thinking is wrong), risk-first analysis (focus on worst case), contrarian positioning (buy when others are selling, sell when others are buying), patient capital deployment. London office houses ~100 investment professionals covering European distressed, real estate credit, leveraged finance. 2008-2009 was Oaktree's iconic year: deployed $7B of Oaktree Opps VII across 1000+ distressed securities in weeks during the GFC, generating 2.5-3x money for investors.

## SECTION VI: DAVIDSON KEMPNER CAPITAL — THE MULTI-STRATEGY CREDIT
Davidson Kempner Capital Management (DKCM) was founded 1990 by Marvin Davidson (who had founded MH Davidson & Co in 1983) and Thomas Kempner. NY HQ with major London and Hong Kong offices. AUM $38B. Strategy: multi-strategy focused on credit — distressed, event-driven credit, capital structure arbitrage, claims trading, rescue financing, structured credit. DKCM is known for particularly aggressive European distressed positioning — active in Abengoa, Steinhoff, Europcar, Casino, Atos, various Italian/Spanish corporates. London office ~40 staff includes former Lehman, Morgan Stanley, and Blackstone GSO credit specialists. Performance has been steady: ~9-12% net CAGR with moderate volatility, Sharpe ~1.0. DKCM is less public than Elliott or Oaktree (few press appearances, no public memos) which is typical of the "grind-it-out" credit shops that prefer to stay below the radar.

## SECTION VII: SILVER POINT CAPITAL — THE MID-MARKET SPECIALIST
Silver Point Capital was founded 2002 by Edward Mulé and Robert O'Shea, both ex-Goldman Sachs leveraged finance. Greenwich HQ with London office of ~30. AUM $25B. Strategy: middle-market distressed and special situations, structured credit, rescue financing. Silver Point is notable for deep credit analysis, relationship-driven deal origination (many situations come through sponsor and bank relationships rather than auction processes), and willingness to take illiquid positions with 2-5 year holding periods. London office covers European middle-market distressed, particularly UK situations, German Mittelstand restructurings, Nordic opportunities. The firm has flex between hedge fund and private credit structures, and has raised substantial committed-capital vehicles alongside the hedge fund format.

## SECTION VIII: CQS — THE LONDON MULTI-STRATEGY CREDIT
CQS was founded 2000 by Michael Hintze (Australian, ex-Salomon Brothers/CSFB proprietary trading). London HQ Fleet Street. AUM $13B (down from peak $15B in 2015). Strategy: multi-strategy credit including convertible arbitrage (CQS's founding strategy), ABS (asset-backed securities), directional credit, multi-asset, and convertibles. Flagship: Directional Opportunities Fund ($5B). CQS has been through difficult periods — heavy losses in 2008 ABS book, further stress 2015-2016 on directional calls, more losses 2022-2023 on rates trades. Hintze personally is a prominent Conservative Party donor and philanthropist, knighted for services to philanthropy. The firm has transitioned toward lower-volatility strategies in recent years and has cut headcount. London office ~100 staff with ~40 investment professionals.

## SECTION IX: CHEYNE CAPITAL — THE STRUCTURED CREDIT SPECIALIST
Cheyne Capital Management was founded 2000 by Jonathan Lourie and Stuart Fiertz, both ex-Morgan Stanley. London HQ Mayfair. AUM $11B. Strategy: European credit across multiple formats — real estate debt (largest), convertible arbitrage (heritage), structured credit, impact credit. Cheyne is one of the largest European real estate direct lenders (£4-5B deployed in CRE debt), providing loans to UK and European property developers and owners at senior, mezzanine, and junior levels. The firm has expanded into "impact credit" — financing with ESG/sustainability overlays — a growing niche. Convertible arbitrage, Cheyne's founding strategy, has been a more modest contribution in recent years as convertibles supply has shrunk. Performance has been stable mid-single digits. The firm is known for conservative leverage and strong risk management.

## SECTION X: FIXED INCOME RELATIVE VALUE (FIRV) — THE LEVERAGE GAME
Fixed income relative value (FIRV) is the high-octane sub-category of credit/rates trading where funds use extreme leverage (10-30x) to extract small but reliable mispricings between closely-related fixed income instruments. The canonical trades: (1) Swap spread — 10Y USD swap rate vs 10Y UST yield, should be small and stable; when swap spreads widen or narrow beyond historical norms, trade expecting mean reversion. Typical swap spread ~10-30bps; historical range 0-60bps. (2) Basis trades — cash bonds vs futures (CTD — cheapest-to-deliver analysis), CDS-bond basis (CDS spread vs asset swap spread of same bond). (3) Cross-currency basis — EURUSD FX basis swap pricing the premium to convert EUR into USD, driven by supply/demand imbalances in global funding. Post-2008, cross-currency basis has been persistently negative (USD funding scarcity) by 20-100bps. (4) Asset swap (ASW) trades — buy bond, pay fixed/receive float in swap, leaves exposure to credit spread only. (5) Curve trades — 2s10s, 5s30s, butterflies, captures mean reversion in steepness. (6) Repo basis and general collateral financing arbitrage. (7) Off-the-run vs on-the-run Treasury basis (the 2014-2020 iconic trade was long off-the-run, short on-the-run). FIRV funds typically target 8-15% net returns with moderate Sharpe (1.0-1.5) and very high leverage. Risks: funding dislocation (when repo rates spike, FIRV positions can't be financed and must be unwound at bad prices — this is what killed LTCM in 1998).

## SECTION XI: CAPULA INVESTMENT MANAGEMENT — THE LONDON FIRV GIANT
Capula Investment Management was founded 2005 by Yan Huo (Chinese-born, Columbia MBA, ex-JPMorgan fixed income prop trader). London HQ 7 Clifford Street Mayfair. AUM $30B+ making it one of the largest London-based hedge fund managers. Strategy: multi-strategy FIRV including global rates relative value, macro (adjacent to discretionary macro but systematic-informed), volatility arbitrage, and credit. Flagship: Capula Global Relative Value Fund (GRV, ~$15B, the original FIRV strategy). Other funds: Capula Macro (macro-oriented), Capula Volatility Arbitrage, Capula Tail Risk Fund (explicit tail hedge), Capula Enhanced Strategies. Performance has been consistently strong: Capula GRV delivered ~9-12% CAGR with Sharpe >1.5 since inception. The firm is known for extreme discretion (rarely in press), rigorous risk management, and heavy use of leverage with tight controls. Yan Huo is reportedly one of the wealthiest individuals in the UK HF industry. Capula is notable for having avoided the 2015-2018 flat period that hurt many FIRV peers, and delivered strong returns in 2020 COVID and 2022 rates crisis.

## SECTION XII: LTCM (LONG-TERM CAPITAL MANAGEMENT) — THE FIRV CAUTIONARY TALE
LTCM was founded 1994 by John Meriwether (ex-Salomon Brothers legendary fixed income prop trader) with partners including Nobel Prize winners Robert Merton and Myron Scholes (1997, for options pricing), plus ex-Fed vice-chair David Mullins. LTCM pursued FIRV with extreme leverage — at peak 1997, LTCM had $4.8B of equity supporting $129B of balance sheet assets (27x leverage) and $1.25T of derivatives notional. The core strategies were classic FIRV: swap spreads, convergence trades in European bonds (Italy/Spain/France convergence to core as Eurozone formed), on-the-run vs off-the-run Treasury basis, equity pair convergence, volatility trades. The fund delivered exceptional returns 1994-1997: 21% 1994, 43% 1995, 41% 1996, 17% 1997. In August 1998, Russia defaulted on domestic GKO debt, triggering a global flight-to-quality that blew out spreads LTCM was betting would converge. Within weeks LTCM's equity fell from $4.8B to $600M as losses mounted across all strategies simultaneously (they were not as uncorrelated as LTCM's risk models assumed). On 23 September 1998 the Federal Reserve Bank of New York orchestrated a $3.625B bailout consortium of 14 Wall Street banks to prevent a disorderly unwind that could have cascaded through the financial system. Lessons: (1) model risk — Merton and Scholes's Gaussian correlation assumptions failed catastrophically when correlations jumped to 1.0 in crisis; (2) leverage amplifies losses exponentially — 27x leverage means 4% adverse move = 100% loss of equity; (3) liquidity risk — LTCM's positions were supposedly liquid in normal times but were impossible to exit in crisis without moving the market against itself; (4) crowding — many other banks had copied LTCM's trades, so when LTCM needed to unwind, counterparties refused to take the other side; (5) counterparty contagion — 14 Wall Street banks were collectively exposed to LTCM and could not afford a disorderly default. Book recommendation: "When Genius Failed" by Roger Lowenstein is the definitive account. LTCM is required reading for any credit/FIRV interview.

## SECTION XIII: CAPITAL STRUCTURE ARBITRAGE — THE CROSS-INSTRUMENT TRADE
Capital structure arbitrage exploits mispricings between different securities issued by the same company. The classic trade: if a company's bond yield implies higher default probability than its equity volatility implies (via Merton structural model), go long the bond and short the equity. The mathematical framework: Merton model treats equity as a call option on firm assets with strike at debt face value; implied asset volatility should be consistent between bond and equity pricing. When they diverge, there's an arbitrage. Practical implementation: (1) screen for companies with large spread between "bond-implied default probability" (computed from CDS or bond spread) and "equity-implied default probability" (computed from put prices via Merton model); (2) take opposite positions in bond/CDS and equity/options; (3) hedge remaining Greeks (delta, gamma, vega). Challenges: the Merton model has many assumptions that break down in practice (constant volatility, single debt maturity, lognormal asset values). Many cap-structure arb trades lose money when theory and reality diverge. Famous blowup: the GM/Ford cap-structure arb in May 2005 — when GM was downgraded to junk, the bonds fell but the equity rose (short-covering), blowing up long-bond/short-equity trades and causing several credit RV funds to close (Highland Credit Strategies, Ritchie Capital took losses). Iconic London practitioner: Caius Capital, historically CQS convertibles desk, now GSA Capital's credit sleeve.

## SECTION XIV: STRUCTURED CREDIT — CLOs, CMBS, RMBS, ABS
Structured credit covers tranched securitisations of pools of loans. Key categories: (1) CLO (Collateralized Loan Obligations) — pools of 150-300 leveraged loans, tranched into AAA (60-70% of capital stack, paid first), AA, A, BBB, BB, B, equity (residual). CLO arbitrage = difference between weighted average loan spread and weighted average debt cost, ~2-5% annually accruing to equity tranche. London CLO managers: Alcentra, GoldenTree, CVC Credit Partners, Permira Debt Managers, CIFC, Carlyle Credit, Bain Capital Credit, Blackstone Credit (GSO), Onex Credit Partners, ICG, Babson/Barings, Apollo Global Management, Octagon Credit. (2) CMBS (Commercial Mortgage-Backed Securities) — pools of commercial real estate loans on office, retail, hotels, multifamily. 2023-2025 has been the CMBS crisis year with office vacancies in US cities and Europe causing significant losses in BB/B tranches. London distressed CRE funds taking positions: Cheyne, Oaktree, Starwood Capital, KKR. (3) RMBS (Residential Mortgage-Backed Securities) — prime RMBS (GSE-guaranteed, virtually risk-free), subprime (2008 crisis namesake), non-QM RMBS (newer category). (4) ABS (Asset-Backed Securities) — auto loans, credit cards, student loans, equipment. Structured credit strategies: directional long spread, basis (AAA vs same-rated corporates), tranche trades (long BB tranche short BBB tranche), CDS on tranches, CLO warehouse financing. Capacity for hedge funds: $150-200B globally, niche specialists making up most of it.

## SECTION XV: SOVEREIGN DISTRESSED — THE ARGENTINE SAGA AND BEYOND
Sovereign distressed debt is a distinct subcategory. Key features: (1) sovereign immunity limits legal enforcement (though Elliott showed it's not absolute); (2) restructurings are politically driven rather than legally structured (Chapter 11 equivalents don't exist for sovereigns); (3) holders have collective action problems — individual holdouts can be forced into restructurings via collective action clauses (CACs, now standard in post-2003 sovereign bond documentation). The famous sovereign distressed situations: (1) Argentina 2001 default — $95B default, 75% haircut offered to holders 2005, Elliott holdout strategy over 15 years ultimately yielded 4x return; (2) Greece 2012 PSI — €206B restructuring with 53.5% nominal haircut, forced bail-in of private bondholders via retroactive CACs in Greek domestic law, Elliott and Dart won recovery via foreign-law bonds; (3) Ukraine 2015 — $18B restructuring with GDP warrants; (4) Puerto Rico 2016-2022 — $72B debt restructured over 5-year PROMESA process; (5) Venezuela 2017-present — ongoing default; (6) Zambia 2023 — first African sovereign restructuring under G20 Common Framework; (7) Ghana 2022-2023 — $28B restructuring; (8) Sri Lanka 2022 — default and ongoing restructuring. Specialist sovereign distressed funds: Elliott (largest), Gramercy (EM focus), Greylock Capital, VR Capital, Autonomy Capital (founded by Robert Gibbins ex-Lehman London, $10B London-based with strong EM sovereign focus). Sovereign distressed requires extraordinary patience and legal sophistication.

## SECTION XVI: LOAN AND BANK DEBT TRADING
Leveraged loans (also called "bank debt" or "term loans" — typically LBO financing held initially by banks and later sold into CLO market) are a large segment of distressed and credit opportunities. Mechanics: loans trade on LSTA (Loan Syndications and Trading Association US) or LMA (Loan Market Association Europe) documentation standards, settlement is T+20 typical (extremely slow vs bonds), pricing is quote-driven with wide bid-ask. When a loan becomes stressed (trading below 90c), bank desks sell to credit hedge funds; when distressed (<70c), specialist distressed funds accumulate. The 2015-2016 energy loan distress saw $100B+ of E&P loans trade from 95c to 40c and back; the 2020 COVID distress saw similar moves in retail, hospitality, airlines loans; 2023-2024 has seen distressed moves in real estate loans and levered software LBO debt. London loan-trading HFs: Alcentra (acquired by Franklin Templeton 2022), CVC Credit Partners, KKR Credit, Blackstone Credit (GSO), Apollo, ICG, M&G Alternatives, Barings, Permira Credit, Carlyle Credit, Oaktree, Davidson Kempner, Silver Point. Desk structure: senior credit analyst + trader + legal counsel per situation; teams of 5-15 per strategy. Performance: patient distressed funds have delivered 12-18% net IRRs through cycles.

## SECTION XVII: CREDIT RISK MANAGEMENT & VAR
Credit fund risk management: (1) position-level limits by issuer, typically 3-5% of NAV per single name, 10-15% per industry; (2) rating concentration limits (e.g., max 40% below BB-); (3) duration exposure monitored via key rate duration at 1Y, 2Y, 5Y, 10Y, 30Y buckets, total DV01 capped; (4) credit spread DV01 (CR01) — the P&L impact of a 1bps move in spreads; (5) default scenarios — stress test each position under default with assumed recovery values; (6) correlation scenarios — stress test with correlation spikes to 1.0 (the LTCM failure mode); (7) liquidity stress — model forced liquidation at current bid-minus-30% to check NAV under stress; (8) counterparty exposure across prime brokers and ISDA counterparties. Credit VaR methodology: parametric or Monte Carlo using credit spread vol and correlation inputs, typical 99% 10-day VaR 3-6% of NAV. Credit funds distinguish between "mark-to-market" risk (spread widening causing unrealised loss) and "default risk" (actual principal loss). Many credit fund blowups have been MtM driven not default driven — 2008 credit funds faced spread widening from 200bps to 1500bps on IG BBB corps that ultimately repaid at par.

## SECTION XVIII: CREDIT FUND FEE STRUCTURES
Credit fund fees vary by strategy: (1) Credit long/short: traditional 1.5-2 and 15-20, similar to equity L/S; (2) Distressed: typically 1.5 and 20 with 8-10% hurdle rate reflecting the longer holding periods and higher target returns; (3) FIRV: 1-1.5 and 15-20, lower management fee reflecting higher capacity and lower return targets; (4) Structured credit/CLO management: 0.25-0.75% management fee on managed AUM plus 20% performance above hurdle (typically 8%); (5) Committed capital distressed funds (PE-like): 1.5-2% on committed capital during deployment period, then on invested capital, plus 20% carry above 8% hurdle with catch-up (this is Oaktree's structure). Incentive alignment issues: credit fund drawdowns are less common than equity but last longer when they occur (2008 credit funds had 2-3 year drawdowns vs equity funds' 1 year). High-water mark can be a career-ending burden.

## SECTION XIX: CREDIT MARKET PLUMBING — REPO, ISDA, CDS STANDARDISATION
Credit fund trading depends on market infrastructure that can fail catastrophically. Key components: (1) Repo market — short-term secured lending using bonds as collateral, funds leveraged credit positions via repo at SOFR/SONIA + 20-100bps depending on collateral quality. Repo market stresses: 2007-2008 RMBS repo crisis (haircuts jumped from 5% to 50%), 2019 US repo rate spike (SOFR spiked 10%), 2022 UK LDI crisis (gilt repo stressed). (2) ISDA Master Agreement — standardised documentation for OTC derivatives including CDS, swaps, FX forwards. 2002 ISDA Master is standard; 1992 ISDA is legacy. Credit support annex (CSA) governs collateral posting — bilateral daily margin calls at 0 threshold with 0 minimum transfer amount is now standard for HF counterparties. (3) CDS standardisation — ISDA 2009 Big Bang standardised CDS contracts (fixed 100bps or 500bps coupons, upfront payment at trade inception), improving liquidity and clearing. CDS now trade through central counterparties (ICE Clear Credit, LCH CDSClear) for index products; single names mostly bilateral but moving toward clearing. (4) Trade reporting — post-Dodd-Frank and EMIR, all OTC derivatives reported to swap data repositories (DTCC, ICE Trade Vault, LSEG UnaVista). (5) Clearing obligations — CDS indices, IRS in major currencies, FX NDF must be cleared through CCPs. Credit funds need significant back-office infrastructure to handle ISDA agreements, collateral management, repo funding, and CCP margin.

## SECTION XX: CREDIT INTERVIEW PREP & KEY CONCEPTS
Typical credit interview questions: (1) "Explain capital structure" — senior secured (1st lien) → senior unsecured → subordinated → mezzanine → preferred → common equity. Liquidation waterfall in Chapter 7, absolute priority in Chapter 11 with exceptions. (2) "What's the difference between LIBOR and SOFR?" — LIBOR (discontinued 2023) was unsecured interbank offered rate, SOFR is secured overnight financing rate (Treasury repo based), SOFR is generally lower than LIBOR was by ~10-20bps. (3) "What's a CDS and how do you value it?" — Credit Default Swap is insurance contract paying par minus recovery on default. Valued via credit curve (hazard rate model) + recovery assumption (typical 40% for senior unsec, 20% for junior). Mark-to-market = PV(fixed coupon) - PV(expected protection payments). (4) "Calculate the DV01 of a 10Y UST" — 10Y at 4% yield, price ~100, modified duration ~8.2, DV01 ≈ $8.20 per $10,000 face. (5) "Walk me through a distressed trade" — describe identification, cap structure analysis, fulcrum security, position sizing, restructuring process, exit. Have a specific example ready (e.g., "I looked at Atos 2024: €5B senior debt, company needed rescue financing, I believed senior secured would recover 80c+ while subordinated was worth 20c max, so went long senior secured, short subordinated as pair, exit on restructuring announcement"). (6) "Explain LTCM" — history, strategies, Russia default trigger, Fed-orchestrated bailout, lessons. (7) "What's the difference between hard catalyst and soft catalyst credit trading?" — hard catalyst has a specific dated event (refinancing, tender, ratings action), soft catalyst requires broader thesis playing out. Formulas: bond price = Σ(coupon + principal at maturity) / (1+yield)^t; duration = Σ(t × CF) / Σ(CF) discounted; modified duration = Macaulay / (1+y); convexity = Σ(t² + t) × CF / (1+y)^(t+2); CR01 = bond price sensitivity to 1bps spread change; asset swap spread ASW = bond yield - swap rate (hedged to eliminate rate duration); Z-spread = parallel shift to swap curve that equates bond price to PV of cash flows discounted at swap + Z.

---

# CHAPTER 37 — THE EVENT-DRIVEN, MERGER ARB, VOLATILITY & CONVERTIBLE ARB SPECIAL

## SECTION 0: EVENT-DRIVEN CATEGORY DEFINITION
Event-driven hedge funds take positions in securities of companies undergoing corporate events that create predictable(ish) price movements: mergers and acquisitions, spinoffs, divestitures, bankruptcy emergences, rights issues, tender offers, litigation settlements, regulatory decisions, index rebalances. The category has four distinct sub-strategies: (1) Merger Arbitrage ("risk arb") — buy the target, short the acquirer (if stock deal), capture deal spread to closing; (2) Special Situations — broader hard-catalyst trades (spinoffs, restructurings, divestitures, holding company discounts); (3) Activist-adjacent event-driven — take positions in companies where other activists are pushing changes, ride the catalyst (distinct from Ch31 activist which drives the change); (4) Soft-catalyst event — longer-horizon trades where an expected corporate action will drive re-rating without a committed hard date. Event-driven is a London strength because European M&A volumes are substantial (~$1.0-1.5T annually), regional fragmentation creates cross-border deal complexity, and the UK Takeover Code creates well-defined merger arb mechanics. Volatility arbitrage and convertible arbitrage are technically distinct strategies but share the event-driven mindset of exploiting quantitative mispricings around specific corporate events, so they are covered in this chapter together.

## SECTION I: MERGER ARBITRAGE FUNDAMENTALS — THE RISK ARB MATH
Merger arb exploits the "deal spread" — the difference between the target's trading price and the consideration offered by the acquirer. The spread exists because markets discount deals for (i) time value (closing is months away), (ii) completion risk (regulatory, financing, shareholder vote, MAC clauses), (iii) opportunity cost of capital. Typical spreads: friendly announced strategic deal 2-6% annualised; contested deal 5-15%; regulatory-risky deal 10-30%; hostile 15-40%. The trade mechanics: (1) For cash deal at $60 when target trades at $58, buy target at $58, hold to close, capture $2 per share ($2/$58 = 3.4%, annualised over expected 6-month close = 6.9%); (2) For stock deal where acquirer offers 0.5 shares of acquirer (trading $120) = $60 per target share, buy 1 target share at $58 and short 0.5 acquirer shares at $60, capture $58 + $60 - $58 = $60 at close, locking in $2 spread; (3) For mixed cash/stock, hedge the stock component proportionally. Sizing: typically 3-8% of NAV per deal for tight-spread friendly deals, 1-4% for riskier. Position count: 30-80 live positions across a merger arb book. Return targets: 6-12% net annualised in normal markets, 0-4% in tight-spread environments. Sharpe 0.8-1.2 historically but has compressed.

## SECTION II: THE DEAL-BREAK SPREAD DECOMPOSITION
Deal spread mathematics: Expected return = P(completion) × deal spread + P(break) × (break price - current price). If a deal is priced at $58 with $60 offered and market estimates $45 break price at 90% completion probability: Expected return = 0.9 × ($60-$58) + 0.1 × ($45-$58) = $1.8 - $1.3 = $0.5 per share, = 0.9% on $58. This decomposes the headline spread into its components. Merger arb alpha comes from being better at estimating P(completion) than the market average. Sources of edge: (1) legal/regulatory expertise — knowing FTC, DOJ, EC, UK CMA case law better than consensus; (2) industry knowledge — understanding antitrust concentration in specific markets; (3) closing mechanics — shareholder vote whip counts, financing conditions, proxy battle dynamics; (4) channel checks — speaking to competitors, regulators (where permissible), advisors; (5) pattern recognition — the classic merger arb team has seen 500+ deals and can predict outcomes from structural features. Named London merger arb specialists: Sand Grove Capital, Pentwater Capital (large London desk), Davidson Kempner London, Magnetar London, Kite Lake Capital Management ($2B London, founded 2010 by Jason Lewis ex-TCI), Athos Asset Management, PSquared Asset Management, Syquant Capital (Paris with London), Edoma Partners (Pierre-Henri Flamand ex-Goldman, closed 2012 after poor performance). US merger arb players with London desks: Millstreet Capital, Magnetar, D.E. Shaw event-driven sleeve, Paulson & Co event-driven.

## SECTION III: UK TAKEOVER CODE MECHANICS
The UK Takeover Code ("The Blue Book") is the regulatory framework for public M&A in the UK, administered by the Takeover Panel. Key provisions: (1) Rule 2.7 — firm offer announcement, triggers a 60-day offer timetable; (2) Put-Up-or-Shut-Up (PUSU) rule — after a possible offer announcement, target can require bidder to "put up" a firm offer within 28 days or walk away for 6 months; (3) Mandatory offer rule (Rule 9) — acquirer crossing 30% of voting rights must bid for 100% at highest price paid in previous 12 months; (4) Substantial stake building rules — 1% creeper rule, 3% TR-1 disclosure rule; (5) Squeeze-out right — 90% acceptance allows compulsory acquisition of remaining 10%. Offer structures: (i) scheme of arrangement — court-sanctioned, requires 75% shareholder vote, single binary outcome, faster (3-4 months), preferred by friendly deals; (ii) contractual offer — simple tender offer, requires 90% acceptance for squeeze-out, longer timeline (up to 10 months if extensions), used for hostile deals. Financing certainty requirements: acquirer must have cash financing committed before Rule 2.7. Regulatory timeline integration: CMA Phase 1 review 30-40 working days; Phase 2 if referred, additional 24 weeks; EU merger control similar. London merger arb desks maintain deep expertise in UK Takeover Code mechanics because it creates clear timeline predictability that helps pricing.

## SECTION IV: EUROPEAN M&A REGULATORY LANDSCAPE
European M&A faces complex multi-jurisdictional regulatory review: (1) EU merger control — European Commission DG COMP under EUMR (Merger Regulation), required for deals with combined EU turnover >€5B and individual >€250M each. Phase 1 25 working days; Phase 2 90 working days if concerns raised, with potential remedies negotiation. (2) National merger control in each EU member state if EU thresholds not met — separate filings in Germany (BKartA), France (Autorité de la concurrence), Italy (AGCM), Spain (CNMC), Netherlands (ACM), Austria (BWB), etc. (3) UK CMA post-Brexit now reviews independently — Phase 1 40 working days, Phase 2 24 weeks. (4) Foreign Direct Investment (FDI) screening — expanded dramatically 2019-2024, now mandatory in most EU states plus UK's National Security and Investment Act 2021 covering 17 sectors. (5) FCA/PRA approval for regulated financial institutions. (6) Sector-specific approvals (telecoms, energy, pharma, defence). A cross-border European deal can require 15-25 separate regulatory filings with staggered timelines. The 2022-2024 period has seen increased regulatory intervention: EC blocked Illumina-GRAIL ($8B), CMA blocked Microsoft-Activision (initially — later cleared after remedies), DOJ blocked JetBlue-Spirit. Merger arb desks track regulatory "risk barometers" with detailed case-by-case scoring.

## SECTION V: SPECIAL SITUATIONS & SPINOFFS
Special situations beyond pure merger arb include: (1) Spinoffs — parent divests a subsidiary via tax-free distribution. Academic research (Cusatis-Miles-Woolridge 1993) documents that spinoffs outperform the market by 8-12% annually in the 1-3 years after distribution. Causes: neglected small caps, forced selling by index funds, better management incentives. Recent London-relevant spinoffs: GSK spinning Haleon (consumer health, 2022, £10B market cap), Vivendi spinning Universal Music Group (2021, €40B), Daimler spinning Daimler Truck (2021), Hitachi spinning Hitachi Energy. (2) Holding company discounts — holding companies trading below NAV of underlying stakes. Classic London trades: Prosus/Naspers discount to Tencent (traded 30-50% discount 2020-2024), Exor's discount to Ferrari/Stellantis, Investor AB discount to ABB/Atlas Copco stakes, Hal Trust discount. Trades often involve buying hold-co and shorting underlying to capture discount narrowing. (3) Tracking stocks — dual-class representing economic interests in specific business units (Alphabet Class A/B/C, Berkshire Hathaway A/B). (4) Rights issues — heavily discounted share issuances create arb opportunities between rights and underlying. (5) Index rebalances — MSCI, FTSE, S&P, STOXX quarterly rebalances cause forced buying/selling of additions/deletions; arb desks front-run. (6) Share buybacks — especially Dutch auction tender offers create short-term opportunities.

## SECTION VI: SOFT CATALYST & SUM-OF-PARTS TRADES
Soft catalyst event-driven trades target longer-horizon re-rating catalysts without committed dates. Examples: (1) Sum-of-parts discounts — conglomerate trading below value of divisible business units; strategy involves building position, engaging management, advocating for separation. Named London trades: Vivendi breakup (Bolloré family ownership, ultimately separated Universal Music 2021), Vodafone towers monetisation (Vantage Towers spinoff 2021), GSK-Haleon (2022), Bayer Crop Science breakup debate (ongoing 2024), Unilever food business separation debate. (2) Forgotten jewels — subsidiaries buried in larger structures that the market underappreciates. (3) Regulatory-driven value creation — expected court/regulator decisions unlocking value (e.g., court approval of patent validity, regulatory approval of new drug). (4) Strategic review outcomes — companies announcing strategic reviews often lead to M&A or spinoffs within 12-24 months. Soft catalyst trades typically hold 12-36 months with 2-5% position sizes, target 20-40% returns if thesis plays out, accept 0-5% dead money if thesis fails. Higher risk-reward than pure merger arb.

## SECTION VII: VOLATILITY ARBITRAGE FUNDAMENTALS
Volatility arbitrage exploits mispricings between implied volatility (from option prices) and realised volatility (from underlying price path). The canonical trade: when implied vol > expected realised vol, sell options and delta-hedge continuously, capturing the vol spread. When implied < realised, buy options and delta-hedge. Delta-hedging mechanics: short a call option (delta +0.5 exposure to underlying = -0.5 short equivalent), hedge by buying 0.5 shares. As underlying moves, delta changes (gamma), requiring re-hedging. The P&L of delta-hedged short call: (i) collect theta (time decay, profit over time) = ~1/2 × implied² × dt × underlying price; (ii) pay gamma loss when underlying moves = 1/2 × gamma × (price move)². Net P&L = (implied² - realised²) / 2 × position size. This is the "variance swap" payoff. If implied vol = 20% and realised vol = 15%, the trader earns 20%² - 15%² = 0.04 - 0.0225 = 0.0175 = 1.75% vol points per time period.

## SECTION VIII: KEY VOL ARB STRATEGIES
Specific vol arb trades: (1) Short variance / short vol — systematically short ATM straddles with delta-hedging, targeting 2-4% annualised harvest against tail risk. Most common volatility-selling strategy. Risk: catastrophic in vol spikes (Feb 2018 "Volmageddon" when VIX spiked 115% overnight, XIV (inverse VIX ETN) lost 95% and several vol funds blew up). (2) Vol term structure — buy cheap tenor, sell rich tenor on same underlying (typically sell 1M vs buy 3M during stable periods). (3) Skew trading — trade the relative prices of OTM puts vs OTM calls. Equity put skew is persistently steep (crash protection demand); skew arb shorts expensive OTM puts against long ATM. (4) Cross-asset vol relative value — equity vol vs rates vol vs FX vol, when inter-market correlations change. (5) Dispersion trading — long single-stock vol, short index vol, captures the correlation premium embedded in index options. When correlation falls, index vol falls faster than individual stock vol, generating P&L. (6) Gamma scalping — actively re-hedge long gamma positions to capture intraday range-trading. (7) Volatility risk premium (VRP) — systematic harvesting via short vol strategies with tail hedges. Capstone Investment Advisors ($11B, NY with London), Parallax Volatility Advisers, CFM London, LMR Partners vol sleeve, QVR Advisors, 36 South Capital, Universa Investments (Mark Spitznagel) for long-tail; Capula Tail Risk Fund for long-tail London.

## SECTION IX: CONVERTIBLE ARBITRAGE — THE ORIGINAL QUANT STRATEGY
Convertible arbitrage was Ken Griffin's founding strategy at Citadel in 1990 and Edward Thorp's original hedge fund strategy in the 1960s.
