Mechanics: a convertible bond is a bond with an embedded equity call option — holder can convert the bond into shares at a fixed conversion ratio. Theoretical value = straight bond floor + equity call option. The arb: buy convertible, short the underlying stock at an appropriate delta to hedge equity exposure, collect the bond coupon while maintaining delta-neutral exposure. The P&L drivers: (1) bond coupon carry (positive); (2) short rebate on stock (positive, unless hard-to-borrow); (3) theta on the embedded call (negative if short time decay, positive if long time decay — depends on position); (4) gamma from rehedging (positive if vol realises); (5) credit spread movement (negative if spreads widen, positive if tighten); (6) interest rate movement (negative if rates rise, positive if rates fall). The strategy typically runs delta-neutral (net zero directional equity exposure) but long gamma (benefits from volatility) and long credit (hurt by spread widening). Target returns 6-12% with Sharpe 0.8-1.5 historically, though has compressed as conv supply has shrunk (market down from $250B outstanding in 2008 to $100B in 2020 and ~$150B in 2024). London conv arb specialists: CQS (heritage strategy), Advent Capital NYC with London, Polar Capital Technology convertibles, Capula convertibles sleeve, Marshall Wace GaveKal sleeve historically, Millennium convertibles pods. The category has shrunk dramatically — at peak in 2007 there were 100+ dedicated conv arb funds, now 20-30.

## SECTION X: CONVERTIBLE VALUATION FRAMEWORK
Convertible valuation decomposition: Convertible price = max(Bond Floor, Parity) + Option Premium, where Bond Floor = PV of bond cash flows at straight bond yield, Parity = current stock price × conversion ratio, Option Premium = convertible price - bond floor - max(parity - bond floor, 0). Implied credit spread: solve for the credit spread that makes the bond floor + zero option premium = convertible price (this is the upper bound on credit if you assumed the option was worthless). Implied volatility: given credit spread, solve for equity vol that equates model price to market price. A typical convertible has implied vol 0.5-2.0x the underlying realised vol; the difference is the "cheapness" or "richness". Conv arb screens identify converts trading below fair value (cheap) and trades long with equity hedge. Risk factors via "Greeks": Delta (sensitivity to stock), Gamma (delta change), Vega (volatility sensitivity), Rho (rate sensitivity), credit delta (spread sensitivity). Conv arb managers typically run market-neutral on delta, long gamma, long vega, modestly long credit. P&L attribution separates each factor.

## SECTION XI: EVENT-DRIVEN RISK MANAGEMENT
Event-driven risk management specific concerns: (1) Deal break risk — diversification across 30-80 positions, position sizing inversely to break risk, stress testing full book for simultaneous breaks (tail scenario); (2) Correlation risk during market stress — in 2008 and 2020, deal spreads widened simultaneously across most deals as market stress caused even well-priced deals to trade wide; stress test for 3-sigma simultaneous widening; (3) Financing risk on leverage — event-driven books typically run 1.5-2.5x gross, subject to PB financing and margin calls; (4) Regulatory risk concentration — monitor % of book exposed to specific regulator (e.g., EC Phase 2 deals); (5) Activist risk — if an event-driven position depends on activist success, monitor activist funding and credibility; (6) Vol arb specific — short vol positions require tail hedging via OTM protection; (7) Conv arb specific — credit spread stress tests, stock borrow availability monitoring, forced redemption risk from LPs during drawdowns. Position limits: 5-8% NAV per deal in risk arb, 3-5% in soft catalyst, 1-3% in conv arb single name, 10% per issuer across strategies. Liquidity discipline: event-driven positions tend to be illiquid (wide bid-ask, low volume), requiring longer-dated redemption terms (45-90 day notice standard).

## SECTION XII: MERGER ARB LIVE CASE STUDIES
Live case studies from 2023-2024: (1) Microsoft-Activision Blizzard — $69B announced Jan 2022, closed Oct 2023 after 18-month regulatory review. FTC sued to block (dismissed), UK CMA initially blocked (later approved after Microsoft offered cloud gaming remedies), EU approved with conditions. The deal spread blew out to 25%+ at peak regulatory uncertainty; merger arb funds that held through collected the full premium. Pentwater and other London/NY event-driven funds profited heavily. (2) Broadcom-VMware — $61B announced May 2022, closed Nov 2023. Regulatory clearance in EU, UK, China took 18 months. Spread traded 10-20% during regulatory limbo. (3) Amgen-Horizon — $28B announced Dec 2022, closed Oct 2023 after FTC settlement. (4) Adobe-Figma — $20B announced Sept 2022, terminated Dec 2023 after EU/UK regulatory opposition; break risk realized, long positions lost ~30% from deal announcement price. (5) Capri Holdings-Tapestry — $8.5B announced Aug 2023, FTC sued April 2024, judge blocked Oct 2024, terminated. (6) UK-specific: Walgreens-Sainsbury's (not consummated, UK CMA concerns); Morrisons take-private by CD&R 2021 successful; Meggitt-Parker Hannifin 2021-2022 successful with UK national security conditions.

## SECTION XIII: CAPITAL STRUCTURE ARB WITHIN EVENT-DRIVEN
Some event-driven funds extend into capital structure arbitrage around specific events. Example: ahead of a highly-levered LBO announcement, short the acquirer's bonds (expecting spread widening on leverage increase) and long the target's bonds (expecting takeover premium via change-of-control puts). Or ahead of an expected restructuring, long the senior secured / short the unsecured / long the equity (betting on absolute priority violation). These trades require deep legal analysis of indenture language (change-of-control covenants, negative pledges, subsidiary guarantees, equal-and-ratable clauses) and are more niche. Practitioners include Davidson Kempner, Silver Point, King Street Capital, Monarch Alternative Capital. 2022's iconic cap-structure arb around Twitter's LBO by Musk: bonds traded to 65c, equity to 40 (below 54 deal price), sophisticated event funds played various legs of the trade depending on completion conviction.

## SECTION XIV: DISPERSION TRADING DEEP DIVE
Dispersion trading deserves special attention as a signature vol arb strategy. Thesis: index volatility is a function of individual stock vols and pairwise correlations. When correlations are high, index vol is close to weighted stock vols; when correlations fall, index vol falls faster. Implied correlation is backed out from index vol vs weighted stock vols — and is persistently priced ~5-10 points above realised correlation (a premium paid for portfolio-insurance demand). Dispersion trade: long single-stock variance (buy straddles on index constituents), short index variance (sell SPX/SX5E straddle), delta-hedge all legs. The P&L is (weighted single-stock vol - index vol) - (weighted realised individual vol - realised index vol) = correlation change and vol dispersion capture. Sizing: typically 50-80 single names + 1 index position. Position can be structured with options directly or via variance swaps (pure variance exposure without delta). Risks: simultaneous correlation spike in crisis (all stocks move together, index moves as much as stocks, strategy loses). Dispersion did very well 2016-2019, poorly in 2020 COVID, recovered 2021-2022. London dispersion traders: Capstone, Parallax, CFM, LMR, certain pods at Citadel and Millennium.

## SECTION XV: INDEX REBALANCE ARBITRAGE
Index inclusion/exclusion trades exploit forced passive flows. Mechanics: S&P 500 rebalances quarterly; MSCI semi-annual and quarterly; FTSE quarterly; STOXX quarterly; index funds and ETFs must buy additions and sell deletions at the close on effective date. Arb trade: buy additions 1-3 weeks before effective date, sell at close; short deletions, cover at close. Typical premium 2-5% on additions, -2-5% on deletions. Sizing is constrained by passive flows — a $100M ETF rebalancing a new inclusion at 0.1% weight = $100k of flow, but aggregate passive flows across all index funds tracking S&P 500 can be $5-10B per addition. Specialist desks: Bloomberg AIM (passive flows data), D.E. Shaw rebalance desk, Millennium and Citadel systematic pods, Tudor systematic rebalance. Academic documentation: Chen-Noronha-Singal 2004 "The Price Response to S&P 500 Index Additions and Deletions" documented 5-8% addition premium.

## SECTION XVI: CASH MERGER VS STOCK MERGER MECHANICS DEEP DIVE
Cash mergers (bidder pays $X per target share): simplest, no hedging of consideration required, only question is completion probability. Stock mergers (bidder offers Y of its own shares per target share): must short bidder to lock in consideration. Ratio can be fixed (Y always = 0.5 shares) or floating (Y adjusted based on bidder price with collar). Fixed ratio has exchange risk between announcement and close — if bidder falls 20%, consideration falls 20%. Floating ratio protects target shareholders but transfers risk to bidder. Collars limit adjustment range — "walkaway" collar triggers deal termination if bidder drops below threshold. Mixed cash/stock (e.g., $30 cash + 0.2 bidder shares): hedge only the stock component. Cross-border complications: FX risk if consideration is in different currency than target trading (hedge via forwards). Withholding tax risk on stock distributions. Tax-free reorganisation requirements (IRS Section 368, EU Merger Directive) impose structural constraints. Arb desks model every scenario: base case, regulatory intervention, financing failure, material adverse change (MAC) invocation, proxy rejection, superior proposal.

## SECTION XVII: MAC CLAUSES & DEAL DOCUMENTATION
Material Adverse Change (MAC) or Material Adverse Effect (MAE) clauses allow acquirers to walk away from deals if target's business deteriorates materially between signing and closing. Traditional interpretation: MAC invocation has been extremely rare and difficult to enforce. The Delaware courts have historically required "durationally significant" and "materially" adverse effects, with most invocations losing. Famous MAC cases: (1) Tyson Foods v IBP (2001) — Tyson tried to walk, court forced closing; (2) Genesco v Finish Line (2008) — court denied MAC; (3) Hexion v Huntsman (2008) — ruling mixed; (4) Akorn v Fresenius (2018) — first successful MAC invocation in Delaware history, fraud-related; (5) Simon Property Group v Taubman Centers (2020) — COVID MAC, settled at reduced price; (6) LVMH v Tiffany (2020) — COVID MAC threatened, ultimately re-priced down. Post-COVID, deal documentation has tightened MAC carve-outs explicitly excluding pandemic/epidemic, market conditions, regulatory changes — making MAC even harder to invoke. Merger arb desks read every MAC carefully because invocation risk is material.

## SECTION XVIII: CONVERTIBLE ARBITRAGE HISTORICAL BLOWUPS
Conv arb has had several significant drawdowns: (1) 1994 — rising rates caused bond floor deterioration simultaneously with stock selloff, ~-8% across category; (2) 2005 — GM/Ford credit crisis plus stock rally caused simultaneous stock hedge losses and credit spread widening, ~-10%, Highland Credit Strategies and others closed; (3) 2008 — credit spread blowout, stock borrow unavailability, forced deleveraging, -30% to -50% for many funds, led to collapse of several large shops (Highbridge Convertibles, Stark Conv, Deephaven Conv); (4) 2020 March — COVID crash caused simultaneous credit/equity/vol stress, -15% to -25% for conv arb funds that had reduced tail hedges. Survival requires: (i) conservative leverage (typically 2-3x vs 5-8x in better times); (ii) credit concentration limits; (iii) borrow concentration awareness; (iv) liquidity stress testing. Modern conv arb shops have dramatically scaled down leverage post-2008 and run at 1.5-2.5x gross vs 4-6x historically.

## SECTION XIX: VOLATILITY MARKET STRUCTURE & VIX ECOSYSTEM
The VIX Index (Cboe Volatility Index) measures implied volatility of 30-day SPX options. VIX levels: <15 very low vol regime, 15-20 normal, 20-25 elevated, 25-35 high/stress, >35 crisis. VIX futures (started 2004) and VIX options (started 2006) allow direct trading of vol expectations. VIX ETPs (VXX, UVXY, SVXY — after 2018 rebalancing, leveraged VIX products are now capped) create significant flows. The "Volmageddon" of Feb 5, 2018: VIX spiked from 17 to 38 intraday on massive short-vol unwind as VIX futures curve flattened, XIV (Credit Suisse inverse VIX ETN) terminated with 95% loss, SVXY ETF lost 85%, causing ~$3B of P&L across vol funds and structured products. Lessons: (i) short-vol strategies are subject to gap risk from passive/retail flows; (ii) correlation of short-vol positions to broader risk sentiment means diversification fails in crisis; (iii) tail hedges are expensive but necessary. Post-2018, vol arb funds maintain much more conservative positioning. The VIX term structure (1M, 3M, 6M VIX futures) reveals market expectations: upward sloping (contango) in calm markets (creates negative carry for long VIX), downward sloping (backwardation) in crisis (positive carry for long VIX).

## SECTION XX: EVENT-DRIVEN INTERVIEW PREP & FORMULAS
Event-driven interview questions: (1) "Walk me through a merger arb trade." — Pick a recent deal, compute the spread, describe completion probability drivers (regulatory, financing, shareholder), risk/reward, position sizing. Example: "Microsoft-Activision announced Jan 2022 at $95 cash; target traded $85 in Jun 2023 = $10 spread, 12% gross, regulatory risk focused on UK CMA. I estimated 75% completion, 25% break with break price $65. Expected return = 0.75 × 12% + 0.25 × (-24%) = 3%. Positive but thin; I'd size 2% of NAV and wait for CMA clarity before scaling up." (2) "What's the difference between a hard catalyst and a soft catalyst?" — Hard has a specific dated event; soft requires thesis playing out. (3) "Explain the Greeks of an option." — Delta (price sensitivity), Gamma (delta sensitivity), Vega (vol sensitivity), Theta (time decay), Rho (rate sensitivity). (4) "Why do convertible arbs make money?" — Long gamma from delta-hedged position, bond coupon carry, conv cheapness capture vs fair value. (5) "What's your view on current merger arb spreads?" — Have a ready answer with 2-3 live deals, typical spreads, regulatory environment. (6) "Explain volatility term structure." — VIX curve: contango is normal, backwardation indicates stress; carry costs for long-vol strategies; roll yield arithmetic. Key formulas: Merger arb annualized return = (Deal price - Current price) / Current price × (365/Days to close); Expected return = P(completion) × spread - P(break) × downside; Convertible parity = stock price × conversion ratio; Conversion premium = (convertible price - parity) / parity × 100; Implied vol solved from Black-Scholes given option price; Delta-hedge ratio for convertible = N(d1) × conversion ratio where d1 is Black-Scholes d1 formula; Variance swap PV = NB × (K² - σ²_realized) where NB is notional, K is strike variance, σ²_realized is realized variance.

---

# CHAPTER 38 — EMERGING MARKETS & DIGITAL ASSETS SPECIAL

## SECTION I: LONDON AS THE GLOBAL EM HUB
London is the undisputed centre of EM hedge fund activity outside of the onshore markets themselves, historically anchored by the City's dominance in EM debt trading (JPMorgan EMBI benchmark, Citi WGBI, Bloomberg EM local debt indices), FX primacy (London accounts for ~40% of global FX turnover per BIS 2022, with EM FX a disproportionate share), and proximity-time-zone advantage for trading Asia close, CEEMEA full session, and Latin America open. Mayfair EM-focused hedge fund cluster includes Pharo Management ($10-12B, founded 2000 by ex-Deutsche Bank traders Guillaume Fonkenell and Rick Haller), Autonomy Capital ($6B, founded 2003 by Robert Gibbins ex-Lehman), Finisterre Capital ($3B, founded 2002 by ex-Morgan Stanley team, acquired by Principal 2011), Northwest Investment Management (EM equity), Amia Capital, VR Global Partners, GSA Capital (multi-strat with EM exposure). EM specialist platforms also include pods at Brevan Howard, Caxton, Rokos focused on EM macro. Sell-side trading infrastructure: JPM, Citi, BofA, Goldman, Deutsche Bank, Santander, BNP, StanChart, HSBC dominate London EM market-making with full coverage of 25+ currencies and 40+ sovereign credits.

## SECTION II: EM STRATEGY TAXONOMY
EM hedge fund strategies break into: (1) EM Macro — discretionary rates/FX/sovereign credit trading across a universe of 20-30 countries, typically run by ex-sellside EM traders; expressed through FX NDFs, IRS, local-currency bonds, hard-currency sovereigns, CDS; (2) EM Sovereign Credit — fundamental analysis of sovereign creditworthiness, distressed sovereign event trading (Argentina, Venezuela, Lebanon, Sri Lanka, Zambia, Ghana), Paris Club/London Club restructuring dynamics; (3) EM Corporate Credit — corporate bonds from EM issuers (state-owned enterprises, private corporates, quasi-sovereigns), often trading at wider spreads than developed market equivalents; (4) EM Equity Long/Short — fundamental stock picking in emerging markets (Mexico, Brazil, India, Korea, Taiwan, SE Asia, South Africa, CEE), with challenges around liquidity, governance, political risk; (5) EM Frontier — smaller, less liquid markets (Kenya, Nigeria, Pakistan, Bangladesh, Vietnam) with even higher return/risk; (6) EM Quant — systematic factor investing adapted for EM (value, momentum, quality, carry); (7) EM Distressed — sovereigns and corporates in default or near-default, complex restructurings.

## SECTION III: EM FX MECHANICS — NDFs AND DELIVERABLE
EM FX splits into deliverable currencies (MXN, ZAR, PLN, CZK, HUF, TRY, ILS, SGD, HKD — freely convertible, traded as spot/forwards) and non-deliverable currencies (BRL, CNY, CNH offshore vs CNY onshore, INR, KRW, TWD, IDR, PHP, CLP, COP, PEN, RUB pre-2022, EGP, NGN — capital controls prevent physical delivery, traded as NDFs). Non-Deliverable Forward (NDF) mechanics: two parties agree to exchange USD amount at a future date based on the difference between contracted forward rate and fixing rate on settlement date; settlement occurs in USD only, no underlying currency changes hands. Fixings: WM/R London 4pm, EMTA published fixings (varies by currency), central bank fixings. NDF tenors: 1W, 1M, 3M, 6M, 1Y most liquid; out to 5Y for major NDFs (BRL, INR, KRW). NDF points reflect interest rate differentials plus currency-specific risk premium (onshore vs offshore rate divergence). Key liquidity providers: JPM, Citi, Deutsche, StanChart, HSBC in London. NDFs are documented via ISDA with EMTA template supplements.

## SECTION IV: EM LOCAL CURRENCY DEBT
Local currency sovereign bonds denominated in the country's own currency, held primarily by domestic investors plus foreign investors with currency hedging or unhedged EM FX exposure. Key markets: Mexico (MBonos), Brazil (NTN-F fixed-rate, NTN-B inflation-linked, LTN zero-coupons), South Africa (RSA bonds), Indonesia (IDR sovereign), Turkey (TRY sovereign), Poland (POLGBs), Hungary, Czech, Israel, Chile, Colombia, Peru, Malaysia, Thailand, Philippines, India (foreign access restricted), China (CGBs — Bond Connect access). Benchmark index: JPM GBI-EM Global Diversified — 20 countries capped at 10% weight, ~$300B AUM tracking. Typical foreign hedge fund strategy: long carry in high-yielding EM locals (TRY, BRL, ZAR, MXN) hedged selectively with credit protection and FX overlay. Risks: currency depreciation, inflation spikes, central bank policy surprises, capital controls imposition (e.g., Russia 2022, Argentina frequent), withholding taxes (variable by country). The withholding tax landscape is complex — some countries exempt foreign sovereign debt holders via treaty, others charge 15-30%. Tax optimization via Luxembourg SICAVs, Cayman funds with specific jurisdictional structures.

## SECTION V: EM SOVEREIGN HARD CURRENCY DEBT
USD/EUR denominated sovereign bonds issued by emerging market governments under New York law (most common) or English law. Benchmark: JPM EMBI Global Diversified — ~70 countries, market cap weighted with 10% cap per country, ~$400B AUM tracking. Spreads vs US Treasuries (for USD bonds) or German Bunds (for EUR bonds) reflect credit risk, with investment-grade EM trading 100-200bps over and high-yield EM trading 400-1000bps+. Structures: bullet bonds most common; amortizing structures for some frontier; GDP-warrants (Argentina post-2005 restructuring, Greece, Ukraine) linked to GDP growth; collective action clauses (CACs) in NY law bonds since 2014 to facilitate orderly restructuring. Distressed sovereign trading: Argentina (defaulted 2001, 2014, 2020, 2024 again flagged); Venezuela (defaulted 2017, bonds trading 10-20c, blocked from trading by US sanctions); Lebanon (defaulted 2020); Sri Lanka (defaulted 2022, restructured 2023-2024); Ghana (defaulted 2022, restructured 2023); Zambia (defaulted 2020, restructured 2023 under G20 Common Framework). Restructuring takes 2-4 years; holdout strategies (Elliott Argentina 2001-2016, $2.4B recovery on $120M invested) vs participating strategies. London-based EM sovereign specialists include Pharo, Finisterre, Greylock Capital, Vontobel EM.

## SECTION VI: EM CORPORATE CREDIT
EM corporate debt market now exceeds $3 trillion (Bloomberg EM Corporate Aggregate), larger than EM sovereign hard currency debt. Key issuers: Latin American blue chips (Petrobras, Pemex, Vale, América Móvil, Cemex), Asian quasi-sovereigns (China SOEs, Korean chaebols, Indian infra), CEEMEA corporates (Russian pre-2022, Turkish industrials, Gulf issuers — ADNOC, Saudi Aramco, QNB, Emirates NBD), African corporates (MTN, Sasol, Eskom). Credit analysis challenges: disclosure quality varies, accounting differences, related-party transactions, FX mismatch (USD debt with local revenue), political risk, expropriation risk. Spreads typically 50-150bps over comparable sovereign (sovereign ceiling concept — rare for corporate to trade tighter than sovereign, Brazilian exporters a notable exception). Chinese USD corporate market ($500B+) had major dislocation 2021-2023 as property developers (Evergrande, Country Garden, Kaisa, Sunac) defaulted, creating opportunity for distressed specialists. London EM corporate specialists include BlueBay, Ashmore, Pictet Total Return, Amundi EM.

## SECTION VII: EM EQUITY LONG/SHORT
EM equity hedge fund strategies. Universe: MSCI EM Index covers ~1,400 stocks across 24 countries (China 30%, Taiwan 15%, Korea 13%, India 12%, Brazil 5%, Saudi 4%, South Africa 3%, Mexico 2.5%, etc.). Frontier: MSCI Frontier Index covers ~30 smaller markets (Vietnam, Morocco, Kenya, Bangladesh, Nigeria). London-based EM equity specialists: Blackfriars Asset Management (Asia), Steadfast Capital (global EM), Kairos Partners (Russian pre-2022), Sloane Robinson (historical Asian specialist, closed 2019), Trium Capital, Prosperity Capital (Russia/FSU, closed 2022). Strategy nuances: (1) Limited shorting in many markets — India restricted single-stock shorts, China A-shares shorting via SBL is limited; (2) Liquidity concentration — top 10-20 stocks in most EM markets often represent 40-60% of total market cap liquidity; (3) Governance issues — controlling shareholder dominance (Mexican grupos, Korean chaebols, Indian promoters, Russian oligarchs pre-2022); (4) Information asymmetry — local vs foreign investor information gap; (5) Political intervention — tariffs, nationalisation, price controls, FX controls. China specific: ADRs vs H-shares vs A-shares vs Stock Connect access, VIE structures under regulatory scrutiny since 2021.

## SECTION VIII: EM MACRO FAMOUS TRADES
EM macro history is littered with famous trades: (1) 1997 Asian crisis — Soros shorted Thai baht, Hong Kong dollar attack (failed), Korean won, Indonesian rupiah, generating billions; (2) 1998 Russian default — several funds caught long Russian GKOs (ruble T-bills) and sovereign Eurobonds; LTCM's Russian position was a major contributor to collapse; (3) 1999 Brazilian real devaluation — Soros and Druckenmiller short; (4) 2001 Argentine default — Elliott's 15-year holdout trade; (5) 2014-2015 Russian ruble crisis — oil price collapse + sanctions drove ruble from 35 to 80, massive trade for prepared funds; (6) 2015 Swiss franc peg abandonment (technically not EM but shocked FX markets); (7) 2018 Turkish lira crisis — TRY from 3.8 to 7 over 2018, further to 30+ by 2024; (8) 2022 Russian ruble — sanctions froze foreign positions, but onshore ruble rallied from 130 to 55 on capital controls and trade surplus; (9) 2023 Argentina peso collapse under Milei — devaluation from 350 to 1000+; (10) China property crisis 2021-2023 — Evergrande/Country Garden shorts, China equity shorts. Pharo, Autonomy, and similar funds have built careers on EM crisis trading.

## SECTION IX: EM SOVEREIGN RESTRUCTURING PROCESS
Modern EM sovereign restructurings follow a pattern: (1) Default event — missed coupon or principal payment; (2) IMF engagement — Extended Fund Facility or Stand-By Arrangement providing bridge financing conditional on policy reforms; (3) Debt sustainability analysis (DSA) by IMF determining required debt relief; (4) Restructuring negotiation — with bilateral creditors (Paris Club for official creditors, China increasingly outside Paris Club), commercial bondholders (private creditor committees), and multilateral creditors (IMF/World Bank typically senior, not restructured); (5) Exchange offer — new bonds issued in exchange for old at haircut (face value reduction) and/or maturity extension and/or coupon reduction; (6) CAC activation — collective action clauses (75% typical threshold) bind holdout bondholders if supermajority agrees. G20 Common Framework (2020) established coordinated approach for low-income country restructurings, attempting to bring China (largest bilateral lender to low-income countries now) into coordinated process. Zambia 2023 restructuring was first major test case — took 3 years. Ghana 2023, Sri Lanka 2023, Ethiopia ongoing as of 2026.

## SECTION X: DIGITAL ASSET HEDGE FUNDS — THE LONDON LANDSCAPE
Digital asset / crypto hedge funds emerged as a distinct category post-2017 bull run. London digital asset specialists: Nickel Digital Asset Management (founded 2019 by ex-Bankers Trust/Goldman Anatoly Crachilov and Michael Hall, ~$400M AUM), CoinShares (London/Jersey, largest European digital asset manager by AUM ~$4B, listed on Nasdaq Stockholm), Wave Financial (London office), Eldridge Crypto, Brevan Howard Digital (launched 2021, ~$1B, part of Brevan Howard group, co-run by Colleen Sullivan and Peter Johnson). Global leaders visible to London LPs include Galaxy Digital (Mike Novogratz, NY/Jersey), Pantera Capital (Dan Morehead, California), Polychain (Olaf Carlson-Wee, SF), Multicoin Capital (Kyle Samani, Austin), Paradigm (Matt Huang/Fred Ehrsam, SF), Jump Crypto (Chicago), Wintermute (London-based market maker, crypto equivalent of Citadel Securities). Trading volumes: 2024 daily crypto spot ~$50-100B, derivatives ~$150-300B. Crypto custody challenge — few institutions willing to self-custody; reliance on Coinbase Custody, BitGo, Fidelity Digital Assets, Anchorage, Fireblocks for institutional custody.

## SECTION XI: CRYPTO INSTRUMENT SET
Crypto trading instruments: (1) Spot BTC, ETH, and ~20-30 other liquid tokens (SOL, AVAX, LINK, DOT, MATIC, ADA, XRP, DOGE, etc.); (2) Perpetual futures ("perps") — the dominant derivative format, funding rate mechanism keeps price anchored to spot, 24/7 trading, up to 50-100x leverage historically on unregulated venues; (3) Dated futures — CME BTC futures (cash-settled, regulated), CME ETH futures, Deribit monthly/quarterly; (4) Options — Deribit dominant (~90% of crypto options volume), CME, Paradigm block trading; (5) ETFs post-Jan 2024 US spot BTC ETF approval — BlackRock IBIT ($50B+), Fidelity FBTC, Grayscale GBTC (converted from trust); spot ETH ETFs approved July 2024; (6) DeFi protocols — Uniswap, Aave, Compound, Curve, Lido (staking), MakerDAO (DAI stablecoin), yield farming, liquidity provision. Key venues: Binance (largest by volume, regulatory issues in multiple jurisdictions), Coinbase (US-regulated, listed), Kraken, OKX, Bybit, Deribit (options), CME (US regulated institutional).

## SECTION XII: CRYPTO STRATEGIES
Crypto hedge fund strategies: (1) Directional (long-biased) — venture-style token picking, active management vs passive BTC/ETH; (2) Market neutral / cash and carry — exploiting basis between spot and perps funding rate (funding rate can be 10-50%+ annualized during bull markets, collected by longing spot and shorting perp); (3) Statistical arb — pairs trading, cross-exchange arb, momentum; (4) DeFi yield farming — providing liquidity on Uniswap/Curve, lending on Aave/Compound, capturing token emissions; (5) Staking — earning validator rewards on ETH (~3-4%), SOL (~6%), ATOM (~15%), AVAX (~7%); (6) MEV (Maximal Extractable Value) — front-running/back-running/sandwich attacks on DEX transactions, operated by sophisticated searchers; (7) Options/vol — crypto IV is structurally high (BTC 60-80, ETH 70-100 vs SPX 15-20), creating rich short vol premium; (8) Liquid venture — investing in tokens of new protocols via OTC, early exchanges listings; (9) Arbitrage-heavy prop shops like Jump Crypto, Wintermute, GSR.

## SECTION XIII: CRYPTO REGULATION LANDSCAPE
Crypto regulatory landscape by jurisdiction: (1) EU: MiCA (Markets in Crypto-Assets Regulation) took full effect December 2024, providing comprehensive framework for crypto asset issuers, service providers (CASPs), stablecoins (e-money tokens and asset-referenced tokens); (2) UK: FCA regulates crypto for AML purposes (registration required), financial promotions rules since October 2023 (any crypto promotion to UK consumers must be FCA-authorized or from an FCA-registered crypto firm); Financial Services and Markets Act 2023 provides framework for regulating crypto as investment; (3) US: SEC has taken enforcement-heavy approach, classifying many tokens as unregistered securities (XRP partial loss 2023, LBC loss, Ripple/Terraform/Binance/Coinbase/Kraken actions), CFTC asserts jurisdiction over BTC and ETH as commodities; (4) Switzerland (FINMA), Singapore (MAS), Hong Kong (SFC), UAE (VARA Dubai, ADGM) all have specific crypto regimes. Impact on London HFs: must navigate UK FCA + target-market rules; most London crypto HFs are registered as collective investment schemes in offshore domiciles (Cayman, BVI) with FCA-registered managers.

## SECTION XIV: LANDMARK CRYPTO BLOWUPS
Crypto blowups essential for understanding risk: (1) Mt. Gox 2014 — Japanese exchange lost 850,000 BTC to hack, users still being repaid 10 years later; (2) 2017 ICO collapse — thousands of tokens from 2017 ICOs went to zero; (3) 2018 bear market — BTC fell from $20k to $3k, many funds closed; (4) May 2022 Terra/Luna collapse — Do Kwon's UST algorithmic stablecoin de-pegged, LUNA went from $80 to $0 in 48 hours, $60B market cap wiped, triggered cascading failures; (5) June 2022 Three Arrows Capital (3AC) — Kyle Davies/Su Zhu's $10B fund blew up on Terra/Luna and GBTC arb losses, triggered contagion; (6) July 2022 Voyager Digital, Celsius Network — crypto lenders collapsed; (7) November 2022 FTX/Alameda — Sam Bankman-Fried's exchange ($32B valuation) exposed as fraud, $8B customer funds missing, SBF convicted 2023 sentenced 25 years; (8) 2022 BlockFi — bankrupt; (9) March 2023 Silvergate, Silicon Valley Bank, Signature — crypto banking rails collapsed; (10) June 2023 Binance SEC suit (partial settlement), CZ stepped down as CEO November 2023. Post-FTX environment: dramatic tightening of custody requirements, counterparty diligence, operational risk. Crypto HFs that survived 2022 became much more risk-averse on counterparty exposure.

## SECTION XV: STABLECOINS AS HF INFRASTRUCTURE
Stablecoins (USD-pegged crypto tokens) are critical infrastructure for crypto trading. Market cap ~$170B as of 2026. USDT (Tether, ~$110B) — most widely used, some historical transparency concerns, but has operated without de-peg at scale; USDC (Circle, ~$40B) — more regulated, used by institutions, had brief de-peg during SVB collapse March 2023 (Circle had $3.3B in SVB); DAI (MakerDAO, ~$5B) — decentralized, over-collateralized by crypto assets; PYUSD (PayPal USD), FDUSD (Binance-linked), algorithmic experiments (UST deceased). For HFs, stablecoins enable: (1) Exchange settlement without fiat banking; (2) Cross-exchange arbitrage at crypto speed; (3) DeFi protocol interaction; (4) Yield via lending (USDC on Aave earns 3-8%). Risks: de-peg events, issuer insolvency, smart contract risk, regulatory action. MiCA imposes 1:1 reserve backing, daily redemption rights, and limits on non-euro stablecoin use in EU.

## SECTION XVI: ETHEREUM ECOSYSTEM & STAKING YIELD
Ethereum (ETH) is the dominant smart contract platform, hosting most DeFi activity. Key post-2022 developments: (1) The Merge September 2022 — transition from proof-of-work to proof-of-stake, ETH supply became deflationary under high usage; (2) Staking — 32 ETH required to run validator, or pool via Lido (largest liquid staking, ~30% of staked ETH), Rocket Pool, Coinbase, Kraken. Staking yield 3-5% paid in ETH; (3) Shanghai upgrade April 2023 enabled staking withdrawals; (4) Layer 2 scaling — Arbitrum, Optimism, Base (Coinbase), zkSync, Polygon zkEVM, StarkNet offering cheaper transactions. HF strategies: liquid staking yield capture, L2 yield opportunities, MEV, restaking (EigenLayer, ~$15B TVL by 2024). ETH price history: $1,400 March 2020 → $4,800 Nov 2021 → $880 June 2022 → $4,000+ by 2024-2025 cycles.

## SECTION XVII: DeFi YIELD STRATEGIES & RISKS
DeFi (decentralized finance) enables on-chain yield generation. Core protocols: Aave/Compound (lending pools earning interest on deposits), Uniswap v3 (concentrated liquidity provision earning trading fees), Curve (stablecoin swaps, low slippage), Lido/Rocket Pool (liquid staking), MakerDAO (DAI generation), GMX/dYdX (perpetual trading). Yield sources: trading fees, borrowing interest, token emissions (protocol inflation as incentive), points programs (airdrop hunting). Risks unique to DeFi: (1) Smart contract risk — bugs or exploits drain funds (DAO hack 2016 $60M, Poly Network 2021 $600M, Wormhole 2022 $325M, Ronin 2022 $625M, Euler 2023 $200M, Curve 2023 $70M); (2) Oracle manipulation — price feed attacks; (3) Rug pulls — token creators abscond with liquidity; (4) Impermanent loss — LP positions lose vs HODL when prices diverge; (5) Gas fees during congestion; (6) Regulatory risk — treasury OFAC sanctions on Tornado Cash 2022. Institutional DeFi participation requires KYC'd pools (Aave Arc, Maple Finance institutional), segregated wallets, professional custody.

## SECTION XVIII: EM + CRYPTO INTERSECTION
Interesting intersection between EM and crypto: (1) Crypto as EM FX alternative — Argentina, Venezuela, Turkey, Nigeria citizens use stablecoins as dollar proxy given capital controls; (2) Emerging market adoption indices (Chainalysis) show Vietnam, India, Nigeria, Ukraine among highest per-capita crypto adoption; (3) El Salvador made BTC legal tender 2021; (4) Russia 2022 — sanctions drove some trade settlement interest in crypto; (5) Hedge funds positioning crypto as "digital gold" during EM crises. Cross-border crypto flows complicate AML/sanctions enforcement. London-based crypto hedge funds with EM focus: Nickel Digital has significant EM/frontier client base; several family offices from Middle East, Latin America allocate to London crypto funds.

## SECTION XIX: FRONTIER MARKET SPECIFICS
Frontier markets warrant separate attention: smaller, less liquid, higher risk/return. Key markets: Vietnam (VNIndex, offshore via P-Notes and direct foreign ownership limits), Bangladesh, Pakistan, Sri Lanka, Kenya, Nigeria (KYC challenges, FX access issues), Ghana, Egypt (recent currency collapse 2023-2024, 50%+ devaluation), Morocco, Saudi Arabia (promoted from frontier to EM 2019), UAE (promoted 2014), Qatar (promoted 2014), Ukraine (pre-2022). Ashmore Investment Management (London-based, ~$50B AUM, founded 1999, LSE-listed) is the largest dedicated EM/frontier asset manager. Frontier challenges: settlement issues (T+5 in some markets), custody constraints, currency convertibility, political risk, low disclosure, limited hedging tools. Frontier returns can be 20-30%+ in good years, -30-50% in crises.

## SECTION XX: EM / DIGITAL INTERVIEW PREP
Interview questions: (1) "Walk me through the Argentine debt restructuring history." — 2001 default $100B, 2005 and 2010 exchanges (93% participation, 70% haircut), Elliott holdouts 2001-2016, RUFO clause, pari passu NY judgment, 2016 settlement under Macri, 2020 default again under Fernández, 2020 restructuring, Milei election 2023 introduced new dynamics. (2) "Explain the NDF vs deliverable forward distinction." — NDF cash-settles USD P&L against fixing, deliverable exchanges physical currency. (3) "What drives EM FX crises?" — Current account deficit + fiscal deficit (twin deficits), FX reserve inadequacy, inflation, political instability, external shock (oil prices, US rate hikes, sudden stops). (4) "Why is the TRY so volatile?" — Erdoğan's unorthodox monetary policy (rate cuts during high inflation), negative real rates, politicized central bank, dollarisation of savings. (5) "Explain staking yield economics." — ETH staking: inflation + MEV + tip rewards; yield inversely proportional to total staked. (6) "How did Terra/Luna collapse?" — Algorithmic stablecoin relying on Luna mint/burn mechanism; bank run triggered death spiral; no hard collateral backing. (7) "Why is crypto IV so high?" — Underlying volatility, thin market depth, 24/7 trading, concentrated retail flows, regulatory uncertainty. (8) "Explain cash and carry in crypto." — Long spot BTC, short perp to capture funding rate (positive in bull markets, negative in bear). (9) "What is MEV?" — Maximal Extractable Value, profit from reordering/inserting transactions in blocks, captured by searchers via flashloans and priority gas bidding. Key formulas: NDF P&L = Notional × (Contract Rate - Fixing Rate) / Fixing Rate; EM sovereign recovery estimate = Present Value (New Bonds) / Face Value Old Bonds; Crypto basis annualized = (Perp Price - Spot Price) / Spot Price × (365/Days); Staking APR = (Issuance Rate + Tip Rewards + MEV) / % ETH Staked.

---

# CHAPTER 39 — PRIME BROKERAGE, FINANCING & COUNTERPARTY

## SECTION I: PRIME BROKERAGE OVERVIEW
Prime brokerage (PB) is the integrated bundle of services that investment banks provide to hedge fund clients, consisting of: (1) Margin financing — lending cash against securities collateral to enable leverage; (2) Securities lending — borrowing shares to facilitate short selling; (3) Trade clearing and settlement — consolidating trades executed across multiple counterparties; (4) Custody — holding assets on behalf of the fund; (5) Capital introduction (cap intro) — arranging meetings between hedge fund managers and potential LP investors; (6) Risk analytics and reporting — portfolio-level risk systems, stress testing, margin forecasting; (7) Office/technology support — legacy services for startup funds. The PB relationship is foundational to a hedge fund's operation — margin pricing, borrow availability, and counterparty credit terms determine what strategies can be run and at what scale. Global PB market estimated at $30-40B annual revenue for the top 10 banks combined, with Goldman Sachs, Morgan Stanley, JPMorgan, Barclays, BofA, UBS, Citi, BNP Paribas, Deutsche Bank, Credit Suisse (pre-collapse) as historical top 10.

## SECTION II: PB MARKET STRUCTURE POST-CREDIT SUISSE COLLAPSE
The March 2023 Credit Suisse collapse and emergency UBS acquisition was a major PB event. Credit Suisse Prime had been ~10% of global PB revenues and a major London PB, and its absorption by UBS (which shut down most of CS Prime, retaining selected clients) dislocated ~$500-700B of client balances that migrated to other primes (mostly GS, MS, JPM, Barclays). Post-CS environment: PB concentration has increased — GS and MS combined control ~40% of global HF PB balances; margin pricing has tightened at smaller PBs reflecting funding costs; HFs now universally require multi-prime relationships (3-5 primes typical for large funds) to ensure continuity if one prime exits the business. Previous PB market exits: Lehman 2008 (collapse, client assets trapped in UK administration for years — the "Lehman London" rehypothecation issue), Bear Stearns 2008, Credit Suisse 2023. RBS/ABN prime disappeared into Royal Bank of Scotland wind-down. Deutsche Bank sold prime to BNP Paribas 2019 as part of strategic exit.

## SECTION III: MARGIN METHODOLOGIES
PB margin is calculated using various methodologies: (1) Reg T — US regulatory minimum, 50% initial margin on equity long, 150% on short (plus 50% equity in account = 50% short margin); simple and conservative; (2) Portfolio margin (US) — risk-based methodology permitted since 2007 for sophisticated accounts, calculates margin based on theoretical gain/loss of portfolio under various price and vol scenarios (TIMS methodology), can reduce margin 30-50% vs Reg T; (3) Risk-based margin (international) — primes' proprietary methodologies for non-US accounts, typically simulating -15% equity move + 50% vol shock + other scenarios, applied to gross positions with offsets for correlated longs/shorts; (4) VaR-based margin — historical simulation VaR at 99% confidence over 10-day holding period; (5) House margin adjustments — primes apply additional margin requirements above regulatory minimums based on concentration, liquidity, historical drawdown. Portfolio margin vs Reg T example: a $100M long/short book with $50M gross long + $50M gross short + offsetting beta positions might have Reg T margin of $75M but portfolio margin of $20-30M.

## SECTION IV: SECURITIES LENDING MECHANICS
Securities lending enables short selling. Mechanics: (1) HF wishes to short 100k shares of XYZ; (2) HF requests borrow from PB; (3) PB sources shares from internal inventory (other HF longs, prop book, custody clients), external lenders (BlackRock iShares lending pool, State Street, BNY Mellon, Vanguard, pension funds), or via rehypothecation chain from other primes; (4) PB delivers borrowed shares to HF, HF sells short into market; (5) HF posts collateral (cash or securities) against borrow, typically 102-105% of market value; (6) Lender earns rebate on cash collateral + fee spread; (7) Daily mark-to-market of collateral vs borrow value; (8) When HF covers, shares returned to lender, collateral returned to HF. Borrow fees: "general collateral" (GC) borrows trade at 25-50bps (ultra low); special borrows (hard-to-borrow names like heavily shorted or small-cap stocks) can trade at 200-2000bps; "super special" borrows can exceed 10000bps annualized (e.g., GameStop Jan 2021 peaked ~100% annual borrow fee). Recalls: lender can demand shares back with ~3-day notice; forced recalls during market stress can cause short squeezes (classic example: Volkswagen 2008 — Porsche cornering caused 5,000% price spike in 2 days, VIC: Lynne Tilton and many funds lost fortunes).

## SECTION V: SWAP-BASED FINANCING
Instead of margin loans, HFs often use total return swaps (TRS) to gain synthetic exposure. Mechanics: PB buys equity position on balance sheet, swaps economic return (price change + dividends) to HF in exchange for financing cost (typically SOFR + 40-80bps). Benefits to HF: (1) Off-balance-sheet — no securities on HF books, just the swap; (2) Tax efficiency — no withholding tax on non-US holder of US swap; (3) Confidentiality — no 13F disclosure of swap positions (until 2023 rule change for Section 13(d) 5% holder disclosure via swaps); (4) Capital efficiency — swap margin often lower than cash margin. The Archegos 2021 implosion was catastrophic because Bill Hwang used TRS across multiple PBs (Credit Suisse, Morgan Stanley, Nomura, UBS, Goldman, MUFG) to accumulate massive concentrated positions ($50B+ of notional on $10B equity) without any single PB seeing the full picture. When positions fell, margin calls could not be met, PBs were forced to unwind, Credit Suisse lost $5.5B and was fatally wounded, Nomura lost $2.9B. Post-Archegos: primes now require swap position disclosure across other primes via counterparty surveys and concentration monitoring.

## SECTION VI: REPO FINANCING
Repo (repurchase agreement) is the dominant financing mechanism for fixed-income positions. Mechanics: HF sells bond to PB with agreement to repurchase tomorrow (or next week, or next month) at slightly higher price reflecting repo rate. PB holds bond as collateral, HF receives cash to finance position. Haircut: HF receives less than market value (typically 2-5% for sovereign bonds, 5-15% for corporates, 20-30% for high-yield, 30-50% for distressed). Repo rates: GC (general collateral) repo rate for US Treasuries tracks SOFR closely; specific Treasury issues can trade "special" (below GC rate) if demand to borrow the specific issue is high (trading short, basis trades). Repo market structure: tri-party repo (via BNY Mellon or JPM clearing) vs bilateral repo; centrally cleared repo via FICC. Repo can be term (fixed maturity) or open (rollover daily). Funding squeeze risk: September 2019 US repo spike where GC rates hit 10% briefly due to tax payments + settlement pressures, requiring Fed emergency intervention. March 2020 repo pressures during COVID liquidity crisis.

## SECTION VII: COUNTERPARTY CREDIT RISK & COLLATERAL
HFs face counterparty risk in multiple directions: (1) Via prime broker (bank) — if PB fails, HF assets held as "street name" can be frozen, rehypothecated collateral may not be recoverable (Lehman London 2008 is the cautionary tale); mitigation via segregated accounts, selected custody; (2) Via OTC derivative counterparties (ISDA trading relationships for swaps, options, forwards) — managed via ISDA Master Agreements with CSA (Credit Support Annex) requiring variation margin and initial margin under Uncleared Margin Rules (UMR phase-in 2016-2022); (3) Via exchanges and CCPs (central counterparties) — LCH.Clearnet, CME, Eurex, ICE, OCC for listed derivatives. Collateral management has become sophisticated post-2008 — dedicated collateral management teams, triparty agreements, daily mark-to-market, dispute resolution protocols. Initial Margin for uncleared swaps is a major cost — required by UMR for counterparties exceeding threshold, typically 10-25% of notional for long-dated swaps.

## SECTION VIII: ISDA DOCUMENTATION FRAMEWORK
The ISDA (International Swaps and Derivatives Association) Master Agreement is the foundational legal framework for OTC derivatives trading. Components: (1) Master Agreement — standard terms including events of default, termination events, cross-default, close-out netting mechanics; (2) Schedule — bilateral amendments to standard terms; (3) CSA (Credit Support Annex) — collateral posting terms, eligible collateral, haircuts, thresholds, MTA (minimum transfer amount); (4) Confirmations — economic terms of individual trades; (5) Credit Support Deed (under English law) or Security Deed. Close-out netting: in default, all trades are terminated and netted to a single amount owed by one party to the other — critical for counterparty risk management since it reduces gross notional exposure to net exposure. Post-2008 reforms required central clearing of standardized swaps (Dodd-Frank, EMIR), variation margin and initial margin requirements for uncleared swaps, trade reporting to swap data repositories. London market practice: ISDA English law is the global standard; NYC law ISDA common for US trades.

## SECTION IX: REHYPOTHECATION & THE LEHMAN LONDON PROBLEM
Rehypothecation is the practice of a prime broker re-using client collateral (e.g., HF long positions pledged as margin) for the PB's own purposes — to borrow against, to lend to other clients, or to finance inventory. This boosts PB profitability and lowers financing cost for HFs (since PB effectively recycles the collateral). Under UK law (pre-2008), PBs could rehypothecate unlimited amounts of client collateral. Under US Reg T/SEC 15c3-3, rehypothecation is limited to 140% of customer debit balance. When Lehman Brothers collapsed September 2008, Lehman London (Lehman Brothers International Europe — LBIE) had rehypothecated massive quantities of hedge fund client collateral. Clients found their assets trapped in administration for years — full recovery took a decade, with some clients receiving 100% eventually but only after losing access for 5+ years. Post-Lehman, most HF PB agreements now limit rehypothecation (e.g., 100-140% of margin loan) and sophisticated HFs segregate "excess margin" into fully-segregated accounts that cannot be rehypothecated. This was a fundamental lesson in counterparty risk management.

## SECTION X: MULTI-PRIME STRATEGY
Modern HFs universally run multi-prime relationships. Rationale: (1) Counterparty diversification — no single point of failure; (2) Competitive pricing — PBs compete for wallet share via margin, borrow, financing rates; (3) Borrow access — different PBs have different inventory for hard-to-borrow names; (4) Service coverage — different PBs excel in different product areas (GS in US equity, MS in international, JPM in futures, Barclays in European credit); (5) Cap intro diversity — multiple PBs provide multiple channels to LPs. Typical large HF ($5B+) runs 3-6 prime relationships; multi-strategy platforms ($20B+) may have 8-10. Allocation of wallet is a constant negotiation — top PBs track client wallet share and adjust pricing accordingly. PB league tables (published by HFM, EurekaHedge) rank based on HF client balances and influence.

## SECTION XI: FINANCING RATES & COST DECOMPOSITION
HF financing costs are decomposed as: (1) Benchmark rate — SOFR (US), SONIA (UK), ESTR (EU), TONAR (Japan); (2) PB spread — typically 25-100bps depending on client size, product, asset quality, relationship; (3) Borrow fees for short sides — 25bps to several %; (4) Dividend withholding — for long dividends on foreign stocks; (5) Swap financing — typically benchmark + 40-80bps; (6) Repo rate — benchmark plus narrow spread for GC, can be negative (specials) for specific issues. Post-rate-hike environment 2022-2024: HF financing costs quintupled as base rates rose from ~0% to 5%+, creating significant drag on leveraged strategies (net of carry). Current environment 2025-2026: SOFR ~4%, typical HF financing all-in ~5-6%. This rate environment favours strategies with high gross-to-net spreads (e.g., stat arb with Sharpe > 2.5) over low-Sharpe strategies that rely heavily on leverage.

## SECTION XII: CAP INTRO EVOLUTION
Cap intro was historically a major PB service — PBs held events connecting managers with LPs, made bilateral introductions, and effectively functioned as salespeople for HFs (in exchange for trade flow). Post-2008 regulatory changes (MiFID II, AIFMD) and more sophisticated LP-direct channels have diminished cap intro's centrality. Modern cap intro: (1) Annual conferences — Milken Institute, SALT, IPE, HFM events, CAIS Summit; (2) Curated smaller dinners/roundtables; (3) Digital platforms — Preqin, eVestment, iCapital, CAIS; (4) Third-party marketers (3PMs) — independent placement agents licensed under MiFID II. Cap intro effectiveness varies dramatically — launch managers benefit more than established managers with direct LP relationships.

## SECTION XIII: COUNTERPARTY DUE DILIGENCE
HFs conduct ongoing counterparty due diligence on their prime brokers and derivative counterparties: (1) Credit ratings (S&P, Moody's, Fitch), CDS spreads, stock price; (2) Capital ratios (CET1, leverage ratio, LCR, NSFR under Basel III); (3) Stress test results (Fed CCAR, EU SSM stress tests); (4) Regulatory concerns and enforcement actions; (5) Earnings volatility and profitability trends; (6) Senior management stability; (7) Peer comparisons. Post-2023 SVB/Credit Suisse events accelerated this discipline — funds now monitor counterparty equity prices and CDS daily, with trigger thresholds for reducing exposure. Many HFs have formal counterparty committees meeting monthly with defined risk limits and escalation protocols.

## SECTION XIV: CLEARING & CCPS
Central clearing counterparties (CCPs) have expanded dramatically post-2008 reforms requiring clearing of standardized OTC derivatives. Major CCPs: LCH.Clearnet (London — now part of LSE group — dominant in interest rate swaps via SwapClear, ~90% global IRS clearing), Eurex Clearing (Frankfurt — Deutsche Börse — IRS, repo), CME Group (Chicago — futures, options, cleared swaps), ICE Clear (credit default swaps, energy), FICC (fixed-income clearing corp, repo), DTCC. CCP risk management: initial margin (IM) based on VaR, variation margin daily, default fund contributions from members, mutualised loss-sharing. CCP concentration risk is itself a systemic concern — LCH clears $1 quadrillion+ notional annually. Brexit Brussels/Frankfurt vs London clearing debate continues, with temporary equivalence extensions allowing EU firms to continue using UK-cleared swaps.

## SECTION XV: TRADE EXECUTION & ALGOS
HF trade execution has moved beyond voice to electronic: (1) DMA (direct market access) via PBs into exchanges; (2) Algo execution — VWAP, TWAP, POV, implementation shortfall, liquidity-seeking algos — offered by sell-side equity desks (GS SIGMA X, MS Benchmark, JPM Aqua, UBS Athena, Barclays BARX) and third parties (ITG/Virtu, Instinet); (3) DMA futures via FIX protocols; (4) RFQ platforms for fixed income (MarketAxess, Tradeweb) and FX (360T, FXall); (5) Block trading for large equity blocks via capital commitment from dealers; (6) Dark pools — GS SIGMA X, MS MS Pool, UBS ATS, Liquidnet, ITG POSIT offering anonymous block matching. Best execution requirements under MiFID II require documented best execution policies and regular reviews. Transaction cost analysis (TCA) is standard post-trade — comparing execution prices to benchmarks (arrival, VWAP, close).

## SECTION XVI: LIBOR TRANSITION & BENCHMARK REFORM
Libor ceased publication December 2021 for most tenors, June 2023 for USD. Replacement risk-free rates: SOFR (Secured Overnight Financing Rate — US), SONIA (Sterling Overnight Index Average — UK), ESTR (Euro Short-Term Rate — EU), TONAR (Tokyo Overnight Average Rate — Japan), SARON (Swiss). Key differences: RFRs are overnight unsecured/secured rates vs Libor which was forward-looking term rates for multiple tenors (1M, 3M, 6M, 12M). Compounded-in-arrears methodology replaces forward-looking rates for most products. Term SOFR (1M, 3M, 6M) derived from SOFR futures used for some cash products. Transition complexities: legacy contracts, fallback language, basis risk between Libor and SOFR, curve construction for long-dated swaps. HFs benefited/lost from various basis trades during the transition period.

## SECTION XVII: FUND-LEVEL LEVERAGE METRICS
Fund-level leverage is measured multiple ways: (1) Gross Market Value / NAV — total long + total short divided by equity; (2) Net Market Value / NAV — longs minus shorts / equity; (3) Notional leverage — total notional of all positions / NAV (inflated by derivatives); (4) Regulatory leverage (AIFMD) — commitment approach (notional netted) and gross approach (notional un-netted); (5) Economic leverage — position deltas adjusted for beta and correlation. Typical leverage ranges: Long/short equity 1-3x gross; macro 3-10x gross (inflated by FX/rates notionals); credit 2-5x gross; multi-strat 5-10x gross; fixed income RV 15-30x gross (high notional, low Sharpe); LTCM 1998 ran ~30x peak (with off-balance-sheet swaps, effective leverage was far higher). AIFMD requires leverage disclosure and regulatory reporting via Annex IV, with special oversight if commitment approach leverage exceeds 3x.

## SECTION XVIII: PB CONTRACTS — KEY TERMS
Prime brokerage agreements (PBAs) are lengthy legal documents covering: (1) Margin requirements and methodology (house margin discretion); (2) Rehypothecation limits and segregation terms; (3) Close-out and default provisions (events of default, grace periods, liquidation mechanics); (4) Termination rights (either party can terminate with notice, typically 30-90 days); (5) Fee schedule (financing, borrow, commissions, clearance fees); (6) Indemnification and liability caps; (7) Governing law (typically NY or English). Key negotiating points for HFs: (a) Rehypothecation caps, (b) Notice periods for margin changes, (c) Definition of "material adverse change" triggering default, (d) Cross-default provisions with affiliates, (e) Termination triggers (AUM decline thresholds, key person provisions, regulatory actions). Large established funds negotiate heavily customized PBAs; small funds typically accept PB standard forms with minimal negotiation.

## SECTION XIX: ARCHEGOS LESSONS IN DETAIL
The Archegos Capital Management collapse of March 2021 warrants deep study. Bill Hwang (founder, previously of Tiger Asia which pled guilty to insider trading 2012) ran Archegos as a family office with ~$10B equity. Using total return swaps across Credit Suisse, Nomura, Morgan Stanley, Goldman, UBS, MUFG, Wells Fargo, Mizuho, Hwang built $50B+ of notional long exposure in a concentrated basket: ViacomCBS, Discovery, Baidu, Tencent Music, Vipshop, Farfetch, iQIYI, GSX Techedu (now Gaotu), RLX Technology. Peak leverage ~5x on equity, but concentrated and cross-correlated. March 24, 2021: ViacomCBS announced secondary equity offering, stock fell 9%. March 25: Discovery fell similarly. Margin calls hit Archegos from all primes. March 26: Archegos could not meet calls. Morgan Stanley (tipped early) began unloading positions pre-market. Credit Suisse and Nomura, slower to react, were left with remaining positions that collapsed 30-50% on forced selling. Losses: CS $5.5B (+regulatory fines, CEO fired, ultimately contributed to CS collapse March 2023), Nomura $2.9B, Morgan Stanley ~$900M, UBS ~$800M, smaller losses at others. GS reportedly exited with near-zero loss. Hwang convicted July 2024 on fraud/racketeering charges. Post-Archegos reforms: (1) Enhanced concentration reporting across PB, (2) Swap counterparty surveys of cross-PB exposure, (3) Tightened margin on concentrated positions, (4) Senior management accountability under SMCR.

## SECTION XX: PB INTERVIEW PREP & FORMULAS
Interview questions: (1) "Walk through how a hedge fund finances a $1B long/short equity book." — Describe PB margin, allocation of longs (equity financing) and shorts (borrow), net financing cost, all-in financing rate. (2) "What is rehypothecation?" — Re-use of client collateral by PB; explain Lehman London example. (3) "Explain the Archegos blowup." — Walk through swap financing, concentration, margin call cascade, PB losses. (4) "What's the difference between Reg T and portfolio margin?" — Reg T is 50% rule-based; portfolio margin is risk-based and can reduce requirement 30-50%. (5) "How does securities lending work?" — Lender → PB intermediary → short-seller; collateral; rebate/fee mechanics. (6) "What is ISDA close-out netting?" — In default, all trades terminated and netted to single amount. (7) "Why do hedge funds use multiple primes?" — Counterparty diversification, competitive pricing, borrow access. (8) "What happened to Credit Suisse prime?" — March 2023 CS collapse, UBS emergency acquisition, prime shut down, clients migrated. Key formulas: Gross leverage = (Long MV + |Short MV|) / NAV; Net leverage = (Long MV - Short MV) / NAV; All-in financing rate = Base rate + PB spread + Borrow fee (on shorts) - Dividend yield (on longs); Margin requirement under portfolio margin ≈ max loss across scenarios; Borrow fee = Market value × Borrow rate × (Days / 360); Repo interest = Principal × Repo rate × (Days / 360); Haircut = (Market value - Cash lent) / Market value.

---

# CHAPTER 40 — LEGAL STRUCTURES, DOMICILE & FEE ECONOMICS

## SECTION I: THE MASTER-FEEDER ARCHITECTURE
The master-feeder structure is the dominant architecture for global hedge funds serving both US and non-US investors. Mechanics: (1) Master fund is typically a Cayman Islands limited partnership (LP) that holds all portfolio positions and trades; (2) US feeder is a Delaware LP that invests its capital into the master; (3) Offshore feeder is a Cayman Islands exempted company that invests its capital into the master; (4) Both feeders own a pro-rata interest in the master. The master-feeder structure allows unified trading and risk management while providing tax-efficient investment vehicles for different investor types — US taxable investors invest in the US LP (pass-through taxation), US tax-exempt investors (endowments, pensions, foundations) and non-US investors invest in the offshore corporation (blocking ECI — effectively connected income — and UBTI — unrelated business taxable income — for tax-exempts). Advantages: single trading entity simplifies operations, cap allocation is straightforward, single set of investment books and records, single auditor. Alternative structures include standalone LPs, SMAs (separately managed accounts), and managed account platforms.

## SECTION II: CAYMAN ISLANDS AS DOMICILE HUB
The Cayman Islands is the dominant offshore fund domicile — ~10,000+ registered hedge funds with aggregate AUM exceeding $5 trillion. Why Cayman: (1) Zero local taxation on funds and income; (2) Established legal infrastructure with English common law basis and Privy Council as final appellate court; (3) Sophisticated local service providers — administrators (MUFG Fund Services, SS&C, Apex, Maples), lawyers (Maples, Walkers, Ogier, Conyers, Carey Olsen), auditors (PwC, EY, KPMG, Deloitte all have Cayman offices); (4) Regulatory regime — CIMA (Cayman Islands Monetary Authority) regulates funds under the Mutual Funds Act, requiring registration, annual audits, local director requirements; (5) Confidentiality and privacy protections; (6) Flexibility — no minimum capital, light registration (~$3k filing fees), fast setup (2-4 weeks). The Cayman Islands Exempted Company is the most common offshore feeder structure; Cayman Exempted LPs are used for master funds. Cayman Islands LLC (2016+) added as alternative structure.

## SECTION III: ALTERNATIVE DOMICILES
Other offshore fund domiciles: (1) British Virgin Islands (BVI) — Business Companies Act, less common than Cayman but used; (2) Bermuda — more focused on insurance-linked funds and reinsurance; (3) Jersey/Guernsey — UK crown dependencies, heavily used for European LP bases, Expert Funds regime; (4) Luxembourg — EU-regulated domicile, SICAV-SIF and RAIF structures popular for European distribution, UCITS for retail-accessible hedge-style funds; (5) Ireland — QIAIF (Qualified Investor Alternative Investment Fund) regime, ICAV (Irish Collective Asset-management Vehicle), used for EU distribution; (6) Malta — PIF (Professional Investor Fund) regime, smaller scale; (7) Delaware — for US-only funds, Delaware LP or LLC is common; (8) Singapore — VCC (Variable Capital Company) structure since 2020 for Asian funds. Post-Brexit dynamic: EU managers increasingly use Luxembourg/Ireland domiciles for EU passporting; UK managers retain Cayman for non-EU and sophisticated investors.

## SECTION IV: UCITS vs AIFMD FUND UNIVERSE
UCITS (Undertakings for Collective Investment in Transferable Securities) is the EU regulated retail fund framework — hedge fund strategies can be implemented in UCITS structures ("liquid alts") subject to constraints: daily/weekly liquidity, 10% diversification limits, 200% gross leverage cap (commitment approach), no physical shorting (synthetic via swaps OK), eligible asset limits. UCITS hedge funds ("Newcits") enjoy retail distribution across EU. AIFMD (Alternative Investment Fund Managers Directive) regulates all non-UCITS funds marketed in EU. Master hedge fund structures typically fall under AIFMD; the manager must be authorized as an AIFM and provide Annex IV reporting to regulators. AIFMD passporting allows cross-border EU marketing for EU-authorized AIFMs. UK post-Brexit: runs parallel UK-AIFMD regime; EU managers marketing to UK need NPPR (National Private Placement Regime) registration with FCA.

## SECTION V: STANDARD FEE ECONOMICS — 2 AND 20
The traditional hedge fund fee structure is "2 and 20" — 2% management fee on AUM plus 20% performance fee on profits above a high-water mark. Reality is more varied: (1) Management fees have compressed to 1.25-1.75% for larger funds, 1-1.25% for founder shares, 0.75-1% for large early investors; (2) Performance fees typically remain 15-25%, with 20% standard; (3) High-water mark — performance fees only charged on new highs; if fund loses 10% and recovers 10%, no fee is charged on the recovery; HWM is "hard" (permanent) or "soft" (HWM resets after 3 years of underperformance, controversial); (4) Hurdle rates — some funds only charge performance fees above a hurdle (T-bill rate, S&P 500, zero, etc.); (5) Lock-ups — capital committed for 1-3 years before redemption allowed, in exchange for lower fees; (6) Gates — redemption limits per quarter (e.g., 25% of fund per quarter) to prevent runs; (7) Side pockets — illiquid positions segregated with separate liquidity terms.

## SECTION VI: PASS-THROUGH FEE EXPLAINED IN DETAIL
Multi-strategy pod shops (Millennium, Citadel, Balyasny, Point72) have pioneered "pass-through" fee structures that differ fundamentally from 2 and 20. Mechanics: (1) Investors pay all fund expenses — salaries, bonuses, technology, data, rent, legal, audit, administration, risk systems; (2) Plus a performance fee on net profits (typically 20-30% for established shops); (3) No management fee per se. Actual investor cost: Expenses typically 6-10% of NAV annually (Millennium runs ~7-8%, Citadel ~5-7%, Point72 ~6-8%), plus 20-30% performance fee. Why investors accept this: the funds have consistently delivered 10-15% net returns (Citadel Wellington ~15% CAGR since inception, Millennium ~12% CAGR since inception), and the alignment of fee structure with expense passthrough enables massive talent spending (guaranteed contracts, expensive data, top PMs). Pass-through transparency varies: some funds publish detailed fee disclosures, others provide less granularity. Critics argue passthrough creates moral hazard around cost discipline.

## SECTION VII: HIGH-WATER MARK MECHANICS
High-water mark (HWM) is the highest NAV achieved by an investor's share class on which performance fees have been charged. Only profits above HWM are subject to performance fees. Example: investor enters at $100 NAV, fund rises to $110 (HWM becomes $110, performance fee charged on $10 gain), falls to $95 (no fee), rises back to $105 (no fee — still below HWM of $110), rises to $115 (fee charged on $5 above HWM). HWM creates challenging dynamics for underwater funds: (1) Demotivates existing talent/managers since performance won't earn bonuses until HWM recovered; (2) Incentivizes shutdown/relaunch to "reset" HWM (controversial and often criticized — Lansdowne's LDMF closure, Odey's multiple fund closures); (3) Some funds implement "crystallization" resets after 3-5 years of underperformance. Hedge fund managers have been known to launch new funds post-drawdown, effectively resetting HWM for new investors while original investors remain underwater.

## SECTION VIII: SHARE CLASS ENGINEERING
Sophisticated funds offer multiple share classes with different economics: (1) Founder class — lower fees (e.g., 1.5% / 15%) available to early investors; (2) Standard class — 2% / 20%; (3) Liquid class — higher fees but shorter lockup; (4) Locked class — lower fees but longer lockup; (5) Currency classes — USD/EUR/GBP/JPY hedged shares; (6) Seed class — significantly discounted fees for seed investors providing launch capital in exchange for revenue share; (7) Founder's class with revenue share — initial investor receives % of management company revenue. Seed investor economics often include 15-25% of gross revenue of management company for 3-7 years, plus reduced fees, in exchange for $50-200M initial capital. Seed investor examples: Blackstone Strategic Alliance Fund, PAAMCO Prisma, Goldman Sachs Petershill, Dyal Capital (acquired by Blue Owl).

## SECTION IX: INVESTMENT MANAGER EXEMPTION (IME) UK
The UK Investment Manager Exemption (IME) is a critical tax feature that enables London to function as a hedge fund hub. Under UK tax law (ITA 2007 s 1146), a non-UK resident fund (e.g., Cayman LP) is not deemed UK tax resident merely because an investment manager in the UK makes investment decisions — provided certain conditions are met: (1) The manager is customary, on normal commercial terms, dealing at arm's length; (2) Manager receives customary remuneration; (3) Manager does not hold equity in the fund exceeding 20%; (4) Manager operates across multiple independent clients. Without IME, London-managed offshore funds would risk UK tax residency (triggering 25% UK corporation tax on all gains), which would destroy the Cayman domicile advantage. IME is the legal foundation enabling London managers to operate offshore funds. Parallel regimes exist in other hubs: US has similar safe harbors for foreign investor activity via "10 commandments" under Section 864(b)(2); Singapore has 13R/13X exemptions; Hong Kong has Unified Funds Exemption.

## SECTION X: CARRIED INTEREST TAX TREATMENT
Carried interest (the 20% performance allocation) has historically enjoyed favorable capital gains tax treatment in the US (where it remains taxed at long-term capital gains rates ~20% vs ordinary income 37%) and the UK (treated as capital gains at 28% pre-2024 vs income tax 45% + NI), creating significant tax arbitrage. UK carried interest rules (Finance Act 2015 onwards): carried interest taxed at 28% CGT rate if held for >40 months (full capital treatment); shorter holding periods trigger income tax treatment via "Income-Based Carried Interest" rules. Labour government post-2024 election signaled review of carried interest tax treatment. US Section 1061 (TCJA 2017) extended holding period from 1 year to 3 years to qualify for LTCG — affects private equity more than hedge funds (which typically don't hold long enough for either treatment).

## SECTION XI: PERFORMANCE FEE CRYSTALLIZATION
Performance fees are "crystallized" (converted from accrual to cash) at defined intervals — typically annually on December 31. Some funds crystallize quarterly or semi-annually; pass-through platforms may crystallize monthly or quarterly at PM level. Pre-crystallization, performance fees are accrued as a liability on the fund NAV (reducing reported NAV to investors). The crystallization date matters: investors redeeming mid-year may or may not pay performance fees on their pro-rata profits depending on fund terms. Some funds have "equalization" mechanics to ensure fair treatment across investors entering/exiting at different times (series accounting, equalization accounting). The gap between performance accrual and crystallization is where some famous historical frauds hid losses (Madoff ran a Ponzi, but traditional fund fraud often involved mismarking positions to inflate accrued fees before crystallization).

## SECTION XII: SIDE LETTERS
Side letters are bilateral agreements between the fund and specific LPs providing custom terms: (1) Most Favored Nation (MFN) — investor receives best terms offered to any LP (common protection); (2) Fee discounts — reduced management/performance fees for large commitments; (3) Liquidity — shorter lock-up or notice for strategic investors; (4) Information rights — enhanced reporting, position-level transparency, LP consultation rights; (5) Capacity — right to additional allocation when fund is closed; (6) Co-invest — rights to participate in specific deal opportunities; (7) Key person — redemption rights triggered if specific PMs depart. Side letters create administrative complexity and potential conflicts (since different LPs have different terms). SEC has focused on side letter disclosure under the 2023 Private Fund Adviser rules (later partially vacated by 5th Circuit 2024).

## SECTION XIII: LOCK-UPS, GATES, AND LIQUIDITY TERMS
Liquidity terms define investor redemption rights: (1) Hard lock-up — capital cannot be redeemed for X years; (2) Soft lock-up — capital can be redeemed but with early redemption fee (typically 2-3%) during lock-up period; (3) Redemption frequency — monthly, quarterly, annually, bi-annually; (4) Notice period — 30, 60, 90, 180 days; (5) Investor-level gates — individual investor's redemption limited to 25% per period; (6) Fund-level gates — aggregate redemptions limited to 25-33% of NAV per period; (7) Side pockets — illiquid positions segregated, no redemption until monetized; (8) Suspension — manager can suspend redemptions in extraordinary circumstances. 2008 crisis triggered widespread gating and suspensions; the reputation damage from gating led many funds to remove gates post-2010 in favor of longer notice periods and lock-ups. Liquidity mismatches (daily/weekly LP redemptions against illiquid positions) are a major source of HF blowups (LTCM, Amaranth, Long-Term Credit Strategies, Peloton, Basis Capital all failed partly due to liquidity mismatch).

## SECTION XIV: GP/LP ALIGNMENT
Manager (GP) alignment with investors (LPs) is critical. Alignment mechanisms: (1) GP capital commitment — manager invests significant personal capital into the fund (typically 5-50% of NAV for founders); (2) Deferred compensation — performance fees paid into the fund (not cash) for some period; (3) Clawback provisions — performance fees returned if subsequent losses occur; (4) Expense discipline — management fees benchmarked to actual operating costs, excess returned to investors; (5) Transparency — position-level reporting, counterparty exposure, risk limits. Misalignment: (a) Asymmetric fee structure (GP upside on performance, limited downside on losses) encourages risk-taking; (b) Pass-through expense structures remove GP cost discipline; (c) Bonus schemes based on short-term performance vs long-term value creation. LPs increasingly demand alignment as a condition of allocation.

## SECTION XV: TAX EFFICIENT TRADING STRUCTURES
Hedge funds employ various tax-efficient trading structures: (1) Short-term vs long-term capital gains management in US (1-year holding for LTCG); (2) Dividend avoidance via total return swaps to escape withholding tax; (3) Treaty shopping — using Dutch or Luxembourg holding companies to reduce withholding; (4) ISDA "892" exemption for foreign sovereign investors; (5) Section 871(m) US dividend equivalent rules limiting swap-based dividend avoidance (tightened 2017); (6) Tax-loss harvesting — deliberately realizing losses to offset gains; (7) Constructive sale rules (IRC 1259) limiting some hedging strategies; (8) Wash sale rules (IRC 1091) preventing immediate re-entry after losses. UK hedge funds benefit from IME for fund-level tax efficiency. Investment manager remuneration in UK traditionally used offshore structures before deemed residency rules (2008+) and carried interest reforms.

## SECTION XVI: FATCA AND CRS
FATCA (Foreign Account Tax Compliance Act, 2010) requires non-US financial institutions to identify US account holders and report to IRS via IGAs (intergovernmental agreements) or risk 30% withholding on US-source income. Cayman Islands signed Model 1 IGA 2013. CRS (Common Reporting Standard, OECD 2014) is the global equivalent — ~100+ countries automatically exchange financial account information. Impact on HFs: significant compliance infrastructure (FATCA/CRS officer, identification procedures, reporting), GIIN registration for fund entities, client onboarding identification. Compliance costs ~$200-500k per fund annually for administration.

## SECTION XVII: AML/KYC FRAMEWORKS
Anti-money laundering (AML) and Know Your Customer (KYC) frameworks govern HF onboarding: (1) Customer due diligence (CDD) — identify beneficial owners, verify documentation; (2) Enhanced due diligence (EDD) — for high-risk customers (PEPs — politically exposed persons, high-risk jurisdictions); (3) Ongoing monitoring — transaction monitoring, periodic reviews; (4) Suspicious activity reporting (SAR) — report suspicious transactions to FIU; (5) Sanctions screening — OFAC, EU, UK sanctions lists. Cayman Islands AML regime (Anti-Money Laundering Regulations 2020) requires appointed AML Compliance Officer (AMLCO), AML Reporting Officer (AMLRO), deputy; MLR and MLRO typically outsourced to fund administrator. UK AML regime (Money Laundering Regulations 2017 as amended): fund manager is subject to UK AML rules, onboarding of LPs requires KYC/EDD.

## SECTION XVIII: FUND ADMINISTRATION & INFRASTRUCTURE
Third-party fund administrators provide: (1) NAV calculation — daily/monthly NAV striking, fair value pricing, equalization; (2) Investor services — subscription/redemption processing, investor reporting, AML/KYC support; (3) Regulatory reporting — Form PF, Annex IV, CIMA returns, FATCA/CRS; (4) Accounting — GAAP/IFRS financial statements, partnership allocations; (5) Performance reporting — returns calculation, benchmark comparison; (6) Tax reporting — K-1 generation for US LPs, PFIC statements. Top administrators: SS&C (largest, acquired Advent/Geneva, DST Systems, Intralinks), Citco (largest hedge fund admin by AUA), Northern Trust, BNY Mellon, State Street, JPM, MUFG Investor Services, Apex, Maples Fund Services, IQ-EQ, Alter Domus. Fees typically 2-10 bps of NAV, minimum $150-300k per fund annually.

## SECTION XIX: AUDITOR RELATIONSHIPS
Hedge funds require annual audit by a qualified audit firm. Big 4 (PwC, EY, KPMG, Deloitte) dominate hedge fund audit — audit quality, brand recognition with LPs, cross-border coverage. BDO, Grant Thornton, RSM also active in HF audit. Audit scope: financial statements (income statement, balance sheet, cash flow), partners' capital rollforward, portfolio valuation, existence confirmations with counterparties, compliance with fund offering documents. Fair value measurement (ASC 820 / IFRS 13) is central — Level 1 (active market prices), Level 2 (observable inputs), Level 3 (unobservable inputs, requiring model-based valuation and extensive disclosure). Level 3 assets receive significant audit scrutiny. Audit firm rotation is not required for private funds (unlike public companies); long-standing relationships common (EY with Citadel, PwC with Millennium, historically). Madoff's 17-year fraud was not detected because his "auditor" was a 3-person firm in a strip mall — post-Madoff, LPs universally require Big 4 or major second-tier audit firms.

## SECTION XX: LEGAL/STRUCTURAL INTERVIEW PREP
Interview questions: (1) "Explain the master-feeder structure." — Describe master fund (Cayman LP) + US feeder (Delaware LP) + offshore feeder (Cayman corp), purpose of structure (tax efficiency for US taxable, tax-exempt, and non-US investors). (2) "Why Cayman?" — Zero tax, legal infrastructure, regulatory flexibility, service provider ecosystem. (3) "What is the IME and why does it matter for London?" — Investment Manager Exemption; allows London managers to run offshore funds without triggering UK tax residency; foundational for London as HF hub. (4) "Explain high-water mark." — NAV peak on which performance fees calculated; only new highs trigger fees.
