(5) "What's the difference between 2 and 20 and pass-through fees?" — 2 and 20 is flat rate; pass-through bills actual expenses; pass-through common at multi-strat platforms. (6) "What are side letters and why are they contentious?" — Bilateral agreements with specific LPs providing custom terms; create disclosure and conflict issues. (7) "Walk through a carried interest tax analysis." — UK CGT rate (28% if held 40+ months) vs income (45% + NI); US LTCG vs ordinary; policy debate. (8) "Explain the Lehman London rehypothecation problem." — PB reuse of client collateral; Lehman LBIE trapped client assets for years post-2008. (9) "What is AIFMD and how does it affect London HFs?" — EU directive regulating alternative fund managers; UK has parallel AIFMD regime post-Brexit; NPPR for EU marketing. (10) "Explain fund gates and suspensions." — Redemption limits to protect liquidity; 2008 gating crisis damaged HF reputation.

---

# CHAPTER 41 — LONDON REGULATION: FCA, AIFMD, MiFID II, SMCR, SFDR

## SECTION I: THE FCA AS HF REGULATOR
The Financial Conduct Authority (FCA) is the UK regulator for hedge fund managers (formally "AIFMs" — Alternative Investment Fund Managers). Headquartered at 12 Endeavour Square, Stratford, the FCA replaced the FSA (Financial Services Authority) in April 2013 as part of post-financial-crisis restructuring. HFs and HF managers deal with the FCA through: (1) Authorization — manager must be FCA-authorized as an investment firm; (2) Supervision — ongoing supervision via relationship manager + periodic reviews; (3) Enforcement — investigations, fines, bans, criminal referrals. Hedge fund managers typically registered as: (a) MIFIDPRU investment firm (post-Jan 2022 UK investment firm prudential regime, replacing IFPRU/BIPRU); (b) Small AIFM (sub-threshold, lighter requirements) for funds under €100M leveraged or €500M unleveraged; (c) Full-scope UK AIFM for larger funds. FCA Handbook provides detailed rules: COBS (Conduct of Business Sourcebook), SYSC (Senior Management Arrangements, Systems and Controls), MAR (Market Abuse Rules), SUP (Supervision), FINMA (Financial Crime), MIFIDPRU, FUND (Investment Funds), DISP (Complaints). FCA fines HFs for breaches — examples: Odey AM £1.8M 2023 (pre-collapse), BlueCrest £40M US SEC 2020 (parallel UK concerns), numerous smaller market abuse cases.

## SECTION II: FCA AUTHORIZATION PROCESS
New HF managers go through FCA authorization — a 6-12 month process requiring: (1) Detailed business plan — strategies, target AUM, target investors, team bios; (2) Senior management personal questionnaires and fit-and-proper assessments; (3) Capital adequacy demonstration (minimum €125k for MiFIDPRU, more under ICARA assessment); (4) Compliance manual, systems documentation, risk management framework; (5) Conflicts of interest policy; (6) Financial crime controls; (7) Business continuity planning; (8) Outsourcing arrangements documentation; (9) Operational resilience plans (post-2022 requirements). Experienced managers (ex-Goldman, ex-Millennium, etc.) often receive relatively smooth authorization; first-time managers face more scrutiny. The FCA's Approach to Authorization document explains the process. Typical timeline: pre-application meetings → application submission → clarification requests → authorization letter. Authorization costs: legal fees £150-500k, FCA fees £5-25k depending on category.

## SECTION III: AIFMD IN DETAIL
AIFMD (Alternative Investment Fund Managers Directive, 2011/61/EU) applies to EU-authorized AIFMs managing AIFs (alternative investment funds, i.e., anything not UCITS — hedge funds, private equity, real estate, infrastructure). Key requirements: (1) Authorization by member state regulator; (2) Capital requirements — initial €125k + 0.02% of AUM above €250M, capped at €10M; (3) Risk management — independent risk function, risk limits, stress testing; (4) Liquidity management — alignment of fund liquidity terms with portfolio liquidity, stress testing; (5) Valuation — independent valuation, at least annually; (6) Depositary — each AIF must appoint a depositary to safeguard assets and oversee cash flows (major change from pre-AIFMD); (7) Delegation — strict rules on delegating portfolio management (cannot become a "letterbox" entity); (8) Transparency — Annex IV reporting to regulators (annual, semi-annual, quarterly depending on size); (9) Marketing — EU passport for cross-border marketing of EU AIFs by EU AIFMs (non-EU AIFs/AIFMs still rely on NPPR). Leverage reporting: AIFMs must report leverage calculated under gross approach and commitment approach; funds exceeding 3x commitment leverage ("substantially leveraged") receive additional supervisory attention.

## SECTION IV: UK AIFMD POST-BREXIT
Post-Brexit, the UK operates a parallel UK-AIFMD regime (substantially identical to EU AIFMD but onshored via statutory instruments). Key differences: (1) UK AIFMs lost EU passporting rights for marketing into EU member states; must now use NPPR (National Private Placement Regime) country-by-country; (2) EU AIFMs lost ability to use UK passport; must register under UK NPPR with FCA to market to UK investors; (3) Depositary requirements — UK AIFs require UK depositary; EU AIFs marketing in UK can use EU depositary. Most large UK HFs have responded by establishing EU-authorized affiliates (typically in Dublin or Luxembourg) for EU distribution. The loss of passporting has been a moderate inconvenience rather than existential — the UK HF industry has not relocated en masse.

## SECTION V: MiFID II APPLICABILITY
MiFID II (Markets in Financial Instruments Directive II, January 2018) is the EU rule framework for investment services and trading. UK onshored MiFID II post-Brexit. Applies to HFs that: (1) Execute trades (most HFs use sell-side execution, but any direct market access brings MiFID obligations); (2) Provide investment advice (limited HF activity); (3) Deal on own account. Key requirements impacting HFs: (a) Best execution — documented best ex policy, factor weighting (price, cost, speed, likelihood), regular reviews; (b) Transaction reporting — every trade reported to regulator (via approved reporting mechanism — ARM); (c) Transparency — pre-trade and post-trade transparency for certain instruments; (d) Research unbundling — buy-side must pay for sell-side research separately from execution commissions (CSA or direct payment), driving dramatic changes in research consumption post-2018 — many smaller HFs stopped paying for research; (e) Product governance — target market identification and distribution controls (limited HF impact); (f) Inducements — restrictions on receiving non-monetary benefits (limited HF impact). Research unbundling reform: July 2024 FCA/EU allowed optional rebundling of research payments with execution commissions — reversing some of MiFID II's changes.

## SECTION VI: MARKET ABUSE REGULATION (MAR)
MAR (Regulation (EU) 596/2014, effective July 2016, UK onshored) prohibits insider dealing, unlawful disclosure of inside information, and market manipulation. Scope includes financial instruments traded on regulated markets, MTFs, OTFs, and related derivatives. Key prohibitions: (1) Insider dealing — trading on inside information (precise, non-public, price-sensitive); (2) Unlawful disclosure — sharing inside information outside normal course of employment; (3) Market manipulation — transactions or orders that give false/misleading signals, secure artificial price, employ fictitious devices. Penalties: administrative (FCA fines) and criminal (Financial Services Act 2012, Criminal Justice Act 1993 insider dealing offenses). Enforcement: Market Abuse Unit (MAU) within FCA monitors trading patterns, investigates suspicious trades. Famous UK insider dealing cases: Raj Rajaratnam (US Galleon, 2009 — though NY case, had London connections); Mark Lyttleton (ex-BlackRock, 2017 FCA case, £1.8M profit, 12-month sentence); David Einhorn UK fine 2012 (£3.6M for Punch Taverns pre-placement trading); various MAR enforcement cases 2016-2024. STOR (Suspicious Transaction and Order Report) requirements: firms must report suspicious activity to FCA promptly.

## SECTION VII: SMCR DEEP DIVE
The Senior Managers and Certification Regime (SMCR) is the UK individual accountability regime for financial services, extended to asset managers (including HFs) December 2019. Components: (1) Senior Managers Regime (SMR) — specific senior functions (SMFs) requiring FCA pre-approval and personal accountability via statement of responsibilities; typical HF SMFs: SMF1 Chief Executive, SMF3 Executive Director, SMF9 Chair, SMF16 Compliance Oversight, SMF17 MLRO, SMF24 Chief Operations Officer, SMF27 Partner; (2) Certification Regime — material risk takers and customer-facing staff are "certified" annually by the firm (not pre-approved by FCA) — includes portfolio managers, traders, analysts making investment decisions; (3) Conduct Rules — all employees subject to 5 individual conduct rules (act with integrity, act with care/skill/diligence, be open and cooperative with regulators, pay due regard to customers, observe proper market standards). Key accountability mechanism: the "Duty of Responsibility" — senior managers can be personally liable for firm breaches if they fail to take "reasonable steps" to prevent them. SMCR enforcement: FCA has disciplined individuals in several HF cases. SMCR has materially changed London HF culture around documentation, delegation, and oversight. Criticism: over-bureaucratization, defensive documentation culture, especially for smaller firms.

## SECTION VIII: PRIVATE FUND ADVISER RULES (US CONTEXT)
While primarily US, HF managers with US investors or US subsidiaries must understand the SEC private fund adviser rules. Dodd-Frank (2010) required most hedge fund managers to register as investment advisers under Investment Advisers Act 1940 (threshold $150M AUM for private fund advisers). SEC registered advisers subject to: (1) Form ADV filings; (2) Form PF (private fund reporting) — similar to AIFMD Annex IV; (3) Rule 204A compliance — code of ethics, personal trading restrictions; (4) Advertising rule — now modernized as Marketing Rule (2022); (5) Custody rule — independent custodian, surprise audits; (6) Books and records requirements. SEC's 2023 Private Fund Adviser Rules attempted to impose: quarterly LP statements, audit requirements, preferential treatment disclosure, adviser-led secondary fairness opinions. 5th Circuit struck down most of these rules in National Association of Private Fund Managers v. SEC (June 2024), limiting SEC authority over private fund terms. UK HFs with US feeder funds typically have a US SEC-registered investment adviser affiliate for these requirements.

## SECTION IX: SFDR AND ESG REPORTING
SFDR (Sustainable Finance Disclosure Regulation, EU 2019, applied March 2021) requires asset managers to disclose sustainability risks and impacts. Classification: (1) Article 6 — basic SFDR compliance, no specific ESG mandate; (2) Article 8 — products that "promote" environmental or social characteristics; (3) Article 9 — products with sustainability as core objective. UK has not adopted SFDR but introduced SDR (Sustainability Disclosure Requirements) from November 2023 with similar aim but different taxonomy. Impact on HFs: growing pressure from LPs (especially European pension funds, endowments) to demonstrate ESG integration; many HFs have added ESG frameworks, exclusion lists (tobacco, weapons, fossil fuels), proxy voting policies. Greenwashing concerns have triggered regulatory scrutiny — DWS investigation 2022-2023, multiple SEC cases. Actual HF ESG substance varies widely — some funds have genuine integration, others minimal changes.

## SECTION X: SHORT SELLING REGULATION (SSR)
The EU Short Selling Regulation (2012) and UK onshored equivalent require: (1) Disclosure of net short positions — private disclosure to regulator at 0.1%, public disclosure at 0.5%, with further step-ups at 0.6%, 0.7%, etc.; (2) Ban on "naked" short selling of sovereign bonds and equities — shorts must be covered by borrow or equivalent arrangement; (3) Emergency ban powers — regulators can impose temporary short-selling bans during market stress. EU-wide short-selling bans imposed: 2008 (multiple countries banned financials shorts), March 2020 COVID (Spain, Italy, France, Austria, Belgium, Greece imposed 1-month bans). UK did not impose COVID bans. HF impact: public disclosure at 0.5% creates signal to market about short positions, forces position sizing discipline, and reveals manager activity. Short report activists (Muddy Waters, Hindenburg, Gotham City Research) target European-listed companies with short reports coinciding with disclosures — e.g., Viceroy Research vs Steinhoff 2017, Hindenburg vs Adani 2023.

## SECTION XI: EMIR AND DERIVATIVES REPORTING
EMIR (European Market Infrastructure Regulation, 2012) requires: (1) Central clearing of standardized OTC derivatives via authorized CCPs; (2) Risk mitigation for uncleared trades — portfolio compression, dispute resolution, timely confirmation, daily marking, initial margin and variation margin for uncleared OTC (under Uncleared Margin Rules phase-in 2016-2022); (3) Trade reporting — every derivative trade reported to trade repository (DTCC, UnaVista, REGIS-TR, Kaizen). HF impact: significant operational overhead, posting margin on previously unmargined trades, mandatory clearing of vanilla IRS/CDS. EMIR Refit (2019) introduced simplifications for smaller counterparties and financial counterparty small (FC-) category. UK EMIR onshored post-Brexit with FCA oversight.

## SECTION XII: UK TAKEOVER CODE
The Takeover Code (administered by the Takeover Panel) governs public M&A in the UK. Hedge funds active in UK M&A situations must understand: (1) Rule 2.7 — announcement of firm intention to make an offer (binding bid); (2) "Put Up or Shut Up" (PUSU) — target can require bidder to announce intentions within 28 days (extendable); (3) Rule 9 — mandatory bid rule at 30% shareholding (acquirer must offer to buy all outstanding shares at highest recent price); (4) Rule 8 — disclosure of dealings by parties and interested persons during offer period — event-driven HFs must disclose positions once 1% is crossed; (5) Offer period — begins with Rule 2.7 announcement, ends with offer closure or withdrawal. Activist HFs regularly use Takeover Code rules tactically: Elliott, Pelham, Teleios, Parvus, etc. have run campaigns involving UK Takeover Panel actions. Rule 8 disclosure creates information flow: HFs can observe other HFs' positions in a target via public disclosure.

## SECTION XIII: CMA AND ANTITRUST
The UK Competition and Markets Authority (CMA) reviews M&A deals on competition grounds. Post-Brexit, the CMA has jurisdiction separate from EU (DG COMP) and US (DOJ/FTC), creating triple review burden. CMA jurisdictional threshold: turnover > £70M (target) OR combined party share > 25% of UK market. Process: Phase 1 (40 days, administrative review) → if concerns, Phase 2 (24 weeks, detailed investigation) → decision (clear, prohibit, require remedies). Recent notable CMA decisions: Microsoft-Activision 2023 (initial prohibition, reversed after cloud remedies); Meta-Giphy 2021 (prohibited, Meta forced to divest); Adobe-Figma 2023 (contributed to deal termination); Amazon-iRobot (contributed to termination). HFs running UK event-driven books monitor CMA activity closely.

## SECTION XIV: CONSUMER DUTY (LIMITED HF IMPACT)
The FCA Consumer Duty (effective July 2023) imposes heightened standards on firms serving retail customers. For HFs, direct impact is limited since most HFs serve only professional investors. Indirect impact: (a) HFs with UCITS products for retail; (b) HFs distributing via retail platforms; (c) Asset owners (pension schemes, platforms) require demonstration of value from their HF allocations. The Duty requires firms to deliver "good outcomes" across four areas: products/services, price/value, consumer understanding, consumer support.

## SECTION XV: OPERATIONAL RESILIENCE
FCA operational resilience requirements (effective March 2022, full compliance March 2025) require firms to: (1) Identify important business services; (2) Set impact tolerances (maximum tolerable disruption); (3) Map dependencies — internal, third-party, intra-group; (4) Test ability to remain within impact tolerances under severe but plausible scenarios; (5) Communicate with customers during disruptions. For HFs, important services typically include: trade execution, risk management, NAV calculation, LP subscriptions/redemptions. Scenario testing: cyber incident, technology failure, loss of key service provider, pandemic, physical incident. Post-COVID experience showed that HFs generally coped well with remote-work resilience.

## SECTION XVI: DORA (EU DIGITAL OPERATIONAL RESILIENCE)
DORA (Digital Operational Resilience Act, applied January 2025) is the EU equivalent of UK operational resilience but more prescriptive on ICT (information and communication technology) risk management. Applies to EU financial firms including AIFMs. Requirements: (1) ICT risk management framework; (2) Incident reporting to regulators; (3) Digital operational resilience testing; (4) Third-party ICT risk management; (5) Information sharing arrangements. UK has not adopted DORA but has parallel operational resilience rules. EU-managed HFs must comply with DORA from 2025; UK HFs with EU affiliates have compliance burden.

## SECTION XVII: REGULATORY CAPITAL UNDER MIFIDPRU
The UK Investment Firm Prudential Regime (MIFIDPRU, effective January 2022) replaced the previous IFPRU/BIPRU regimes. Classification: (1) Class 1 large firms — subject to CRR (bank-like rules); (2) Class 2 firms — subject to MIFIDPRU standard rules; (3) Class 3 small and non-interconnected firms (SNI) — lighter requirements. HF managers typically fall into Class 2 or Class 3. Capital requirements: (a) Permanent minimum capital (PMC) — typically £75k-£750k depending on activities; (b) Fixed overheads requirement (FOR) — 1/4 of annual fixed overheads; (c) K-factor requirement — based on assets under management (K-AUM), client money held (K-CMH), etc. Total capital requirement = higher of PMC, FOR, or K-factor sum. Additional capital via ICARA (Internal Capital Adequacy and Risk Assessment) process — firm's own assessment of risk and wind-down capital needs. MIFIDPRU requires annual Liquidity Assessment and own funds disclosure.

## SECTION XVIII: CROSS-BORDER MARKETING AND NPPR
Post-Brexit, UK HFs marketing to EU investors rely on National Private Placement Regimes (NPPR) — country-by-country registration with each EU member state's regulator. Process varies by country — some countries (Luxembourg, Germany, Netherlands) have straightforward registration; others (Italy, France) more cumbersome; Spain has effectively closed NPPR for non-EU AIFMs. Reverse solicitation ("passive marketing") — where an EU investor approaches the fund unsolicited — is permissible but narrowly defined. Enforcement has increased: ESMA published "Guidelines on Marketing Communications" 2021 limiting pre-marketing activities. The EU Cross-Border Distribution of Funds Directive (CBDF, 2019) tightened pre-marketing definitions. Some UK HFs have established EU-authorized affiliates (Dublin, Luxembourg) to avoid NPPR complexity and enable passporting; others accept NPPR burden to maintain UK-only structure.

## SECTION XIX: ENFORCEMENT CASE STUDIES
Key FCA enforcement cases for HF learning: (1) BlueBay Asset Management 2014 £68k fine — conflicts of interest in co-investments; (2) Martin Pirrie 2017 (BlueCrest) — insider dealing conviction; (3) Mark Stevenson/Mark Wang ex-Credit Suisse 2014 — gilt market manipulation conviction; (4) Achilles Macris ex-JPM 2016 — partially overturned; (5) Various broker/analyst insider cases; (6) Numerous "private" speaking bans and enforcement settlements against individuals. SEC parallel cases important to London HFs with US operations: BlueCrest $170M 2020 SEC settlement (directed BSMA allocation); Steven Cohen / SAC Capital 2013 — insider trading, firm converted to family office Point72; Odey Asset Management 2023 — pre-collapse FCA investigation of management controls; Crispin Odey personal conduct allegations. The common lesson: controls, documentation, and individual accountability are central to regulatory risk management.

## SECTION XX: REGULATORY INTERVIEW PREP
Interview questions: (1) "Explain AIFMD." — Describe EU framework, authorization, depositary, valuation, leverage reporting, UK onshored version post-Brexit. (2) "What is SMCR?" — Senior Managers Regime + Certification Regime + Conduct Rules; individual accountability; applies to asset managers since Dec 2019. (3) "What did MiFID II change for HFs?" — Best execution documentation, transaction reporting, research unbundling (subsequently softened 2024), product governance, inducement rules. (4) "How does market abuse enforcement work in London?" — FCA Market Abuse Unit + STOR reports + criminal referrals; MAR framework; recent cases. (5) "What is the difference between EU and UK AIFMD post-Brexit?" — Parallel regimes, passporting lost, NPPR required. (6) "Explain SFDR Article 8 vs 9." — Article 8 promotes E/S characteristics; Article 9 has sustainability as objective; UK equivalent SDR. (7) "How do short-selling disclosure requirements work?" — 0.1% private, 0.5% public disclosure under SSR. (8) "What are the Takeover Code Rule 2.7 and Rule 8?" — Binding bid announcement; dealing disclosures by parties in offer period. (9) "Explain MIFIDPRU." — UK investment firm prudential regime from Jan 2022; Class 1/2/3 classification; capital based on PMC/FOR/K-factors. (10) "What's the ICARA?" — Internal Capital Adequacy and Risk Assessment; firm's own assessment of capital needs beyond minimum; required annually.

---

# CHAPTER 42 — LP UNIVERSE, CAPITAL RAISING & INVESTOR RELATIONS

## SECTION I: THE GLOBAL HF LP UNIVERSE
The hedge fund LP (Limited Partner, i.e., investor) universe has evolved dramatically from the high-net-worth-dominated industry of the 1990s to a highly institutionalized base today. Current composition (2025): (1) Pension funds — ~25% of global HF AUM, including US public pensions (CalPERS, CalSTRS, Teachers' Retirement System of Texas, NY State Common, Washington State, Oregon PERS), US corporate pensions, Canadian pension plans (CPPIB, Ontario Teachers, PSP Investments, OMERS, CDPQ), UK DB pension schemes (USS, BT Pension, Rolls-Royce, BBC, BP), European pension funds (ABP/APG Netherlands, ATP Denmark, AP funds Sweden); (2) Sovereign wealth funds — ~15%, including ADIA (Abu Dhabi), Mubadala, PIF (Saudi), KIA (Kuwait), QIA (Qatar), GIC Singapore, Temasek, NBIM (Norway GPFG), AFIM (Australia Future Fund), SAMA (Saudi central bank historically); (3) Endowments and foundations — ~15%, led by Yale, Harvard, Princeton, Stanford, MIT, Notre Dame, Texas UTIMCO, Wellcome Trust; (4) Funds of hedge funds — ~10%, declining from historical peaks, major players Blackstone BAAM (~$80B), Grosvenor Capital, UBS O'Connor, Goldman HFS, Lighthouse, Aurora, Rock Creek, PAAMCO Prisma, Permal, Man FRM; (5) Family offices and HNW via private banks — ~15%; (6) Insurance companies — ~10%, including AIG, Prudential, MetLife, Allianz, Zurich, Ping An, Manulife; (7) Consultants' discretionary mandates — via Albourne, Aksia, Cambridge Associates, Mercer.

## SECTION II: FUND OF FUNDS DECLINE
Fund of hedge funds (FoHF) structures — which pool capital from smaller investors and invest across multiple underlying HFs — were the dominant institutional access channel in 1995-2008, peaking at ~45% of industry AUM. Post-2008 declined due to: (1) Fee compression — FoHFs charge additional layer of fees (typically 1%+10%) on top of underlying fund fees, creating drag; (2) Failed diversification — 2008 showed FoHFs failed to diversify away from systemic shocks; (3) Madoff exposure — many FoHFs (including Fairfield Greenwich, Kingate, Tremont) had significant Madoff losses, destroying credibility; (4) LP sophistication — large LPs built in-house HF programs reducing FoHF dependency; (5) Fee transparency demand. Surviving FoHFs have pivoted to: customized / advisory mandates (bespoke portfolios rather than pooled vehicles), co-investment platforms, emerging manager seeding, access to capacity-constrained funds. Blackstone BAAM remains the largest FoHF with ~$80B, Grosvenor ~$65B, UBS O'Connor ~$15B.

## SECTION III: SWFS AND PENSION CAPITAL FLOWS
Sovereign wealth funds (SWFs) have become the most important HF allocators. ADIA (~$800B) allocates ~10% to HFs (~$80B), making it potentially the largest HF LP globally. Kuwait Investment Authority (~$700B), GIC Singapore (~$800B), Temasek (~$300B), NBIM Norway (~$1.5T but limited HF exposure — mostly passive), KIC Korea, CIC China, PIF Saudi, Mubadala — all material HF allocators. Canadian pension plans have been particularly active in direct HF allocation (reducing FoHF usage) with sophisticated in-house HF teams: CPPIB, Ontario Teachers', PSP Investments employ 20-50+ HF analysts each. US public pensions have been more mixed — CalPERS famously exited HFs in 2014 citing complexity/cost, while Texas Teachers and many others remain committed. European pension demand: ABP/APG (Netherlands), ATP (Denmark), BVK (Germany), AP funds (Sweden) are major HF allocators.

## SECTION IV: ENDOWMENT MODEL
The "Yale Model" or "Endowment Model" — pioneered by David Swensen at Yale Investments Office (1985-2021) — was influential in directing capital to alternatives including HFs, private equity, natural resources, real estate. Yale target allocation historically: ~25-30% absolute return (hedge funds), ~25% PE/VC, ~15% real assets, ~10% public equity, ~5% fixed income. Average endowment allocation to HFs: ~20-25%. Key endowment HF allocators: Yale (~$42B), Harvard (~$50B), Princeton (~$35B), Stanford (~$40B), MIT (~$25B), University of Texas UTIMCO (~$70B), Notre Dame (~$18B). Endowment HF selection: focuses on high-conviction managers, willingness to accept illiquidity for alpha, deep manager due diligence, long-term relationships. Swensen's death in 2021 prompted some soul-searching but the model persists.

## SECTION V: DUE DILIGENCE PROCESS
Institutional LP due diligence on HFs is rigorous and typically takes 6-12 months: (1) Initial screening — meet at conference, review pitch book, check performance; (2) Investment due diligence (IDD) — team assessment, strategy analysis, portfolio construction, risk management, edge/alpha source identification; (3) Operational due diligence (ODD) — separate team reviews infrastructure, compliance, legal, tax, valuation, service providers, cybersecurity — ODD has veto power and can kill investments that IDD otherwise favors; (4) Reference calls — multiple references from other LPs, service providers, ex-employees; (5) Background checks — commercial databases (BackTrack, Kroll) on key principals; (6) On-site visits — office visits, team meetings, system demonstrations; (7) Investment committee presentation; (8) Negotiation — fees, side letter terms, lock-up; (9) Legal review; (10) Subscription documents, AML/KYC, funding. Top ODD consultants: Albourne (largest), Aksia, Cliffwater, Mercer, NEPC, Cambridge Associates.

## SECTION VI: PITCH BOOKS AND MARKETING MATERIALS
HF pitch books typically contain: (1) Firm overview — history, ownership, AUM, key statistics; (2) Team bios — PMs, analysts, operations, board; (3) Investment process — strategy description, idea generation, position sizing, risk management, portfolio construction; (4) Historical performance — net returns by period, drawdowns, Sharpe/Sortino ratios, correlations to indices, strategy attribution; (5) Risk metrics — VaR, stress tests, factor exposures; (6) Portfolio characteristics — sector/geography/market cap exposure, top positions, liquidity profile; (7) Case studies — sample trades illustrating process; (8) Fee structure — management fee, performance fee, hurdle, high-water mark, lock-up; (9) Service providers — PB, admin, auditor, legal; (10) Contact information. Pitch books typically 40-80 pages. Regulated under MiFID II marketing communication rules, SEC Marketing Rule (2022), SFDR disclosure requirements. Pre-marketing activities tightly regulated — must not constitute "marketing" before fund authorization.

## SECTION VII: INVESTOR RELATIONS FUNCTION
HF IR function evolved dramatically from part-time CFO function to dedicated teams: (1) Small fund ($100M-$1B): 1-2 IR professionals; (2) Mid fund ($1-5B): 3-5 IR team; (3) Large fund ($5B+): 10-30+ IR / business development professionals. IR responsibilities: (a) Existing LP servicing — monthly letters, quarterly reviews, ad-hoc queries, consultant reviews, attribution analysis; (b) New capital raising — pitch meetings, conference attendance, third-party marketer relationships; (c) Marketing materials — pitch books, DDQs (due diligence questionnaires), ESG reports, regulatory reports; (d) Operational DDQ support — coordinating ODD visits, responding to lengthy DDQ templates; (e) Consultant relationships — Albourne, Aksia, Mercer, Cambridge; (f) Conference presence — CAIS, Milken, SALT, HFM events. Top IR hires are senior, well-connected professionals often ex-LP side or ex-placement agent. Compensation: senior IR professionals at large HFs earn $1-5M per year; head of IR can exceed $10M at flagship firms.

## SECTION VIII: PLACEMENT AGENTS AND THIRD-PARTY MARKETERS
Placement agents / third-party marketers (3PMs) are independent firms that raise capital for HFs in exchange for retainer + success fees: (1) Retainer: $20-50k/month; (2) Success fee: 1-2% of capital raised upfront, or 15-20% of management fees for 3-5 years (trail). MiFID II requires 3PMs to be authorized investment firms. Major 3PMs: Credit Suisse PFG (historical, now UBS), Cebile Capital, First Avenue, Campbell Lutyens (more PE-focused), Evercore Private Funds Group, Eaton Partners (part of Stifel), Lazard Private Capital Advisory, Park Hill Group (now Blackstone). 3PMs have become more PE-focused as HF fundraising shifted toward direct LP relationships. PCA (Private Capital Advisory) has grown as secondary market activity expanded.

## SECTION IX: CAPACITY MANAGEMENT
High-performing HFs face the challenge of managing capacity. Excess AUM can dilute returns: (1) Market impact grows with size in less-liquid strategies; (2) Idea quality declines as fund must deploy more capital into less-conviction positions; (3) Team scaling challenges. Solutions: (a) Hard close — refuse new capital beyond a defined limit (e.g., Citadel has periodically returned capital or capped new subscriptions; Renaissance Medallion returned all outside capital in 2005); (b) Soft close — maintain existing relationships but no new LPs; (c) Capacity fees — charge a premium for new capacity; (d) Return of capital — distribute excess back to LPs. Famous capacity management: SAC Capital returned capital periodically; Tudor, Caxton, Millennium have managed capacity; Brevan Howard closed flagship in 2016 but has reopened; Renaissance keeps Medallion internal. The economic tension: more AUM → higher management fees → more revenue, but potentially lower returns → lower performance fees → lost talent.

## SECTION X: SEEDING AND LAUNCH CAPITAL
HF seed capital providers fund new launches in exchange for equity stakes in the management company plus reduced fees: (1) Blackstone Strategic Alliance Fund — largest seeder, has backed Senator, Arrowgrass, Nut Tree, Kirkoswald, many others; (2) Blue Owl (formerly Dyal Capital) — GP stakes investor, holds minority stakes in ~75+ alternative managers; (3) Goldman Sachs Petershill — GP stakes investor; (4) Wafra (backed by Kuwait SWF) — seeder and GP stakes; (5) PAAMCO Prisma — historical seeder; (6) Stable Asset Management, Borealis, Stonehaven — smaller seeders. Seed terms typically: $100-300M initial investment, 15-25% of gross revenue of management company for 5-10 years, reduced fees, governance rights. Post-2020 seed market expanded with multiple funds established: Mousse Partners (JP Morgan spinout), etc. Seed capital is particularly valuable for first-time managers lacking institutional track record. The GP stakes market for established managers ($5B+ AUM) has exploded, with Blue Owl, Goldman, Blackstone all active.

## SECTION XI: DDQ PROCESS
The Due Diligence Questionnaire (DDQ) is the standard document LPs require HFs to complete. Standard industry templates: AIMA DDQ, ILPA DDQ, SBAI Open Protocol, custom LP DDQs. A typical DDQ covers 200-500 questions across: (1) Firm overview — history, ownership, team, AUM, assets by strategy/LP type; (2) Investment team — detailed bios, experience, turnover, compensation structure; (3) Investment strategy — philosophy, process, idea generation, position sizing, risk management; (4) Portfolio construction — diversification, concentration, leverage; (5) Risk management — VaR, stress testing, risk committees, limits; (6) Trading and execution — OMS, EMS, counterparties, best execution; (7) Operations — trade settlement, NAV calculation, cash management; (8) Compliance — code of ethics, personal trading, AML, KYC; (9) Legal and tax — fund documents, side letters, tax structure; (10) Service providers — PB, admin, auditor, legal, depositary, custodian; (11) Valuation — valuation policies, Level 1/2/3 breakdown, independent pricing; (12) Disaster recovery and BCP; (13) Cybersecurity; (14) ESG (increasingly); (15) Performance and AUM history. DDQ completion can take 2-4 weeks of dedicated effort. Modern funds use DDQ management platforms (Canoe, Diligend, Dynamo) to streamline responses and update across LPs.

## SECTION XII: INVESTOR REPORTING CADENCE
Standard investor communication cadence: (1) Monthly letter — 1-5 pages, performance summary, commentary on month, portfolio positioning, market outlook; (2) Monthly/weekly NAV — estimate and final NAV published; (3) Quarterly letter — more detailed commentary, case studies, longer-term view; (4) Quarterly risk report — VaR, stress tests, factor exposures, position concentrations; (5) Annual audited financials — full financial statements with notes; (6) Ad-hoc updates — major events, significant portfolio changes, market dislocations. LP expectations vary: institutional LPs often require daily or weekly NAV estimates, transparency reports, position-level reporting (under NDA), risk factor decomposition. Transparency has increased dramatically post-2008, with "transparency funds" offering full position disclosure to LPs with appropriate confidentiality protections.

## SECTION XIII: FEE NEGOTIATION DYNAMICS
Modern LP-GP fee negotiation is sophisticated: (1) Ticket size discount — $50M+ tickets receive 10-25bp management fee reduction; $100M+ receive further discounts; (2) Founder share class — early LPs receive discounted fees as permanent benefit; (3) Lock-up discount — 2-3 year lock-up in exchange for 25-50bp fee reduction; (4) Performance fee hurdle — LP may negotiate 4-5% hurdle (fee only above this level); (5) Most Favored Nation (MFN) — LP insists on receiving best terms offered to any other LP; (6) Claw-back mechanisms — performance fees partially clawed back if subsequent losses; (7) Management fee offset — expense reimbursements (research, travel) credited against management fee. Large LPs (pensions, SWFs) with $100M+ tickets have substantial negotiating leverage; smaller LPs accept standard terms. Capacity-constrained successful funds have the opposite dynamic — LPs accept standard (or even premium) terms for access.

## SECTION XIV: SECONDARY MARKET FOR HF INTERESTS
A secondary market for HF interests has developed but is less liquid than the PE secondary market. Mechanics: LP wishing to exit an HF position before lock-up or redemption date sells its interest to another LP. Challenges: (1) Valuation uncertainty — buyer cannot easily verify NAV or positions; (2) Transfer restrictions — HFs often limit transfers; (3) Limited buyer universe; (4) Discount to NAV typically 5-20% depending on fund quality and timing. Specialized secondary buyers: Landmark Partners, Cebile Capital, Setter Capital, Lexington Partners. The PE secondary market has grown enormously — HF secondary has been smaller due to shorter lock-ups and redemption flexibility reducing need for secondaries.

## SECTION XV: LP ADVISORY COMMITTEES (LPACs)
Some HFs operate LP Advisory Committees (LPACs) consisting of significant LP representatives. LPAC responsibilities: (1) Advise on conflicts of interest; (2) Approve certain transactions (e.g., affiliated counterparty trades); (3) Provide perspective on major decisions. LPACs are more common in PE than HF structures but have appeared in some large HFs. LPAC membership is a privilege for major LPs.

## SECTION XVI: CAPITAL RAISING IN PRACTICE
Practical capital raising dynamics: (1) Existing LP re-ups — majority of net new capital for established funds comes from existing LPs increasing commitments; new LPs are slower; (2) Warm leads — referrals from current LPs, prior relationships, consultants; (3) Cold outreach — limited success, typically requires years of relationship building; (4) Conference attendance — 5-15 conferences per year for large HF IR teams (CAIS, Milken, SALT, HFM events, prime broker events); (5) Roadshows — trip-based coverage of specific regions (NY, London, Zurich, Geneva, Dubai, Singapore, Hong Kong, Tokyo, Sydney); (6) Seed and emerging manager programs — CPPIB, Teacher Retirement System of Texas, UTIMCO, New Jersey Division of Investment run emerging manager programs. Fundraising velocity: top-tier managers raise $1-5B+ annually; established mid-tier $200M-$1B; emerging managers $50-200M; launches $100-500M (if successful). Capital raising timeline from initial meeting to funded: 6-24 months for institutional LPs.

## SECTION XVII: REDEMPTION DYNAMICS
Redemption dynamics are critical: (1) Performance-driven — underperforming funds see redemptions accelerate; (2) Liquidity-driven — LPs rebalancing or needing cash (endowment spending, pension benefit payments); (3) Asset allocation changes — strategic shifts away from HFs; (4) Gating — historical gating events damage reputation and trigger redemption cycles; (5) Key person — PM departures trigger LP redemption rights. Historical examples: 2008 crisis caused ~20% industry redemptions Q4 2008-Q1 2009; underperforming long-only funds 2015-2016 saw accelerated redemptions; Odey collapse 2023 — $850M redemption requests within weeks of Crispin Odey allegations; Archegos cascade triggered prime counterparty reviews but not LP redemptions (family office). Post-gate reputation: funds that gated in 2008 had significant long-term credibility damage. Management of redemption flows: (a) Sufficient cash/liquid positions to meet gates; (b) Clear communication with LPs; (c) Fair treatment across investor base; (d) Potentially raising new capital to offset.

## SECTION XVIII: CONSULTANT DYNAMICS
Investment consultants play a significant role in institutional HF allocation: (1) Albourne Partners (largest HF consultant, ~$650B advised, founded 1994 by Simon Ruddick, based London/NY/Tokyo); (2) Aksia (founded 2006 by Jim Vos, ~$250B advised, focused on alternatives); (3) Cliffwater (~$100B advised); (4) Cambridge Associates (generalist, strong in endowments); (5) Mercer (generalist large); (6) NEPC (generalist US); (7) Meketa, Wilshire, RVK. Consultants run quantitative and qualitative screens, due diligence, portfolio construction advice. Albourne's Consultant Questionnaire is widely used. Consultants charge retainer fees from LPs ($50k-$500k+ per year per client) and may charge HFs for database listing (relatively small fee). Consultants' recommended lists heavily influence institutional allocation — being "approved" by Albourne is materially valuable for HFs.

## SECTION XIX: IR CAREER PATH
HF IR / business development career path: (1) Entry level: analyst / associate at $100-200k base + bonus, responsible for DDQ responses, data updates, LP query responses; (2) Mid-level: VP / Director at $200-500k, responsible for client coverage, meeting prep, relationship management; (3) Senior: Head of IR / Partner at $500k-$5M+, responsible for strategy, major LP relationships, capital raising targets; (4) Top flagships: IR partner can earn $5-20M at firms like Millennium, Citadel, Point72. IR candidates come from: (a) LP side (endowments, pensions) with relationships; (b) Prime broker cap intro; (c) Third-party marketers; (d) Investment banking private funds group; (e) Prior HF IR. The role has grown in importance as HF competition for capital has intensified. Communication skills, relationship building, technical understanding of strategies, and integrity are the key competencies.

## SECTION XX: IR / CAPITAL RAISING INTERVIEW PREP
Interview questions: (1) "Walk me through how you would raise capital for a new $200M long/short equity launch." — Identify LP targets (endowments, FOFs, family offices for launches), seed capital, initial outreach, timeline expectations. (2) "What does an LP look for in a new manager?" — Track record (personal if not firm), team quality, process clarity, infrastructure, alignment, capacity management discipline. (3) "Explain the difference between IDD and ODD." — IDD focuses on investment merits; ODD on operations; ODD typically has veto. (4) "What is Albourne?" — Largest HF consultant, uses Consultant Questionnaire, approved list heavily influences allocation. (5) "How does a pass-through fee structure affect LP economics?" — Description of expense passthrough vs management fee; trade-off between transparency and cost. (6) "Explain the Madoff ODD failure." — 17-year Ponzi undetected; third-tier auditor, OTC broker, cash custody all ignored; major post-2008 ODD wake-up. (7) "What is the Yale model?" — Swensen's endowment allocation framework favoring alternatives; long-term, illiquid, manager-driven. (8) "How has fund of funds declined?" — 45% → 10% of industry AUM; fee compression, 2008 failures, Madoff, LP sophistication. (9) "Explain seed capital economics." — $100-300M initial + 15-25% gross revenue share for 5-10 years; top seeders Blackstone BAAM, Blue Owl/Dyal, Goldman Petershill. (10) "What are the key questions in a DDQ?" — Team, strategy, process, risk, operations, compliance, valuation, service providers; typically 200-500 questions.

---

# CHAPTER 43 — RISK, VAR, PORTFOLIO CONSTRUCTION & ATTRIBUTION

## SECTION I: RISK MANAGEMENT AS HF CORE FUNCTION
Risk management is the central operational discipline of modern hedge funds. The CRO (Chief Risk Officer) or Head of Risk role has grown in stature and power — at many multi-strategy platforms, the CRO has authority to reduce PM exposure unilaterally and report directly to the CEO with independence from portfolio management. Risk functions typically comprise: (1) Market risk — quantitative measurement of exposure to price movements (equity, rates, credit, FX, commodities, vol); (2) Credit and counterparty risk — exposure to counterparty defaults; (3) Liquidity risk — ability to exit positions under stress; (4) Operational risk — systems, processes, people failures; (5) Legal and compliance risk; (6) Model risk — risk of model errors or misuse. Risk functions at multi-strat pods can employ 50-200+ risk professionals across these disciplines. The risk function sets limits, monitors exposures, runs stress tests, and has enforcement authority.

## SECTION II: VALUE-AT-RISK (VaR) METHODOLOGIES
Value-at-Risk (VaR) is the dominant quantitative risk measure. Definition: the maximum loss expected over a specified time horizon at a specified confidence level. Typical parameters: 95% or 99% confidence, 1-day or 10-day horizon. Example: "1-day 95% VaR = $10M" means there's a 5% probability of losing more than $10M in any given day. Methodologies: (1) Parametric VaR — assumes normally distributed returns, VaR = Z × σ × √t where Z is confidence multiplier (1.645 for 95%, 2.326 for 99%), σ is position volatility, t is horizon; simple but ignores fat tails; (2) Historical simulation VaR — applies historical scenarios (e.g., last 500 trading days) to current portfolio, computes P&L distribution, takes appropriate percentile; empirical but backward-looking; (3) Monte Carlo VaR — generates random scenarios from fitted distribution, computes P&L, takes percentile; flexible but computationally expensive; (4) Filtered Historical Simulation — combines historical returns with GARCH-filtered volatility adjustment. Most institutional HFs use historical simulation as primary methodology with parametric overlays for stress scenarios.

## SECTION III: VAR LIMITATIONS AND CVAR
VaR has well-known limitations: (1) Does not measure losses beyond VaR threshold (tail risk); (2) Not sub-additive — VaR of combined portfolio can exceed sum of component VaRs (violates coherence); (3) Backward-looking by construction; (4) Misleads during regime changes; (5) Can be gamed by repositioning around VaR window boundaries. Conditional Value-at-Risk (CVaR), also called Expected Shortfall, addresses these: CVaR is the expected loss conditional on being in the tail (i.e., worse than VaR threshold). Example: "1-day 95% CVaR = $15M" means the average loss in the worst 5% of days is $15M. CVaR is sub-additive and coherent, preferred by risk theorists. Basel III market risk framework adopted Expected Shortfall at 97.5% confidence replacing VaR at 99%. Most HFs report both VaR and CVaR to LPs and internally.

## SECTION IV: STRESS TESTING
Stress testing complements VaR by applying specific scenarios to the portfolio: (1) Historical scenarios — 1987 crash (-22% S&P in a day), 1994 bond crisis, 1998 LTCM/Russia, 2000 dotcom bust, 2008 GFC, 2010 flash crash, 2015 SNB franc unpeg, 2020 COVID crash, 2022 UK gilt/LDI crisis; (2) Hypothetical scenarios — +100bp rate shock, -20% equity crash, USD +15%, oil -30%, credit spreads +200bp, vol +50%; (3) Reverse stress tests — what scenario would cause X% loss (working backward from limit); (4) Factor stress — shocking specific risk factors (value vs growth, momentum crash, carry unwind); (5) Liquidity stress — simulated forced liquidation scenarios with market impact. Stress tests are run daily at sophisticated HFs; regulators (FCA via SREP, SEC via Form PF, AIFMD Annex IV) require regular stress testing disclosure. Limits are set based on stress test outcomes, not just VaR.

## SECTION V: PORTFOLIO CONSTRUCTION FRAMEWORKS
Portfolio construction seeks optimal positioning given expected returns, risks, and constraints. Classical frameworks: (1) Markowitz mean-variance optimization (1952) — maximize return for given variance, or minimize variance for given return; requires expected returns, variances, covariances as inputs; theoretical foundation of modern portfolio theory; (2) Black-Litterman (1990) — blends investor views with market equilibrium to generate stable optimal weights; addresses sensitivity of MV to inputs; (3) Risk parity — allocate risk (not capital) equally across assets or strategies; Bridgewater All Weather, AQR Risk Parity funds; (4) Risk budgeting — allocate total portfolio VaR across PMs/strategies with defined caps; standard at multi-strat pods; (5) Kelly criterion — size positions proportional to edge/variance for long-run growth optimality, though usually scaled (half-Kelly, quarter-Kelly) for risk management; (6) Hierarchical Risk Parity (HRP) — Marcos López de Prado's improvement using clustering to avoid Markowitz inversion instability.

## SECTION VI: FACTOR MODELS AND RISK DECOMPOSITION
Factor models decompose returns into systematic factors and idiosyncratic noise. Standard equity factor models: (1) CAPM (1964) — single factor (market beta); (2) Fama-French 3-factor (1992) — market, size (SMB), value (HML); (3) Carhart 4-factor (1997) — adds momentum (MOM); (4) Fama-French 5-factor (2015) — adds profitability (RMW), investment (CMA); (5) Commercial models — Barra (MSCI, industry standard), Axioma, Northfield, Bloomberg PORT. Barra equity risk model contains 40-80+ factors including country, industry, style (value, growth, size, momentum, volatility, quality, liquidity, leverage, earnings yield, growth). Factor attribution: monthly/quarterly decomposition of P&L into factor contributions + stock-specific (idiosyncratic) P&L. Idiosyncratic alpha is the true measure of stock-picking skill, net of factor exposures. Multi-strategy funds typically run factor risk reports daily, identifying unintended factor exposures and hedging them.

## SECTION VII: BETA AND NET EXPOSURE MANAGEMENT
Beta management is fundamental to long/short equity and event-driven strategies: (1) Beta-adjusted net exposure — longs weighted by beta minus shorts weighted by beta; a 50% long / 50% short book with average long beta 1.2 and short beta 0.8 has net beta of 0.5×1.2 - 0.5×0.8 = 0.2, i.e., 20% net long market exposure; (2) Dollar net exposure is a crude measure; beta-adjusted net is more accurate; (3) Hedging with index futures (SPX, NDX, Russell 2000, STOXX 600, FTSE 100, Nikkei) to adjust net exposure; (4) Single-name vs basket vs index hedges — single-name most accurate but expensive; index most efficient but may not hedge specific risks. Typical long/short equity funds run 0-40% net long, though some specialist directional funds run higher nets.

## SECTION VIII: CORRELATION AND DIVERSIFICATION
Correlation assumptions underpin diversification benefits. Challenges: (1) Correlations are not stable — they typically rise during crises ("correlations go to 1 in crisis"), reducing diversification exactly when needed; (2) Correlation is a linear measure; non-linear dependencies (tail correlations, copulas) may be more relevant; (3) Historical correlations can be misleading for new regimes. Risk parity funds (Bridgewater All Weather) rely on stable stock-bond negative correlation — this broke down in 2022 when both fell sharply. Multi-strategy pods try to achieve "uncorrelated alpha streams" — 100+ PMs whose P&Ls are sufficiently uncorrelated that portfolio Sharpe exceeds individual PM Sharpes. Empirical multi-strat Sharpes have been 2-4 vs individual PM Sharpes often 1-2, validating diversification when properly constructed.

## SECTION IX: LIQUIDITY RISK FRAMEWORKS
Liquidity risk frameworks: (1) Market liquidity — ability to exit positions without significant market impact; measured by days-to-liquidate at various participation rates (10% of ADV common), bid-ask spreads, market depth; (2) Funding liquidity — ability to finance positions; counterparty credit lines, margin requirements, repo access; (3) Liquidity mismatch — divergence between fund redemption terms and portfolio liquidity; (4) Liquidity coverage — ratio of liquid assets to redemption obligations; (5) Liquidity stress testing — modeling forced liquidation scenarios. Liquidity metrics: % of portfolio liquidable in 1 day, 1 week, 1 month at 10%/20%/30% participation; fund redemption terms matched against portfolio liquidity (if 25% of NAV is redeemable per quarter, 25% should be liquidable in 90 days at reasonable participation rates). LTCM 1998 and Amaranth 2006 are liquidity-driven blowups. Post-2008, sophisticated HFs match fund terms to portfolio liquidity more carefully.

## SECTION X: LEVERAGE LIMITS AND METRICS
Leverage limits vary by strategy and firm: (1) Long/short equity — typical gross leverage 1-3x; (2) Market neutral — 3-6x gross; (3) Event-driven — 1.5-2.5x; (4) Credit — 2-4x; (5) Fixed income RV — 10-30x (high notional, low return per unit); (6) Macro — 3-10x (inflated by FX/rates notionals); (7) Multi-strat aggregate — 5-10x; (8) Pod-level — individual PMs typically 3-8x gross within platform. Leverage is measured multiple ways: gross market value / NAV, notional / NAV, risk-weighted exposure / NAV, VaR / NAV. Regulatory leverage (AIFMD, Form PF) uses both commitment (netted) and gross (un-netted) approaches. Central limit: leverage should never exceed what liquidity conditions permit unwinding during stress.

## SECTION XI: CONCENTRATION LIMITS
Position concentration limits control single-name / single-event risk: (1) Single name limit — max % of NAV in any position, typically 5-10% for high-conviction names, 2-5% for smaller positions; (2) Single issuer — max exposure to an issuer across securities (equity, bonds, derivatives); (3) Single sector/industry — max 15-30% in any GICS sector; (4) Single country — max 10-25% in non-core countries; (5) Single theme — max exposure to correlated positions (e.g., all China tech stocks); (6) Single PM — pod shops cap any individual PM's book to avoid concentration in one person's judgment. Archegos cautionary tale: concentrated positions across multiple primes enabled aggregate exposures that would have violated any single-PB concentration limit.

## SECTION XII: LIMIT FRAMEWORKS AND BREACH PROTOCOLS
Modern HFs have comprehensive limit frameworks: (1) Soft limits — internal warning levels triggering review; (2) Hard limits — absolute caps, breach triggers mandatory reduction; (3) Escalation — breaches escalated to CRO, CEO, board; (4) Daily monitoring — automated systems flag breaches; (5) Override protocols — limit increases require formal approval; (6) Temporary overrides during market events. Examples of limits: VaR budget ($5M per PM), gross exposure cap (5x NAV per PM), stop-loss (PM down 5% quarter = reduce to 75% position size; down 7.5% = 50%; down 10% = wound down). Multi-strat platforms are famous for tight stop-loss discipline — PMs who breach are cut quickly.

## SECTION XIII: PERFORMANCE ATTRIBUTION
Performance attribution decomposes P&L into sources: (1) Strategy attribution — within multi-strat, allocate P&L across strategies (equity, macro, credit, etc.); (2) Sector/geography attribution — within equity, by sector/region; (3) Factor attribution — within positions, by style factors (value, growth, momentum, etc.); (4) Security selection vs allocation — Brinson attribution (Brinson-Hood-Beebower 1986, Brinson-Fachler 1985); (5) Idiosyncratic vs beta — alpha (skill) vs beta (market) component; (6) Risk-adjusted metrics — contribution to VaR, contribution to Sharpe. Attribution is done monthly, quarterly, annually — both internally (PM reviews, bonus calculations) and for LPs (quarterly letters, DDQ responses).

## SECTION XIV: SHARPE, SORTINO, CALMAR, INFORMATION RATIO
Risk-adjusted performance metrics: (1) Sharpe ratio = (Return - Risk-free rate) / Volatility; benchmark >1 good, >2 excellent, >3 exceptional; Renaissance Medallion ~3-4 over decades is legendary; (2) Sortino ratio = (Return - Risk-free rate) / Downside volatility; uses downside deviation instead of total volatility; more relevant for asymmetric strategies; (3) Calmar ratio = Annual return / Maximum drawdown; focuses on peak-to-trough loss; >1 good, >3 excellent; (4) Information ratio = (Return - Benchmark return) / Tracking error; measures active management skill vs benchmark; (5) Omega ratio = probability-weighted upside / downside; more comprehensive than Sharpe for non-normal distributions; (6) Pain ratio = Return / Average drawdown depth; (7) MAR ratio = CAGR / Max drawdown. These metrics should be compared over meaningful periods (3-5+ years), benchmarked to peers, and understood in context of strategy.

## SECTION XV: DRAWDOWN ANALYSIS
Drawdown analysis is critical for risk assessment: (1) Maximum drawdown — largest peak-to-trough decline; (2) Drawdown duration — time in drawdown; (3) Recovery time — time to recover to new high; (4) Drawdown frequency — number of significant drawdowns; (5) Calmar — annual return / max drawdown. Tolerable drawdowns vary: long/short equity 10-15%; macro 5-15%; credit 5-20% (with 2008-style events possibly 30%+); market neutral 3-7%. Fund level drawdowns > 10% typically trigger LP concerns; > 15% triggers redemption discussions; > 20% triggers serious redemptions and potential closure. Post-drawdown recovery is harder than drawdown itself — requires sustained outperformance.

## SECTION XVI: TAIL RISK HEDGING
Tail risk hedging strategies aim to protect against crisis scenarios: (1) Long out-of-the-money puts (SPX, index options) — clean hedge but expensive carry; (2) VIX calls / futures — payoff spikes during vol events; (3) Long dated Treasuries (flight-to-quality) — worked 2008, failed 2022; (4) Long gold / precious metals — inflation hedge, sometimes crisis hedge; (5) Long USD — safe haven in EM crises; (6) Dedicated tail funds — Universa (Mark Spitznagel/Nassim Taleb), 36 South, Capstone Tail, Saba Tail Hedge Fund; (7) Structured convexity trades — long vol, long correlation, long credit protection. The challenge: tail hedges cost 1-5% per year in calm markets; must be sized appropriately (5-10% of NAV) and accepted as insurance cost. Universa famously made 4000%+ return in March 2020 COVID crash, though with 10+ years of losses prior.

## SECTION XVII: COUNTERPARTY STRESS TESTS
Counterparty stress testing: (1) Identify top 10-20 counterparties by exposure (swaps, financing, trade settlement, PB balances); (2) Stress scenarios — counterparty credit downgrade, outright default, operational failure; (3) Exposure calculation — gross exposure, netted exposure, collateralized net exposure; (4) Concentration — single counterparty exposure as % of NAV; typical limit 20-30% per counterparty, 5-10% per non-PB counterparty; (5) Contingency planning — what happens if major PB defaults. Post-Lehman, counterparty stress testing became universal at sophisticated HFs. Credit Suisse March 2023 validated the discipline — funds that had reduced CS exposure in preceding months avoided the CS/UBS migration disruption.

## SECTION XVIII: MODEL RISK MANAGEMENT
Model risk — the risk of errors in quantitative models or their misuse — is increasingly prominent: (1) Model validation — independent validation of pricing models, risk models, forecasting models; (2) Model governance — documentation, approval, review cycles; (3) Model monitoring — ongoing performance vs expected; (4) Model limitations — known weaknesses documented and communicated; (5) Model inventory — complete catalogue of all models used in firm. Famous model failures: (1) LTCM 1998 — models assumed normal distribution, correlation stability; (2) CDO pricing 2007-2008 — Gaussian copula models underestimated default correlation; (3) JPM London Whale 2012 — VaR model change masked risk buildup. Quant fund model risk: over-fitting, regime change, data snooping. Modern risk frameworks include "model risk" as a specific category with dedicated oversight.

## SECTION XIX: REGULATORY RISK REPORTING
HFs report extensive risk metrics to regulators: (1) US — Form PF (private fund advisers), reporting VaR, stress test results, portfolio composition, investor composition, financing, counterparty exposures; large fund advisers (Hedge Fund Qualified Purchasers > $500M) file quarterly; (2) EU/UK — AIFMD Annex IV reporting, similar to Form PF, frequency based on leverage level; (3) Central banks — Fed, BoE, ECB periodically survey HF exposures; (4) Market abuse reporting — STOR, SAR. Aggregate regulatory reports inform macroprudential oversight — Form PF data was used to assess 2021 Archegos impact on system, 2022 gilt crisis HF positioning, etc. Reporting is operationally burdensome — large HFs have 5-10+ person regulatory reporting teams.

## SECTION XX: RISK INTERVIEW PREP
Interview questions: (1) "Explain VaR and its limitations." — Definition, methodologies, limitations (tail risk, correlation instability, backward-looking), CVaR as alternative. (2) "Walk through a historical simulation VaR calculation." — Take 500 historical scenarios, apply to current portfolio, compute P&L distribution, take 5th percentile for 95% VaR. (3) "What's the difference between Sharpe and Sortino?" — Sortino uses downside deviation only. (4) "Explain factor attribution." — Decompose returns into Barra factors + idiosyncratic; alpha is idiosyncratic component. (5) "How would you stress test a long/short equity book?" — Historical scenarios (2008, 2020), factor stresses (momentum crash, value rotation), single-name tail risk. (6) "What went wrong at LTCM?" — Models assumed stable correlations, tail risks ignored, leverage ~30x, Russian default triggered correlated losses across convergence trades. (7) "Explain the Archegos risk failures." — Concentration across primes, lack of cross-PB transparency, swap-based leverage evading disclosure. (8) "How do you measure liquidity risk?" — Days-to-liquidate at participation rate, fund-level vs portfolio liquidity matching. (9) "What's a reverse stress test?" — Working backward from a defined loss amount to find scenario causing it. (10) "How does a multi-strategy firm achieve uncorrelated returns?" — Diversification across PMs, strategies, geographies, time horizons; risk limits prevent correlated bets; disciplined capital allocation. Key formulas: Parametric VaR = Z × σ × √t × Portfolio value; Sharpe = (R - Rf) / σ; Information Ratio = (R - Rb) / Tracking Error; Beta-adjusted net = Σ(Long weight × Long beta) - Σ(Short weight × Short beta); Marginal VaR of position i = ∂VaR/∂wᵢ; Component VaR = wᵢ × (∂VaR/∂wᵢ); CVaR_α = E[Loss | Loss > VaR_α].

---

# CHAPTER 44 — OPERATIONS, ODD, TECHNOLOGY, DATA & MIDDLE OFFICE

## SECTION I: THE OPS FUNCTION SCOPE
The operations function at modern HFs encompasses trade processing, reconciliation, corporate actions, cash management, and interaction with external service providers. Scale: small HF ($100M-$1B) might have 2-5 ops staff; mid-sized ($1-5B) 5-15; large ($5B+) 15-50+; multi-strat platforms ($20B+) 100-300+. Core activities: (1) Trade capture — manual or electronic recording of all trades, trade confirmation with counterparties; (2) Settlement — ensuring timely settlement of trades, resolving settlement fails; (3) Reconciliation — daily reconciliation of positions, cash, margin between internal records and PB/custodian records; (4) Corporate actions — dividends, rights offerings, mergers, stock splits, tender offers; (5) Cash management — sweeping cash across accounts, meeting margin calls, managing FX funding; (6) NAV support — providing data to fund administrator for NAV calculation. The failure mode: unreconciled positions, missed corporate actions, failed settlements, mispriced trades.

## SECTION II: ORDER MANAGEMENT AND EXECUTION MANAGEMENT SYSTEMS
OMS (Order Management System) manages trade lifecycle from order creation through execution to settlement. EMS (Execution Management System) provides execution tools — algos, routing, liquidity-seeking. Leading vendors: (1) Enfusion — integrated OMS/PMS/accounting, popular with multi-strategy funds, cloud-based; (2) Eze Software (now SS&C) — long-established multi-asset OMS; (3) Bloomberg AIM — dominant in fixed income and FX, strong integration with Bloomberg terminal; (4) Charles River — classic OMS, strong in long-only; (5) FlexTrade FlexTRADER/FlexONE — multi-asset execution + order management; (6) Portware (now FactSet) — multi-asset execution; (7) Advent Geneva (SS&C) — fund accounting and portfolio management; (8) LiquidityBook — newer cloud-native OMS. Custom-built systems at top-tier shops: Citadel, Renaissance, DE Shaw, Jane Street have in-house tech stacks. Multi-strat platforms often have heterogeneous systems across pods, with centralized reporting layer aggregating data.

## SECTION III: PORTFOLIO MANAGEMENT SYSTEMS
PMS (Portfolio Management System) provides position, P&L, risk reporting to PMs: (1) Real-time P&L — intraday position P&L with live market prices; (2) Position management — current holdings by account, strategy, sector; (3) What-if analysis — pre-trade impact on risk; (4) Performance attribution — factor analysis, security selection; (5) Historical performance — time-series analysis. Market leaders: Enfusion, Imagine, Pacific, IHS Markit (now S&P Global), Bloomberg PORT, FactSet PA. Specialist systems for specific asset classes: Murex (fixed income derivatives), Calypso (cross-asset), Summit (fixed income). Integration is often the challenge — multiple systems across front/middle/back office with reconciliation complexity.

## SECTION IV: DATA INFRASTRUCTURE AND VENDORS
HF data vendors: (1) Bloomberg — dominant terminal ($25k+ per user per year), market data, news, analytics, messaging; (2) Refinitiv (LSE Group post-2021 acquisition) — Eikon terminal, data services; (3) FactSet — research and analytics, popular on buy-side; (4) S&P Global Market Intelligence — Capital IQ (fundamental), IHS Markit data, bond data; (5) ICE Data Services — fixed income and derivatives data; (6) MSCI — indices, ESG data, risk analytics, Barra models; (7) FTSE Russell — indices; (8) Moody's Analytics — credit and economic data. Alternative data explosion: (1) Satellite imagery (Planet Labs, Orbital Insight, RS Metrics) — parking lot counts, shipping, oil storage; (2) Credit card transactions (YipitData, Second Measure, Facteus, Earnest Research); (3) Web scraping (Thinknum, Similarweb, Trilogy) — hiring trends, pricing, web traffic; (4) Geolocation (Advan, Veraset, Placer.ai) — foot traffic; (5) Social media and sentiment (RavenPack, Alexandria, Prattle, Accern); (6) Expert networks (GLG, Third Bridge, Guidepoint, AlphaSights); (7) Corporate filings analytics (Sentieo, AlphaSense). Large HFs spend $50-200M+ annually on data; multi-strat platforms have data governance teams overseeing hundreds of vendors.

## SECTION V: MARKET DATA FEEDS
Real-time market data infrastructure: (1) Exchange direct feeds (CME, NYSE, Nasdaq, LSE, Eurex, ICE, TSE) for low-latency trading; (2) Consolidated feeds — Refinitiv TREP, Bloomberg B-PIPE, ICE Data CTS; (3) Colocation — physical proximity to exchanges for HFT and systematic shops; (4) Tick data — historical tick-level data for backtesting (Thesys, Activ, OneMarketData, Kx KDB+ timeseries database); (5) News feeds — Bloomberg news, Reuters news, Dow Jones Newswires — machine-readable for algos. Cost of market data: $500k-$10M+ annually depending on usage; exchange data fees have been a growing revenue stream for exchanges, creating tension with buy-side consumers. HFs negotiate data contracts carefully — unused feeds are a common cost optimization target.

## SECTION VI: VALUATION AND PRICING
Valuation function determines fair value of all positions for NAV calculation. Hierarchy: (1) Level 1 — active market prices (listed equities, futures, liquid bonds) — straightforward; (2) Level 2 — observable inputs (OTC derivatives with market quotes, broker marks) — requires vendor pricing and validation; (3) Level 3 — unobservable inputs (distressed, private, complex derivatives, side pockets) — requires model-based valuation and significant governance. Valuation policies required: pricing hierarchy, fallback procedures, model documentation, stale price handling, dispute resolution. Independent pricing vendors: IHS Markit (now S&P Global), Bloomberg BVAL, Refinitiv Evaluated Pricing Service, ICE Data, Lukka (crypto). Large funds have dedicated valuation committees reviewing Level 3 valuations monthly, auditors scrutinizing year-end marks heavily. The historical failure mode: mis-marking illiquid positions to inflate NAV (Bayou Fund, KL Group, Wood River — various fraud cases).

## SECTION VII: TRADE SETTLEMENT AND FAILS
Settlement mechanics: (1) US equities — T+1 (post-May 2024, down from T+2); (2) US Treasuries — T+1; (3) European equities — T+2; (4) FX — T+2 (major pairs); (5) OTC derivatives — variable, often T+0 or T+1; (6) Repo — overnight to term. Settlement fails occur when one party fails to deliver or pay by settlement date — triggering financing costs, reputational issues, potentially regulatory penalties. Common fail causes: (a) Short squeeze forcing buy-in, (b) Corporate action processing errors, (c) Trading error, (d) Counterparty issue. Fail rate in US equities historically 2-5% of trades, reduced post-regulatory focus. SEC Rule 204 requires broker-dealers to close out fails. HFs monitor their fail rate as ops quality indicator.

## SECTION VIII: CORPORATE ACTIONS
Corporate actions processing: (1) Cash dividends — straightforward, posted to cash account; (2) Stock dividends — position adjustment; (3) Stock splits — position multiplier; (4) Mergers — cash, stock, mixed consideration, election deadlines; (5) Rights offerings — subscribe, sell, or let expire; (6) Tender offers — accept, reject, partial tender; (7) Spin-offs — new security creation; (8) Consolidations — reverse splits. Corporate actions data vendors: Bloomberg, S&P Global, DTCC. Errors in corporate action processing are a classic ops failure — missed rights expirations, incorrect mergers, bad election decisions. Multi-strat platforms have specialist corporate actions teams processing 100s of events per month.

## SECTION IX: NAV CALCULATION
NAV calculation is typically outsourced to a fund administrator. Process: (1) Trade data — administrator receives daily trade files; (2) Position reconciliation — administrator vs prime broker; (3) Pricing — market prices for Level 1, broker marks for Level 2, model prices for Level 3; (4) Corporate actions — apply to positions; (5) Accruals — management fees, performance fees, expenses, interest; (6) FX conversion — local to base currency; (7) NAV calculation — assets minus liabilities, divided by shares; (8) NAV striking — typically T+3 to T+10 for monthly NAV, T+1 for estimated NAV. Pricing sources are critical — admin independence from PM ensures NAV integrity. NAV errors are disclosed to LPs and can trigger restatements. Side pockets and illiquid positions complicate NAV — often valued by pricing committee, held at cost or conservative marks.

## SECTION X: CASH AND COLLATERAL MANAGEMENT
Cash management function: (1) Daily sweep of excess cash to interest-bearing accounts or money market funds; (2) Margin funding — ensure margin calls are met without shortfalls; (3) FX funding — maintain appropriate currency balances for settlement; (4) Counterparty diversification — no single counterparty holds excessive cash; (5) Yield optimization — money market funds (Fidelity Prime, Morgan Stanley Institutional, BlackRock Liquidity) vs bank deposits vs Treasury bills. Treasury function often handles these tasks plus credit line management. Post-2022 rate environment: cash yield moved from 0% to 5%+, making cash management more significant contributor to returns.

## SECTION XI: COLLATERAL MANAGEMENT
Collateral management function: (1) Daily mark-to-market of OTC derivative positions; (2) Variation margin calculation and calls; (3) Initial margin requirement under UMR; (4) Collateral eligibility — cash, government bonds, high-grade corporates, certain equities; (5) Haircut application — varies by collateral type; (6) Tri-party collateral management — BNY Mellon, JPM, Euroclear, Clearstream; (7) Dispute resolution — when counterparties disagree on mark or margin. Post-2008 reforms have dramatically increased collateral management complexity — initial margin for uncleared swaps, central clearing margining, multi-currency collateral pools. Sophisticated HFs have dedicated collateral teams of 5-20 people.

## SECTION XII: TECHNOLOGY INFRASTRUCTURE
HF technology architecture: (1) Front office — trading systems, PMS, analytics, research; (2) Middle office — risk, P&L, reconciliation; (3) Back office — settlement, NAV, accounting; (4) Data layer — market data, alt data, fundamental data; (5) Infrastructure — servers, storage, networks, cloud; (6) Security — access control, encryption, intrusion detection. Modern HFs increasingly use cloud infrastructure (AWS, Azure, GCP) with appropriate financial services security. Multi-strat platforms typically run massive data centers (on-prem or co-located) for latency-sensitive trading plus cloud for non-latency-critical workloads. Technology spend ranges: small HF $1-5M annually; mid $5-25M; large $25-100M; top tier $100M-$1B+ at firms like Citadel, Renaissance, Two Sigma.

## SECTION XIII: CYBERSECURITY
Cybersecurity is a major operational focus post-2015: (1) Access control — MFA, role-based access, privileged access management; (2) Encryption — data at rest and in transit; (3) Endpoint security — anti-malware, EDR (Crowdstrike, SentinelOne, Microsoft Defender); (4) Network security — firewalls, SIEM (Splunk, Sentinel), intrusion detection; (5) Email security — phishing protection, DKIM, SPF, DMARC; (6) Cloud security — CNAPP tools, CSPM; (7) Third-party risk — vendor security assessments; (8) Incident response — playbooks, practice exercises; (9) Business continuity — backup, disaster recovery; (10) Insurance — cyber insurance coverage. High-profile HF cyber incidents: Melissa Data Theft (2019), various ransomware events at small funds, ongoing phishing / business email compromise attempts. Regulatory focus: SEC cybersecurity risk management rules, FCA operational resilience, EU DORA.

## SECTION XIV: BUSINESS CONTINUITY AND DISASTER RECOVERY
BCP/DR plans address scenarios: (1) Facility unavailability — alternative office, work-from-home; (2) Technology failure — backup systems, failover; (3) Key personnel unavailability — succession plans, cross-training; (4) Service provider failure — backup providers, manual processes; (5) Pandemic — COVID 2020 tested plans comprehensively; (6) Regulatory action — compliance failure handling. Plans must be tested regularly (annually at minimum). COVID March 2020 was the comprehensive test — HFs generally performed well, with seamless transition to remote work within 48 hours for most. FCA operational resilience rules require testing to "severe but plausible" scenarios.

## SECTION XV: OPERATIONAL DUE DILIGENCE (ODD) DEEP DIVE
Operational due diligence (ODD) conducted by LPs focuses on non-investment risks: (1) Fund structure — domicile, legal setup, service providers; (2) Governance — board composition, independent directors, committees; (3) Valuation — policy, process, independence, Level 3 governance; (4) Investor protection — segregation, custody, rehypothecation limits; (5) Compliance — framework, personnel, testing, reporting; (6) Legal — litigation, regulatory actions; (7) Operations — trade processing, reconciliation, errors; (8) Technology — infrastructure, cybersecurity, disaster recovery; (9) Conflicts — side letters, related party, best execution; (10) Fraud detection — segregation of duties, independent checks, background screens. Leading ODD firms: Albourne (large scale), Aksia, Castle Hall Alternatives, PerTrac, independent ODD firms. Madoff cautionary tale: all ODD red flags were present but ignored — no independent custody, third-tier auditor, unusual options strategy, no investor transparency, OTC broker-dealer model. Post-Madoff ODD has strict checklists including independent auditor verification, custody independence, valuation independence.

## SECTION XVI: FRAUD PREVENTION
Fraud prevention mechanisms: (1) Segregation of duties — no single person controls full trade lifecycle; (2) Independent valuation — prices sourced independently from trading; (3) Custody independence — assets held at third-party custodian; (4) Fund administrator independence — NAV calculated by third party; (5) Audit independence — Big 4 or reputable audit firm; (6) Multi-signer approvals — cash movements require multiple approvals; (7) Personal trading restrictions — employees cannot trade positions without pre-approval; (8) Background checks — all senior hires checked; (9) Whistleblower protections. Post-Madoff (2008), frauds have declined but not eliminated — smaller frauds continue, typically at small funds with weak controls (Aminex Capital, various other smaller cases).

## SECTION XVII: KEY ODD REJECTION REASONS
LPs reject HFs on ODD grounds for: (1) Inadequate independent custody — assets held at PB or affiliated custodian; (2) Weak valuation — Level 3 without independent governance; (3) Related party transactions — trading with affiliates, soft dollar arrangements; (4) Inadequate segregation of duties — single person in control; (5) Key person risk — concentration in one individual; (6) Compliance gaps — inadequate framework, insufficient staffing; (7) Service provider concerns — second-tier or affiliated providers; (8) Background check issues — regulatory history, litigation; (9) Prior employer issues — unexplained departures, non-competes; (10) Inconsistent or missing documentation. ODD rejection is the investment killer — IDD may favor a manager strongly but ODD has veto power.

## SECTION XVIII: THIRD-PARTY VENDOR GOVERNANCE
Vendor governance post-2020 increased focus: (1) Vendor inventory — all third-party providers catalogued; (2) Tiering — critical, high, medium, low; (3) Due diligence — financial, operational, security, regulatory; (4) Contracts — SLAs, termination rights, audit rights; (5) Ongoing monitoring — performance, incidents, financial health; (6) Concentration — avoid single-vendor dependency; (7) Exit strategy — ability to transition if vendor fails. FCA outsourcing rules, EU DORA, SEC oversight all increased governance expectations. Vendor failures (Silicon Valley Bank March 2023, FTX November 2022, various administrator issues) highlight vendor concentration risks.

## SECTION XIX: TECH TALENT AND COMPENSATION
HF technology talent competes with FAANG and fintech for top engineers. Compensation: entry-level software engineer $150-250k; senior $300-700k; staff/principal $500-1.5M; head of engineering $1-5M+. Specialized quant developers and low-latency engineers at top HFs earn $500k-$5M+. Citadel, Two Sigma, Jane Street, Renaissance, DE Shaw, Jump are aggressive tech hirers. Engineering-to-PM ratio at systematic shops can be 2:1 or higher. Data science / machine learning engineers are particularly in demand. Office locations: major HFs have significant tech presence in both financial centers (NYC, London, Chicago) and tech hubs (Austin, SF, Dublin, Bangalore, Shanghai).

## SECTION XX: OPS INTERVIEW PREP
Interview questions: (1) "Walk through the trade lifecycle." — Order creation, execution, allocation, confirmation, settlement, reconciliation, reporting. (2) "Explain Level 1, 2, 3 fair value hierarchy." — Observable active market, observable inputs, unobservable inputs. (3) "What is the role of a fund administrator?" — Independent NAV, investor services, regulatory reporting, financial statements. (4) "How does T+1 settlement affect ops?" — Compressed reconciliation window, greater automation, FX funding complexity for cross-border trades. (5) "Explain initial margin for uncleared swaps under UMR." — Regulatory requirement for swap counterparties exceeding threshold; SIMM calculation methodology; segregated collateral. (6) "What are the key ODD red flags?" — Custody issues, valuation independence, segregation of duties, auditor quality, compliance gaps. (7) "Walk through the Madoff fraud." — 17-year Ponzi; lack of independent custody, third-tier auditor, mysterious strategy; post-Madoff ODD rigor. (8) "How would you design a cybersecurity framework for an HF?" — Layered defense, MFA, encryption, monitoring, incident response, testing, vendor management. (9) "Explain valuation governance for Level 3 assets." — Pricing committee, independent sources, model documentation, auditor scrutiny. (10) "What is DORA and how does it affect HFs?" — EU Digital Operational Resilience Act, ICT risk, third-party oversight, incident reporting, applies Jan 2025.

---

# CHAPTER 45 — FAMOUS BLOWUPS: THE COMPLETE CATALOGUE

## SECTION I: WHY BLOWUPS MATTER
Hedge fund blowups are the industry's most valuable educational material.
