Each major blowup reveals a failure mode — leverage, concentration, liquidity mismatch, fraud, model error, counterparty cascade — that informs subsequent risk management practice. The bibliography of blowups is how professional HF risk managers, PMs, and allocators calibrate their intuitions. Common themes across blowups: (1) Leverage beyond stress tolerance; (2) Concentration in correlated bets; (3) Liquidity mismatch between portfolio and liabilities; (4) Model risk from relying on historical correlations; (5) Governance / control weakness; (6) Fraud when controls absent; (7) Sudden counterparty withdrawal. Studying blowups chronologically reveals how the industry learned — and forgot — lessons.

## SECTION II: LONG-TERM CAPITAL MANAGEMENT (1998)
LTCM (founded 1994 by John Meriwether ex-Salomon, partners included Myron Scholes and Robert Merton — Nobel laureates 1997 for Black-Scholes model) was the most iconic HF blowup. Strategy: fixed income relative value arbitrage — convergence trades on swap spreads, on-the-run vs off-the-run Treasuries, sovereign spread convergence, merger arb, volatility arb, equity index arb. Peak leverage: ~30x on $4.7B equity ($125B+ balance sheet positions) plus hundreds of billions in off-balance-sheet derivatives. Returns: +21%, +43%, +41%, +17% in first four years (net of fees), attracting $7B at peak. The trigger: August 17, 1998 Russian default. Flight to quality caused convergence trades to diverge sharply across all strategies simultaneously — spreads widened rather than narrowed. LTCM lost $4.4B in weeks, equity fell to $600M. Fed-organized bailout September 23, 1998 — 14 Wall Street banks contributed $3.6B to orderly unwind under William McDonough (NY Fed) leadership. Bear Stearns refused to participate. LTCM was wound down by 2000. Lessons: (1) Historical correlations unreliable in crisis; (2) Leverage compounds liquidity risk; (3) Even Nobel laureates can be wrong; (4) Counterparty awareness of your positions becomes a weapon. Read Roger Lowenstein's "When Genius Failed" for definitive account.

## SECTION III: TIGER MANAGEMENT (2000)
Julian Robertson's Tiger Management ran $22B at peak (1998), the largest HF of its era. Strategy: long/short equity with deep fundamental research, famous "Tiger Cubs" alumni network (Andreas Halvorsen/Viking, Lee Ainslie/Maverick, Chase Coleman/Tiger Global, Stephen Mandel/Lone Pine, Rob Citrone/Discovery Capital, Bill Hwang/Archegos — infamous). Blowup cause: Tiger's value-oriented strategy missed the late-1990s dotcom bubble — Robertson refused to buy tech stocks at stratospheric valuations. Performance: 1999 down ~15% while Nasdaq doubled. Massive redemptions. Robertson closed the fund March 2000 — immediately before the dotcom bubble burst (which would have vindicated him). Tiger's closure was "being right at the wrong time." Lesson: (a) Capital can be withdrawn before vindication; (b) Liquidity mismatch between long-term thesis and LP patience.

## SECTION IV: BAYOU FUND (2005)
Bayou Fund (founded 1996 by Samuel Israel III and James Marquez) was a Ponzi scheme masquerading as a HF. Reported returns: 100%+ cumulative over 8 years. Reality: total fraud with fabricated NAVs, fictitious auditor ("Richmond-Fairfield Associates" — the same address as Bayou's office, owned by Marquez). Collapse: 2005 when investors demanded redemptions. Israel fled, was arrested 2008 faking his own suicide (abandoned car on Bear Mountain Bridge NY). Convicted, served 22 years. Lesson: independent audit is critical. Pre-Madoff, Bayou was among the largest fraud cases ($450M missing).

## SECTION V: AMARANTH ADVISORS (2006)
Amaranth Advisors (founded 2000 by Nicholas Maounis, ~$9B AUM) was a multi-strategy HF that became dominated by natural gas trader Brian Hunter. Hunter made ~$1B in 2005 by going long natural gas ahead of Hurricane Katrina. Emboldened, he built massive concentrated positions in natural gas spreads (winter-summer calendar spreads, March 2007 vs April 2007) peaking at $30-40B notional. September 2006: natural gas prices and spreads moved against him, $6.6B loss in one week (~65% of fund). Amaranth was forced to sell positions to Citadel and JPM at fire-sale prices. Fund wound down. Lessons: (1) Concentration in a single trader / single strategy within a multi-strat is dangerous; (2) Liquidity in natural gas contracts was insufficient for position size; (3) CFTC and subsequent regulatory investigation of manipulation. Read "Hedge Hogs" by Barbara Dreyfuss.

## SECTION VI: PELOTON PARTNERS (2008)
Peloton Partners (founded 2005 by Ron Beller and Geoff Grant, former Goldman Sachs partners, based in London) ran ABS strategy fund that made 87% in 2007 (top-performing HF of the year) on mortgage shorts. Problem: in 2008, Peloton was long AAA subprime RMBS (betting on recovery) and short lower-rated tranches. When even AAA tranches fell, the long leg collapsed while shorts were gating. Peloton was wound down February 2008, losing $2B. Lessons: (1) Capital structure trades in distressed assets are more dangerous than outright shorts; (2) Credit correlation in crisis rises across tranches; (3) Prior year's star performer can blow up next.

## SECTION VII: SAC CAPITAL / STEVEN COHEN (2013)
SAC Capital (founded 1992 by Steven A. Cohen, ~$15B AUM at peak) was one of the most successful HFs of all time — averaging 25%+ net returns over two decades. Investigation: multi-year SEC/DOJ investigation into insider trading at SAC, with multiple traders pleading guilty (Mathew Martoma, Michael Steinberg, Tim Jandovitz, Donald Longueuil, Noah Freeman). 2013: SAC pleaded guilty to insider trading, paid $1.8B record settlement, converted to family office (Point72 Asset Management) that returned outside capital. Cohen personally never charged criminally. Point72 reopened to outside capital 2018 (5-year civil ban expired). Martoma received longest insider trading sentence ever (9 years) for Elan/Wyeth trades. Lessons: (a) Aggressive research culture can cross legal lines; (b) Information edge requires compliance discipline; (c) Firm-level liability from individual traders.

## SECTION VIII: GALLEON GROUP (2009)
Galleon Group (founded 1997 by Raj Rajaratnam, ~$7B AUM) was a long/short equity HF that collapsed October 2009 following the largest insider trading case in US history. Rajaratnam built an extensive insider information network — corporate executives, consultants (Rajat Gupta ex-McKinsey/Goldman board), hedge fund traders — that was discovered through FBI wiretaps. Rajaratnam convicted 2011, sentenced 11 years, released 2019. Dozens of others convicted. Lesson: the enforcement response was unprecedented — wiretaps, insider cooperation, coordinated prosecutions. Insider trading investigations at HFs became a permanent enforcement priority.

## SECTION IX: MF GLOBAL (2011)
MF Global (futures commission merchant, not technically HF but functionally similar in blowup character) was run by Jon Corzine (ex-Goldman CEO, ex-NJ Governor). Corzine built large European sovereign debt proprietary positions (Italy, Spain, Portugal, Ireland) via repo-to-maturity trades. Rising European debt concerns in October 2011 caused margin calls. MF Global could not meet calls. $1.6B of customer funds were discovered missing from segregated accounts (used to cover prop losses). MF Global bankrupted October 31, 2011. Criminal investigation of Corzine did not result in charges. Lesson: segregation of customer funds is not just regulatory requirement — it's a control that must be monitored in real time.

## SECTION X: BRIDGEWATER PURE ALPHA / DALIO ISSUES
Bridgewater Associates (Ray Dalio, world's largest HF at ~$160B peak, now ~$100B) is not a blowup but deserves mention for sustained underperformance in recent years. Pure Alpha flagship was -12% in 2020 (vs positive HFR index), modest 2021-2023, creating significant investor exit. All Weather (risk parity) also suffered from 2022's simultaneous stock/bond decline — the strategy's foundational assumption of negative correlation broke down. Dalio transitioned leadership from 2017, finally exited 2022. Co-CEO transitions were controversial and publicized. Lesson: (1) Risk parity's historical backtest relies on post-1980 correlations that may not persist; (2) Founder succession is hard.

## SECTION XI: PAULSON & CO. — RISE AND FALL
John Paulson's Paulson & Co. made the "greatest trade ever" in 2007-2008 — shorting subprime MBS via CDS for a $15B personal P&L, fund made $20B+. The GS ABACUS case (2010) implicated Paulson's role in selecting collateral for a CDO that Goldman sold to IKB and ACA with Paulson short. SEC sued Goldman (not Paulson), Goldman settled for $550M. Post-2008, Paulson's funds struggled — concentrated bets on gold (2011-2012), financials, pharmaceutical (Valeant 2015-2016). AUM fell from $38B peak to under $10B by 2019. Paulson converted to family office 2020, returning outside capital. Lessons: (a) Singular great trade is hard to repeat; (b) Concentration after success is particularly dangerous; (c) Sophistication of LPs means past glory fades quickly.

## SECTION XII: VISIUM CAPITAL (2016)
Visium Asset Management ($8B AUM) was a multi-strat with strong healthcare focus. 2016: SEC/DOJ insider trading and mismarking case. PM Sanjay Valvani committed suicide days after charges. Co-founder Jacob Gottlieb forced to wind down. PM Stefan Lumiere convicted of mismarking bond positions. Visium returned capital to investors. Lesson: mismarking of illiquid positions is a classic control failure; even in a multi-strat with sophisticated infrastructure, control gaps can exist.

## SECTION XIII: BLUECREST (2014 conversion, 2020 SEC settlement)
BlueCrest Capital (founded 2000 by Michael Platt ex-JPM, peak $37B AUM) was London's largest macro HF. 2014: Platt announced conversion to family office, returned $7B of outside capital, citing fee pressure and macro environment. 2020: SEC settled $170M with BlueCrest for undisclosed allocation of best traders to internal fund (BSMA) vs external client fund (BCI). Lessons: (1) Allocation fairness between internal and external capital is a compliance focus; (2) Family office conversion enables greater flexibility but requires returning LP capital.

## SECTION XIV: ARCHEGOS CAPITAL (2021)
Archegos Capital (Bill Hwang family office, ex-Tiger Asia which pleaded guilty 2012 insider trading) built $50B+ notional concentrated long positions using swap-based leverage across ~8 prime brokers (Credit Suisse, Nomura, Morgan Stanley, Goldman, UBS, MUFG, Wells Fargo, Mizuho). March 2021: ViacomCBS secondary offering triggered stock decline, margin calls across all PBs, forced unwind. Credit Suisse lost $5.5B (fatally wounded), Nomura $2.9B, Morgan Stanley ~$900M. Hwang convicted July 2024 on fraud/racketeering, sentenced November 2024 to 18 years. Lessons: (1) Cross-PB concentration invisible to individual PBs; (2) Swap-based leverage avoids disclosure (partially addressed by SEC rule changes 2023); (3) Family office lighter regulation enabled excess risk; (4) PBs must conduct peer surveys on swap counterparty exposure.

## SECTION XV: 2022 UK GILT / LDI CRISIS (NOT A HF, BUT CRITICAL)
September 2022: UK Chancellor Kwasi Kwarteng's "mini-budget" triggered a gilt yield spike (30-year gilt yields rose 100bp+ in days). UK pension funds using liability-driven investing (LDI) strategies with leveraged gilt positions faced margin calls they could not meet. BoE emergency intervention with £65B gilt-buying facility prevented systemic collapse. HFs with short gilt positions made significant profits (Brevan Howard, Rokos, Crispin Odey flagship ~+145%). Odey's personal vindication was short-lived (see next). The crisis demonstrated: (1) Leverage in LDI strategies created systemic risk; (2) Even developed market sovereigns can experience liquidity crises; (3) HFs can profit from institutional distress.

## SECTION XVI: ODEY ASSET MANAGEMENT COLLAPSE (2023)
Crispin Odey's Odey Asset Management (founded 1991, ran ~$13B at peak, down to ~$4B by 2023) was a storied London HF. June 2023: Financial Times and Tortoise Media published detailed investigation alleging 20+ years of sexual misconduct by Crispin Odey. Within weeks: LPs announced redemptions ($850M+), prime brokers suspended relationships (Morgan Stanley, JPM, Goldman), Odey removed as partner, firm wound down. Funds transferred to other managers (Lancaster, SW Mitchell). Odey's ODEY EUR and ODEY EUR LP funds were closed. Lessons: (1) Reputational risk can collapse a firm overnight; (2) Individual misconduct risks firm-level failure; (3) Counterparty actions by PBs can force wind-down regardless of trading performance; (4) Key person risk is existential for founder-led firms.

## SECTION XVII: GAMESTOP SHORT SQUEEZE (January 2021)
Not a single blowup but a systemic event. January 2021: retail traders coordinating on Reddit's WallStreetBets forum coordinated GameStop buying, triggering massive short squeeze. GME went from $17 to $483 in weeks. Melvin Capital (Gabe Plotkin, ex-SAC) was short and lost $6.8B (-53% in January); required $2.75B rescue capital from Citadel and Point72. Melvin closed May 2022 after continued poor performance. Light Street Capital, Maplelane, Candlestick Capital also suffered. Citadel's early exposure via Melvin rescue was scrutinized. Lessons: (1) Short squeezes can overwhelm traditional risk management; (2) Retail coordination is a new market force; (3) Public short positions are vulnerable to social media campaigns.

## SECTION XVIII: SILICON VALLEY BANK MARCH 2023 (SYSTEMIC)
Silicon Valley Bank collapsed March 10, 2023 — largest US bank failure since 2008. Cause: duration mismatch (long-duration Treasury/MBS positions against short-duration deposits) combined with deposit concentration (VC ecosystem) and rapid rate rises. $175B deposits fled in 48 hours. Fed and Treasury invoked systemic risk exception to fully protect deposits. Signature Bank failed days later. Credit Suisse failed weeks later (different causes but related crisis of confidence). HF impacts: (1) Direct losses for HFs with uninsured deposits at SVB; (2) Stablecoin USDC briefly de-pegged (Circle had $3.3B at SVB); (3) Opportunities for distressed investors in First Republic, Signature, Pacific West receiverships. Lessons: (1) Treasury portfolios are not risk-free if held at institutions with funding mismatch; (2) Deposit concentration creates bank run risk even for solvent banks; (3) Counterparty diversification matters for cash management.

## SECTION XIX: MELVIN CAPITAL — THE FULL STORY
Melvin Capital (founded 2014 by Gabe Plotkin ex-SAC) peaked at $12.5B AUM with strong returns (44% average 2014-2019). January 2021 GameStop squeeze: 53% monthly loss. $2.75B emergency capital from Citadel/Point72. Multiple 2021 drawdowns. 2022 continued losses. May 2022: Plotkin announced closure, returned remaining capital. Previously proposed restructuring (waiving performance fees until recovery) was rejected by LPs. Lessons: (1) Short squeezes can trigger redemption cycles even if subsequent positioning is fine; (2) Fee restructuring attempts during drawdowns are rarely accepted; (3) Ex-SAC lineage provides strong training but doesn't guarantee longevity.

## SECTION XX: BLOWUP LESSONS SYNTHESIS
Cross-cutting lessons from all blowups: (1) Leverage kills — any time leverage meets liquidity mismatch plus adverse move, blowup risk is material; (2) Concentration compounds risk — concentration in single names, single strategies, single geographies amplifies loss potential; (3) Correlations matter in crisis — diversification fails when you need it most; (4) Fraud is a control failure — segregation, independence, transparency are non-negotiable; (5) Reputational risk is existential — allegations can collapse firms regardless of financial health; (6) Counterparty cascades are real — when confidence fails, forced liquidation follows; (7) Models fail in regime change — historical backtests miss structural breaks; (8) Key person dependency — founder-led firms face succession risk; (9) LP patience is limited — even correct views may not survive short-term drawdowns; (10) Learning fades — each generation repeats similar mistakes with new wrappers. The master principle: survive first, then profit — geometric returns require never hitting zero.

---

# CHAPTER 46 — LAUNCHING & SCALING A LONDON HEDGE FUND

## SECTION I: PRE-LAUNCH STRATEGIC DECISIONS
Launching a London HF begins 18-36 months before day one trading. Key pre-launch decisions: (1) Strategy definition — which strategy will the fund run, what is the edge, what is the capacity; (2) Team assembly — who are the investment team, operations, compliance, ops; (3) Seed vs self-funded — will the fund accept seed capital in exchange for equity, or launch with founder capital; (4) Structure — Cayman master + US/offshore feeder is standard, but UK LP options exist; (5) Domicile of management company — UK is obvious for London operations; (6) Regulatory route — self-authorize as FCA AIFM vs appoint a regulatory host (AIFM-as-a-service provider) during early period; (7) Office location — Mayfair prestige (St James's, Berkeley Square, Curzon Street) vs Canary Wharf efficiency vs City of London practicality; (8) Target AUM at launch — $100M minimum typically, $300-500M preferred for credibility. The decision tree shapes everything that follows — from legal structuring to LP targeting to cost base.

## SECTION II: THE FOUNDER PROFILE
Successful London HF launches typically feature founders with: (1) Proven track record at a reputable prior firm (Goldman, Morgan Stanley, JPM, Brevan, Millennium, Citadel, Marshall Wace, TCI, Tiger Cubs, etc.) — ideally 5-10+ years of attributable performance; (2) Deep network of LPs from prior firm or personal relationships; (3) Sufficient personal wealth to fund 6-24 months of operating expenses during pre-launch and early period ($2-10M personal commitment typical); (4) Strong reputation — no regulatory issues, no controversial departures, clean SMCR record; (5) Clear articulation of edge — why will this fund outperform; (6) Ability to attract partner-level talent — co-PMs, senior operations, CFO/COO, compliance; (7) Age 35-50 typical — young enough for energy and runway, experienced enough for track record. Key consideration: gardening leave periods (typical 6-12 months for non-compete) bridge the gap between leaving old firm and launching new one.

## SECTION III: SEED CAPITAL NEGOTIATION
Seed capital economics as noted earlier: typical $100-300M initial investment + 15-25% of gross revenue of management company for 5-10 years + reduced fees on seed money. Seed sources: Blackstone Strategic Alliance, Blue Owl/Dyal, Goldman Petershill, Wafra, Stable Asset, Borealis, Stonehaven, family offices with seed strategies (Investcorp, Tages Capital). Negotiation: (1) Seed size — more capital means more equity given up; (2) Revenue share percentage and duration; (3) Redemption rights — seeder typically has after lock-up; (4) Preferred return / waterfall — standard or customized; (5) Governance rights — board seat, observer rights, information rights; (6) MFN protections — seeder wants best terms; (7) Exclusivity — seeder may want sole seed status; (8) ROFR (right of first refusal) on future launches or capacity; (9) Exit mechanics — buy-out options, tag-along / drag-along. First-time managers have limited leverage; experienced managers can negotiate more favorable terms.

## SECTION IV: LEGAL SETUP COSTS AND TIMELINE
Legal setup for a new HF: (1) UK management company formation — typically Limited Company or LLP under English law, registered at Companies House; (2) Cayman master fund formation — Exempted Limited Partnership registered with CIMA, director appointments, MLRO registration; (3) Feeder fund formation — US Delaware LP + Cayman offshore feeder corp; (4) Offering documents — PPM (private placement memorandum) typically 80-150 pages, subscription documents, side letter templates, LPA (limited partnership agreement); (5) Service provider engagements — administrator, auditor, prime brokers, depositary (for EU AIFs), custodian, legal counsel; (6) FCA authorization application — substantial document set. Legal counsel: leading fund formation lawyers include Schulte Roth & Zabel, Simpson Thacher, Kirkland & Ellis, Dechert, Sidley Austin (US firms with London offices); Clifford Chance, Linklaters, Freshfields (Magic Circle); Macfarlanes, Travers Smith, Herbert Smith Freehills (UK firms). Cayman counsel: Maples, Walkers, Ogier, Conyers. Total legal costs for launch: $500k-$2M depending on complexity.

## SECTION V: FCA AUTHORIZATION JOURNEY
FCA authorization for a new HF manager: (1) Pre-application discussion — meet FCA authorization team, discuss business model, receive feedback on likely issues; (2) Application submission via FCA Connect — covers firm details, senior manager approvals (SMFs), key individuals, business plan, compliance arrangements, financial resources, systems & controls; (3) Case officer assigned — reviews application, requests clarifications; (4) Individual approvals — Senior Managers (SMF1 CEO, SMF3 Executive Director, SMF16 Compliance Oversight, SMF17 MLRO) each require separate approval; (5) Decision — typically 6-12 months for experienced founders, longer for first-timers. Typical challenges: source of capital verification, compliance framework adequacy, financial resources demonstration, systems documentation. Pre-approval planning: engage regulatory consultants (Bovill, Cordium/ACA Compliance, Duff & Phelps, FairXchange) to manage process. Authorization is the gating item — no trading can begin without it.

## SECTION VI: COMPLIANCE INFRASTRUCTURE AT LAUNCH
New fund compliance requirements: (1) Compliance manual — documented policies covering COBS, SYSC, MAR, AML, SMCR, outsourcing, best execution, order handling, research unbundling (historical), personal account dealing; (2) Financial crime controls — AML policies, KYC procedures, sanctions screening, SAR reporting framework; (3) Compliance monitoring programme — scheduled testing of controls; (4) Compliance officer (SMF16) appointment — typically outsourced for small firms or hired in-house; (5) MLRO (SMF17) appointment — can be same person as compliance officer; (6) Market abuse surveillance — STOR procedures, trade monitoring; (7) Conflicts of interest register; (8) Gifts and entertainment register; (9) Personal account dealing approval process; (10) Training programme. Many small HFs outsource compliance to firms like ACA Compliance Group, Bovill (now acquired by ICE), Waystone, IQ-EQ, Duff & Phelps (now Kroll). Typical outsourced compliance cost: £50-150k annually. Building in-house compliance starts at ~£250k annually (senior compliance officer salary + systems).

## SECTION VII: OPERATIONAL READINESS
Operational build-out pre-launch: (1) Order management system selection and implementation — Enfusion typical for new launches (cloud-based, fast setup, integrated OMS/PMS/accounting); (2) Prime broker selection — typically 2-3 PBs at launch; (3) Administrator selection — SS&C, Citco, Apex, IQ-EQ, MUFG, Alter Domus commonly used; (4) Auditor selection — Big 4 preferred; (5) Tax adviser — PwC, EY, KPMG, Deloitte, specialist HF tax (Deloitte particularly strong in HF taxation); (6) Cash management accounts; (7) Data and research — Bloomberg (~$25k per terminal), Refinitiv, FactSet, Capital IQ, plus research access arrangements; (8) Risk systems — Enfusion built-in or specialist (Imagine, Axioma); (9) Email, collaboration, document management (Microsoft 365 standard), financial messaging (SWIFT or Paxos for settlement); (10) Cybersecurity setup — MFA, encryption, endpoint protection, backup. Launch ops team: typically 2-4 people for small launch, 5-10 for larger.

## SECTION VIII: COST BASE AT LAUNCH
Typical Year 1 operating costs for a $300M launch:
- Personnel (8-15 staff): £3-6M (PMs, analysts, ops, compliance, CFO)
- Rent (Mayfair 2,500 sq ft): £250-500k
- Market data + research: £300-700k
- Technology + OMS: £200-500k
- Legal, admin, audit, tax: £400-800k
- Compliance (outsourced or in-house): £100-300k
- Insurance (D&O, E&O, cyber): £150-400k
- Prime broker fees + financing: variable
- Travel and marketing: £100-300k
- Other (utilities, supplies, subscriptions): £100-200k
Total: £4.6-9.7M annually. On a $300M fund at 1.5% management fee = $4.5M (£3.5M), revenue falls short of operating costs, requiring founder subsidy or performance fees. Break-even typically requires $400M+ AUM at standard fees, or performance fees in first year. Multi-strat platforms' massive cost bases are absorbed by scale ($20B+ AUM justifies £100M+ annual costs).

## SECTION IX: RECRUITMENT AT LAUNCH
Initial team composition: (1) Founding PM(s) — often 1-3 partners; (2) Senior analysts — 2-5 (sector specialists or generalists); (3) Junior analysts/associates — 2-5; (4) CFO/COO — critical hire, often sourced from larger firms' ops teams; (5) Compliance officer — in-house or outsourced; (6) Operations — 2-3 for trade processing, reconciliation, settlements; (7) IR/Business development — 1-2; (8) Admin/EA — 1-2. Recruiting from prior firm: founder typically brings key team members after gardening leave (with legal care around non-solicitation clauses). Search firms: Sheffield Haworth, Glocap, PER, Heidrick & Struggles, Options Group, Odgers Berndtson, Egon Zehnder active in HF recruitment. Compensation for new launches: base salaries competitive with large firms; bonus potential via partnership equity; equity stakes typically offered to early team members as retention.

## SECTION X: LAUNCH CAPITAL STRATEGY
Capital raising pre-launch and at launch: (1) Founder capital — personal commitment (5-20% of initial AUM typical); (2) Day 1 LPs — targeted 5-15 anchor LPs providing $100-300M initially; (3) Seed capital — if applicable, single seed investor providing $100-300M in exchange for equity; (4) Friends-and-family — HNW individuals, family offices, previous colleagues; (5) Fund of funds — some emerging manager FoFs allocate to launches (NewAlpha, FRM Man, PAAMCO Prisma, Aurora); (6) Emerging manager programmes — CPPIB, UTIMCO, Teacher Retirement of Texas, NJDI, various pension programmes. Typical split: 20-30% founder/inside, 40-60% institutional anchors, 10-30% seed if applicable. Pre-marketing (subject to NPPR and pre-marketing rules) — soft-circling LP commitments 6-12 months before launch. Soft commitments vs binding commitments — pre-launch pipeline inflates.

## SECTION XI: MARKETING MATERIALS PRE-LAUNCH
Pre-launch marketing materials: (1) Teaser document — 2-5 pages, high-level strategy and team; (2) Pitch book — 40-80 pages once authorization secured; (3) Track record documentation — attributable track record from prior firm, GIPS-compliant if possible, backed by auditor verification or formal performance letter from former employer; (4) Presentation deck — used for in-person meetings; (5) DDQ responses — standard AIMA DDQ or customized for specific LPs; (6) Offering documents — PPM, subscription, side letters. Pre-launch pitching regulated under MiFID II / UK equivalent — "pre-marketing" is limited, full marketing only allowed after AIF is authorised for marketing in target jurisdiction. Reverse solicitation is an exception but narrow.

## SECTION XII: PRIME BROKER SELECTION AT LAUNCH
PB selection for launches: (1) Top tier (Goldman Sachs, Morgan Stanley) — strong service, wide borrow, best rates for larger clients, but minimum AUM thresholds ($250M+, sometimes $500M+); (2) Mid tier (JPM, Barclays, BofA, UBS, Citi, BNP, Deutsche) — competitive across sizes, good for emerging managers; (3) Specialty / emerging manager (Jefferies, Wells Fargo, Pershing, Interactive Brokers) — focus on smaller HFs, higher minimums have declined; (4) European PBs (Societe Generale, HSBC, NatWest) — regional focus. New launches typically start with 1-2 PBs, add more as AUM grows. Key considerations: (a) Minimum balance requirements, (b) Financing rates, (c) Borrow inventory and cost, (d) Capital introduction services, (e) Technology integration, (f) Service coverage. Letter of intent (LOI) with PBs secured before launch to demonstrate infrastructure to LPs.

## SECTION XIII: THE GARDENING LEAVE PERIOD
Founders typically serve gardening leave (non-compete + non-solicit period) after leaving prior firm — 3-12 months typical, up to 24 months at top firms. Gardening leave terms vary: (1) Paid vs unpaid — larger firms pay full compensation during leave; (2) Scope — complete non-compete vs specific strategy exclusion; (3) Non-solicit — cannot recruit from former firm; (4) Non-deal — cannot trade prior firm's strategies; (5) IP protections — cannot use proprietary systems or data. Use of gardening leave: (a) Legal structuring and authorization planning; (b) Strategy refinement and backtest preparation; (c) Pre-marketing LP conversations (subject to restrictions); (d) Team recruitment (subject to non-solicit); (e) Office and infrastructure setup; (f) Personal reset and preparation. Some founders use gardening leave for sabbatical or travel before intense launch period.

## SECTION XIV: DAY 1 TRADING
Day 1 trading considerations: (1) Small initial AUM — limited position sizing, focus on highest conviction ideas; (2) Systems testing — first real trades stress ops, settlement, reconciliation; (3) Risk controls — limits set conservatively at launch; (4) Documentation — every trade carefully documented; (5) LP transparency — daily/weekly updates to anchor LPs during initial period; (6) Performance pressure — early losses can damage credibility; (7) Costs exceed revenue — founder subsidy required. Many funds run paper portfolios alongside live for first few months to validate systems and processes. Trade volume ramps gradually over first 3-6 months as confidence builds.

## SECTION XV: EARLY PERFORMANCE DYNAMICS
Year 1-2 performance dynamics are critical: (1) Strong returns (10%+ net) build momentum for capital raising; (2) Flat returns (0-5%) are survivable but slow scaling; (3) Negative returns (5%+) create redemption risk and can trigger closure; (4) Volatility matters — even positive returns with high volatility concern LPs; (5) Attribution — LPs scrutinize whether returns match expected process. Common Year 1-2 outcomes: (a) Fund doubles AUM organically on strong returns + new LPs; (b) Fund grows 20-50% — steady but not spectacular; (c) Fund stagnates around launch AUM — survival mode; (d) Fund closes within 18 months — failure rate ~30% of launches per historical industry statistics. Key survival factor: founder commitment and personal capital absorbing early losses.

## SECTION XVI: SCALING FROM $500M TO $2B
Scaling a successful HF from $500M to $2B requires: (1) Team expansion — 5-10 additional investment professionals, adding strategy depth or coverage; (2) Ops expansion — from 3-5 to 10-15 ops staff; (3) Technology upgrades — moving from cloud Enfusion to more sophisticated infrastructure; (4) LP diversification — from 10-20 initial LPs to 40-80 diversified LPs; (5) Consultant approvals — Albourne, Aksia approvals unlock pension and endowment allocations; (6) International expansion — possible offices in NY, HK, Zurich, Dublin for LP coverage; (7) Product expansion — possible additional funds (long only, sector-specific, regional focus); (8) Institutional discipline — board formation, risk committee, audit committee. Capacity management becomes critical — strategy scalability determines terminal AUM.

## SECTION XVII: SCALING FROM $2B TO $10B+
Scaling to $10B+ requires: (1) Multi-strategy or multi-PM evolution — single-PM shop typically caps at $5-10B; (2) Talent acquisition — hiring senior PMs from competitors ($10-50M compensation packages); (3) Infrastructure industrialization — enterprise-grade technology, dedicated data teams, quantitative research; (4) Risk function elevation — independent CRO, risk committees, enterprise risk framework; (5) Compliance scaling — 10-30 compliance staff; (6) Regulatory sophistication — multi-jurisdictional management (London + US + EU + Asia); (7) Founder role transformation — from investor to executive, potential CEO/CIO split. Historical transitions: Marshall Wace, TCI, Egerton, Man Group evolution, Brevan Howard growth 2003-2015, more recently Rokos, Kirkoswald.

## SECTION XVIII: LAUNCH FAILURE MODES
Why launches fail: (1) Insufficient capital — unable to raise enough day-1 capital, burning through cash reserves; (2) Early underperformance — drawdowns before track record established; (3) Key person departures — co-founder or senior analyst leaves; (4) Regulatory delays — FCA authorization takes longer than expected; (5) Strategy mismatch — market regime doesn't suit strategy; (6) Cost overruns — operating costs exceed projections; (7) LP concentration risk — single large LP redemption kills fund; (8) Personal conflict — founder partner disputes; (9) External events — market crash, pandemic, regulatory changes. Historical launch failure examples: many first-year launches in 2008-2009 failed due to capital raising difficulty; 2020 COVID launches had mixed fortunes; various individual launch failures rarely get public attention as founders quietly wind down and move on.

## SECTION XIX: EMERGING MANAGER PROGRAMS
Emerging manager programs allocate capital to new/small managers: (1) US pension programs: CPPIB Emerging Managers, Teacher Retirement System of Texas, UTIMCO Transition Managers, NJ Division of Investment, Illinois Teachers, various state pension systems; (2) FoF emerging manager sleeves: NewAlpha, Rock Creek Group, PAAMCO Prisma, FRM (Man Group); (3) Seeders (different from EMs, provide equity rather than allocation): discussed earlier; (4) Platform firms: Old Mutual Global Investors (now Jupiter), Benchmark Capital; (5) Bank cap intro: GS, MS, JPM, Barclays cap intro teams arrange LP meetings for emerging managers. Emerging manager is defined variously — typically <$1B AUM, <3 years track record. Emerging manager allocations often come with longer lock-ups, lower fees, information rights in exchange for early capital.

## SECTION XX: LAUNCH/SCALING INTERVIEW PREP
Interview questions: (1) "Walk me through launching a $500M long/short equity fund." — Team, strategy, capital, structure, timeline, costs, infrastructure. (2) "What are the key components of a pre-launch build-out?" — Legal structure, FCA authorization, service providers, systems, team, compliance, capital raising. (3) "Explain seed capital economics." — $100-300M initial + 15-25% revenue share for 5-10 years + reduced fees. (4) "What does gardening leave mean and how is it used?" — Non-compete period post-departure; used for legal, team, infrastructure preparation. (5) "What are the typical Year 1 operating costs for a new HF?" — £4-10M for a $300M launch depending on team size. (6) "How does an emerging manager get onto Albourne's approved list?" — Track record, team, process, operations, DDQ/ODD, references, consultant engagement. (7) "What are the biggest reasons launches fail?" — Insufficient capital, early underperformance, key person, regulatory delays, strategy mismatch. (8) "Walk through the FCA authorization process." — Business plan, SMF approvals, compliance framework, financial resources, case officer review. (9) "How does a successful HF scale from $500M to $5B?" — Team expansion, LP diversification, consultant approvals, infrastructure industrialization, capacity management. (10) "What's the difference between a seed investor and an emerging manager allocator?" — Seed provides equity/revenue share; EM allocator provides capital in the fund.

---

# CHAPTER 47 — TALENT, COMPENSATION, CAREERS & POD WARS

## SECTION I: THE LONDON HF TALENT ECOSYSTEM
The London HF talent pool is distinctive in its depth, international character, and mobility. Key sources of HF talent: (1) Investment banks — Goldman Sachs, Morgan Stanley, JPM, Bank of America, Barclays, Citi, Deutsche, UBS, Credit Suisse (historical), BNP Paribas, HSBC, Nomura — both M&A/coverage bankers and sales & trading desks feed HFs; (2) Long-only asset managers — BlackRock, Fidelity, Capital Group, Wellington, T Rowe Price, Baillie Gifford, M&G, Schroders — particularly for fundamental research analysts; (3) Other hedge funds — inter-HF mobility is high, with traders moving between firms every 2-5 years; (4) Management consulting — McKinsey, BCG, Bain send alums to HFs via coverage/analyst roles; (5) Technology companies — Google, Amazon, Meta engineers and data scientists join quant HFs; (6) Quantitative backgrounds — PhD mathematicians, physicists, CS from Oxford, Cambridge, Imperial, LSE, UCL, ETH, MIT, Stanford. London's unique advantage: global time zone, European university access, English language, international cultural pool, 25-year established industry.

## SECTION II: PM COMPENSATION AT MULTI-STRAT PODS
Multi-strategy pod shops have revolutionized PM compensation with formulaic, transparent, aggressive structures: (1) Base salary — $300k-$1M annually; (2) Performance fee — typical 15-25% of net P&L generated by PM's book; (3) Draw structure — guaranteed payments against performance earnings; (4) Stop-loss mechanism — 5% drawdown triggers cut to 50% book, 7-10% drawdown triggers termination; (5) Deferred compensation — portion paid over 2-3 years to retain PMs; (6) Bonus pool alternatives — some firms use discretionary pools rather than formulaic payouts. Example: Millennium PM running $500M book generating $80M gross P&L → 20% performance = $16M PM compensation (before pod operating costs). Top PMs at Citadel, Millennium, Point72 earn $20-100M+ in good years; superstars can exceed $200M. Ken Griffin's total compensation has been reported $4B+ in peak years from Citadel ownership.

## SECTION III: PM GUARANTEED PACKAGES
"Guaranteed" compensation packages are the currency of PM recruitment: (1) Multi-year guarantees — typically 2-3 years, occasionally up to 5; (2) Structure — annual minimum guaranteed bonus plus upside on performance; (3) Size — ranges from $5M (junior PM with track record) to $50M+ (senior PM with proven Sharpe 2+ over 5+ years at prior firm); (4) Balance sheet commitment — guaranteed capital allocation (e.g., "$500M day-1 book") even if AUM raising is slow; (5) Risk capacity — guaranteed VaR budget; (6) Clawback/repayment — some guarantees require multi-year service; (7) Sign-on bonus — immediate cash payment, typically $1-10M for senior hires. Example guaranteed package for senior multi-strat PM move from Millennium to Citadel: $20M sign-on + $15M/year minimum guarantee for 3 years + 25% performance upside + $1B book. Such packages have triggered the "pod wars" as platforms bid aggressively for top PM talent.

## SECTION IV: THE POD WARS
The "pod wars" (2020-2025) refer to intense competition between multi-strategy platforms (Citadel, Millennium, Point72, Balyasny, ExodusPoint, Schonfeld, Eisler Capital, Walleye Capital, Verition, Hudson Bay, Tudor, Centiva, Qube) for PM talent. Drivers: (1) Post-COVID strong returns across pod shops attracting LP capital; (2) AUM growth requiring more PMs to deploy; (3) Pass-through economics making PM costs absorbable; (4) Fear of talent loss if not competitive. Manifestations: (a) Guaranteed packages inflated dramatically — typical mid-level PM 2019 package $5-10M → 2024 package $15-30M; (b) Bidding wars for specific senior PMs (examples: Bobby Jain ex-Millennium launched Jain Global with $5B+ seed, various senior ex-Millennium PMs joined Citadel, ExodusPoint, Schonfeld); (c) Litigation over non-compete enforcement (Millennium vs Schonfeld, Balyasny vs Citadel, various disputes); (d) Cost inflation for all platforms. Industry commentary: Izzy Englander (Millennium founder) reportedly complained about talent cost inflation 2023-2024; Ken Griffin (Citadel) defended aggressive recruiting as necessary for alpha generation.

## SECTION V: JAIN GLOBAL — THE BIGGEST LAUNCH EVER
Bobby Jain (former Co-CIO of Millennium under Izzy Englander) announced his departure 2023 to launch Jain Global, targeting $5-10B seed capital — positioning to be the largest HF launch in history. Launch timeline: announced departure 2023, gardening leave 2024, launched 2024-2025 with $5.3B day-1 capital (largest ever at launch). Strategy: multi-strategy platform competing directly with Millennium/Citadel. Team: Jain hired 40-60 senior investment professionals, many from Millennium, triggering litigation between Millennium and Jain over non-solicit. Investor base: significant LP commitments from SWFs, endowments, pension funds attracted to proven multi-strat operator. The launch represents both the peak of pod wars excess and the institutionalization of alternatives — an LBO-style seeding of a new platform. Performance tracking of Jain Global will be closely watched as benchmark for launch economics.

## SECTION VI: COMPENSATION AT SINGLE-MANAGER FUNDS
Single-manager fund compensation is structured differently from pod shops: (1) Base salary — $150-500k for analysts, $500k-$1.5M for senior PMs/partners; (2) Bonus — discretionary, based on firm performance, individual contribution, team dynamics; (3) Partnership equity — senior professionals may receive equity stakes in management company (typically 1-10% of firm for senior partners); (4) Performance fee allocation — portion of fund performance fees allocated to key professionals; (5) Deferred compensation — retention mechanisms via vesting. Single-manager compensation is typically lower than pod shops at similar levels but offers equity upside if firm succeeds. Example: senior analyst at $3B long/short equity fund earning $1.5M base + $2-5M bonus + partnership equity worth $10-50M at strong outcomes.

## SECTION VII: ANALYST CAREER PROGRESSION
HF analyst career path: (1) Junior analyst / associate (years 0-3): $100-250k base, $50-500k bonus, covering 5-15 names, doing model work, screening, thesis drafting; (2) Senior analyst (years 3-7): $200-400k base, $500k-$2M bonus, covering 15-30 names, generating investment ideas with clear thesis, portfolio responsibility; (3) Sector head (years 7-12): $400-700k base, $1-5M bonus, managing sub-team, broad sector coverage, material P&L contribution; (4) Partner/PM (years 10+): equity stake, performance participation, strategic input, direct book management; (5) Founding partner/CIO (years 15+): equity majority, firm leadership. Progression is merit-based — generating investable ideas that make money. Typical progression time: faster at smaller/nimbler firms, slower at large established shops.

## SECTION VIII: RECRUITING PIPELINE FOR HFs
HF recruiting pipelines: (1) Top banks' M&A / leveraged finance 2-3 year programs (GS TMT, MS M&A, JPM Leveraged Finance) — highly competitive exit to private equity and hedge funds; (2) Sales & trading desks — direct transfer to HF trading, especially in rates, FX, credit; (3) Consulting — McKinsey/BCG/Bain with specific industry coverage for sector specialists; (4) Long-only AM — Fidelity/Capital/Wellington analysts for fundamental research roles; (5) Technology — Jane Street, Citadel Securities, DE Shaw pipeline for quant and tech roles; (6) PhD programs — for quant roles at Renaissance, Two Sigma, DE Shaw; (7) Lateral HF moves — inter-HF mobility increases at 2-5 year intervals. Search firms specialize in HF recruitment: Glocap, Sheffield Haworth, Options Group, PER, Heidrick & Struggles, Egon Zehnder, Spencer Stuart.

## SECTION IX: INTERVIEW PROCESS
HF interview processes are rigorous: (1) CV screening — competitive (1-5% acceptance rate); (2) Initial phone screen — fit, background, motivation; (3) Case study / modeling test — typically given 1-7 days to prepare a pitch on a stock or trade; (4) Technical interviews — valuation mechanics, modeling, market knowledge; (5) Investment pitch presentation — present to PM/partners, defend against hostile questioning; (6) Reference checks — both formal and back-channel; (7) Final interview with founder/CIO — culture fit, long-term vision; (8) Offer negotiation. The stock pitch is the critical test — must demonstrate: investment thesis, valuation framework, catalysts, risks, portfolio fit, intellectual rigor. Top HFs expect candidates to spend 40-100 hours preparing 2-3 high-quality pitches.

## SECTION X: THE "STOCK PITCH" FRAMEWORK
Standard HF stock pitch framework: (1) Investment summary — long or short, current price, target price, timeframe, conviction level; (2) Thesis — key reasons the market is wrong (typically 3-5 points); (3) Business overview — 2-3 minutes on what the company does; (4) Financial analysis — revenue, margins, FCF, balance sheet, capital allocation; (5) Valuation — DCF, multiples, sum-of-parts, historical context; (6) Variant view — what's the consensus, why is it wrong, what will change; (7) Catalysts — specific events that will unlock value (earnings, product launch, divestiture, management change); (8) Risks — bear case scenario, hedging; (9) Position sizing — how much, entry plan, exit plan; (10) Q&A defense. Top pitches feature: original insight (not consensus), data-driven analysis, quantitative rigor, awareness of risks, clear view of catalyst and timeframe.

## SECTION XI: TRADER COMPENSATION
Trader compensation at macro / rates / FX desks: (1) Base: $300k-$1M; (2) Performance: 10-20% of net P&L typically; (3) Stop-loss mechanisms similar to PMs; (4) Risk budget allocation. Traders at Brevan, Rokos, Caxton, Kirkoswald earn $5-50M+ in good years. Very senior rates/FX traders with consistent Sharpe 2+ can command similar packages to equity PMs at multi-strat platforms. Difference: traders typically work on shorter-horizon trades with more immediate P&L attribution than fundamental equity researchers.

## SECTION XII: QUANT RESEARCHER COMPENSATION
Quant researcher compensation at systematic shops: (1) Base: $200-500k for junior, $500k-$1.5M for senior; (2) Bonus: based on alpha/strategy contribution — $500k to $10M+; (3) Total: $1-10M typical for experienced quants, $20M+ for top researchers at Renaissance, Two Sigma, DE Shaw, Citadel; (4) PhD premium — particularly for researchers with strong academic credentials. Renaissance Medallion insiders reputedly earn $10-100M+ from Medallion performance (though fund is closed to outsiders). Jane Street, Jump Trading, Citadel Securities compete aggressively for quant talent. Entry-level quant trader at Jane Street: $400-600k all-in for first year out of PhD.

## SECTION XIII: NON-COMPETE AND NON-SOLICIT CLAUSES
Restrictive covenants in HF employment: (1) Non-compete — cannot work at competitor for X months after departure (3-24 months typical, longest at top firms); (2) Non-solicit — cannot recruit from former firm (12-36 months typical); (3) Non-deal — cannot trade former firm's strategies (variable); (4) Confidentiality — indefinite protection of proprietary information; (5) Gardening leave — paid or unpaid period during notice. Enforceability: UK courts enforce reasonable restrictions if protected legitimate business interests; US (particularly California, which voids most non-competes) more skeptical. Recent litigation: Millennium vs Jain Global (multiple cases), Schonfeld vs Millennium, Balyasny vs multiple departed PMs, Citadel vs Surveyor (early case). FTC non-compete ban (proposed 2023, blocked 2024) would have eliminated most non-competes — still uncertain regulatory future.

## SECTION XIV: DIVERSITY IN HFs
HF industry diversity: historically male-dominated with limited female, ethnic minority, and LGBTQ+ representation at senior levels. Improvement trajectory: (1) Pension fund pressure for diverse manager selection; (2) Formal diversity initiatives at larger firms; (3) Dedicated programs (100 Women in Finance, Girls Who Invest, Women in Hedge Funds); (4) Emerging diverse-owned firms; (5) Educational pipeline efforts. Pace of change remains slow, particularly in senior PM roles and firm ownership. UK/EU regulatory reporting (Women in Finance Charter, gender pay gap reporting) provides some transparency. The industry has far to go but has begun addressing these issues more systematically.

## SECTION XV: LIFESTYLE AND WORK-LIFE
HF careers are demanding: (1) Hours — typical workweek 60-80 hours at analyst/associate level, 50-70 at senior level; (2) Early starts — markets open 8 AM London, requires 6-7 AM arrival for many; (3) Travel — LP meetings, conferences, company visits; (4) Stress — performance pressure, market volatility, firm survival concerns; (5) Compensation — high but volatile (bonus-heavy); (6) Job security — lower than long-only due to performance-based culture and pod-shop stop-losses. Burnout is common. Career duration: many HF professionals target 10-20 years active career before transitioning to family office, personal investing, non-profit, or other pursuits. The high compensation enables earlier financial independence than most careers.

## SECTION XVI: LONDON VS NEW YORK VS ASIA
London vs New York HF comparison: (1) London — European time zone advantage, closer to macro/rates/FX markets, Mayfair prestige, UK tax regime (IME), Cayman structure dominant; (2) New York — largest HF market, closest to US equity markets, highest concentration of multi-strat platforms, more pass-through and large launches; (3) Greenwich CT — suburban HF cluster for NY-adjacent funds, historically more single-manager; (4) Hong Kong — Asian HF hub, declining importance post-2020 political changes, shift to Singapore; (5) Singapore — rising as Asian HF hub, government support via VCC structure, growing Asian SWF capital base; (6) Tokyo — smaller but stable, focused on Japan-specific strategies; (7) Dubai — growing fast post-2020, attracting ex-London/ex-Moscow talent. Career mobility between centres is common; many HF professionals work in multiple locations across career.

## SECTION XVII: EXIT PATHS FROM HF CAREER
HF career exits: (1) Family office — senior HF professionals convert to managing their own wealth plus friends'/family's; (2) Single-family office — working for a single HNW family (less pressure, steady compensation); (3) Investment adviser/consultant — advising LPs, endowments, pensions; (4) Non-profit/impact investing — senior HF alums often transition to philanthropic investing; (5) Academic/research — some PhDs return to universities; (6) Financial services operating roles — CFO, COO, CIO at banks, asset managers; (7) Entrepreneurship — starting non-financial businesses with accumulated wealth; (8) Retirement — financial independence earlier than other careers. Alumni networks (Tiger Cubs, Goldman alumni, Millennium alumni, etc.) remain active throughout post-HF career.

## SECTION XVIII: LONDON UNIVERSITY AND EDUCATION PIPELINE
UK universities feeding London HF industry: (1) Oxford — PPE, economics, mathematics; (2) Cambridge — economics, mathematics, natural sciences; (3) Imperial — mathematics, physics, computer science, engineering; (4) LSE — economics, finance, mathematics; (5) University College London (UCL) — economics, mathematics, computer science; (6) Warwick — mathematics, economics; (7) Edinburgh — mathematics, economics; (8) Bath — mathematics, economics. International universities: ETH Zurich, EPFL, MIT, Stanford, Harvard, Princeton, Yale, Chicago, Wharton, Columbia all feed London HFs. Specific degree preferences: mathematics and physics for quant roles, economics and finance for fundamental, computer science for tech and data roles, PhDs for top quant shops. London HFs tend to be meritocratic — academic pedigree matters but performance matters more.

## SECTION XIX: THE "2 AND 20" MILLIONAIRE FACTORY CHALLENGE
Critics have long argued that 2 and 20 fee structure creates millionaires of HF managers rather than investors. Industry response: (1) Strong net-of-fee returns have still delivered alpha over market; (2) Performance is demanded — poor performers get fired quickly; (3) Capital discipline — successful HFs return capital to maintain capacity; (4) Alignment — significant founder capital in funds. The data: aggregate HF industry has delivered ~8% annualized net returns since 2010, vs S&P 500 ~13% annualized, vs 60/40 ~8%. The alpha debate: HF alpha (risk-adjusted outperformance) exists for top quartile managers but is elusive at industry level. Fee compression has addressed some concerns — fees have fallen ~20% industry-wide since 2008. Pass-through structures are in some sense more transparent but can be more expensive.

## SECTION XX: TALENT/COMP INTERVIEW PREP
Interview questions: (1) "Walk through compensation at a multi-strat pod shop." — Base + performance formula (15-25% of P&L) + stop-loss mechanism + deferred portion. (2) "What is a guaranteed package?" — Multi-year minimum compensation for hired PM, typically $5-30M for 2-3 years. (3) "Explain the pod wars." — Competition for PM talent among Citadel, Millennium, Point72, Balyasny etc., inflating comp packages and triggering litigation. (4) "What is Jain Global?" — Bobby Jain's launch, largest HF launch ever ($5.3B day-1), ex-Millennium team, triggered Millennium-Jain litigation. (5) "How should I approach a stock pitch?" — Thesis, business, financials, valuation, variant view, catalysts, risks, position sizing, Q&A. (6) "What are non-compete clauses?" — Post-departure restrictions on working at competitors; enforceability varies by jurisdiction. (7) "Why is London a major HF hub?" — Time zone, tax regime (IME), Mayfair ecosystem, talent pool, regulatory infrastructure, global clients. (8) "What is the typical analyst career progression?" — Junior → senior → sector head → PM → partner over 10-15 years. (9) "How do pod shops manage PM risk?" — Stop-loss mechanisms (5% down = 50% book, 7-10% = termination), daily risk monitoring, centralised CRO oversight. (10) "What do LPs look for in emerging managers?" — Track record, team, process, infrastructure, capital discipline, alignment.

---

# CHAPTER 48 — HEDGE FUND INTERVIEW PREP & CASE STUDIES

## SECTION I: INTERVIEW PROCESS ARCHITECTURE
The London HF interview process differs by firm type and seniority: (1) Multi-strat platforms (Citadel, Millennium, Point72, Balyasny) — highly structured, multiple rounds, case studies, modeling tests, investment pitches, risk assessment, culture fit; (2) Single-manager funds — less formal, founder-led, pitch-heavy, culture-focused; (3) Quant shops (Renaissance, Two Sigma, DE Shaw, Jane Street, Citadel Securities) — technical brainteasers, programming tests, probability/statistics, research paper discussion; (4) Macro funds (Brevan, Rokos, Caxton) — market knowledge, trade ideas, macro views; (5) Credit/distressed (Elliott, Oaktree, Silver Point) — cap structure analysis, legal, restructuring knowledge. Timelines: 4-8 weeks typical, 3-5 rounds, 8-15 hours of total interview time, plus 20-80 hours of case study preparation. Offers typically made within a week of final round.

## SECTION II: STANDARD FIT QUESTIONS
Common fit questions: (1) "Walk me through your CV." — 2-3 minute summary, key accomplishments, why each role; (2) "Why hedge funds?" — Intellectual challenge, meritocracy, direct market engagement, alignment of rewards with contribution; (3) "Why this firm specifically?" — Research the firm, mention specific strategies, PMs, recent trades, culture, founder philosophy; (4) "Why now?" — Career timing, readiness for this role; (5) "Tell me about a time you were wrong." — Intellectual honesty, ability to learn; (6) "What's your biggest weakness?" — Self-awareness, development plan; (7) "Where do you see yourself in 5 years?" — Ambition, realistic; (8) "Tell me about a recent investment idea." — Opens pitch discussion; (9) "What do you read?" — Show curiosity; (10) "What's your view on [current market topic]?" — Engaged with markets. Key principles: be specific, show intellectual rigor, avoid clichés, demonstrate genuine interest in the firm.

## SECTION III: TECHNICAL BRAINTEASERS
Common quant/trading interview brainteasers: (1) "You flip a fair coin until heads. What is the expected number of flips?" — Answer 2 (geometric distribution); (2) "Three ants on a triangle, each walks randomly to a neighbor. Probability no ant collides?" — 1/4 (two scenarios: all clockwise, all counterclockwise, out of 8 total); (3) "100 prisoners and 100 boxes containing their numbers. Each must find their number in 50 box openings. Strategy?" — Cycle-following strategy gives ~31% success vs (1/2)^100 ≈ 0 for random; (4) "A stick is broken at two random points. Probability pieces form a triangle?" — 1/4; (5) "Two envelopes, one contains 2x the other. You pick one. Should you switch?" — No (paradox arises from improper prior); (6) "Random walk on a line starting at 0, absorbing barriers at +N and -N. Expected time to absorption?" — N²; (7) "You have a biased coin but don't know the bias. How do you make a fair decision?" — Flip twice, HT=heads, TH=tails, repeat if HH or TT (Von Neumann); (8) "Expected value of highest of 3 uniform [0,1] draws?" — 3/4. Quant interviews test mathematical intuition, not memorization.

## SECTION IV: MARKET KNOWLEDGE QUESTIONS
Current market knowledge questions (as of 2026 context): (1) "What's the current Fed funds rate? ECB rate? BoE rate?" — Know current levels; (2) "What's the 10-year Treasury yield?" — Current level; (3) "What's the USD/EUR rate?" — Major FX levels; (4) "Describe the yield curve currently." — Shape, inversion status, steepening/flattening; (5) "What are the biggest market themes right now?" — Be prepared with 3-5 themes; (6) "What's your view on AI/tech valuations?" — Consider productivity, capex, competition; (7) "How do you think about US-China relations?" — Trade, tariffs, tech decoupling; (8) "What's happening in commercial real estate?" — Office distress, cap rate trends; (9) "Current state of crypto?" — BTC ETF flows, regulatory status; (10) "What's your China view?" — Economic trajectory, property sector, geopolitics. Know specific numbers, not just narratives. Interview prep: track key market data daily for 4-6 weeks before interviews.

## SECTION V: CASE STUDY — EQUITY LONG PITCH
Model equity long pitch structure: "I recommend buying XYZ at $45, 12-month target $65 (+44%), based on 3 key points: (1) Mispriced cyclical recovery — consensus assumes continued margin pressure but recent pricing actions suggest 400bp EBITDA margin recovery by 2026 worth $8/share of upside; (2) Hidden value — 35% stake in subsidiary ABC is marked at cost but recent private rounds value it 3x book; worth $7/share; (3) Capital allocation inflection — CEO commentary suggests $2B buyback authorization coming, 12% of market cap — historically strong signal. Catalysts: Q3 earnings (October), investor day (December), buyback announcement. Bear case: recession cuts revenues 15%, margins stay flat, target $35 (-22%). Risk/reward: 44%/22% = 2x asymmetry. Position size: 3% NAV. Hedge: short sector ETF at 50% beta. Key diligence: channel checks confirmed demand recovery; management meetings signal buyback commitment; model supports margin thesis. What could prove me wrong: competitor price war; raw material shock; management change."

## SECTION VI: CASE STUDY — EQUITY SHORT PITCH
Model equity short pitch structure: "I recommend shorting ABC at $120, 12-month target $60 (-50%), based on: (1) Aggressive accounting — DSO increased from 60 to 95 days despite flat revenue; receivables growing 3x faster than sales suggests channel stuffing; (2) Competitive erosion — new entrant XYZ taking market share (XYZ up 15% YoY, ABC flat in same segment); gross margin likely to decline 300bp; (3) Leverage — net debt/EBITDA 4.5x vs historical 2x, covenants at 5x, approaching violation; refinancing 2027 at much higher rates; (4) Insider selling — C-suite sold $150M stock in past 6 months. Catalyst: Q3 earnings likely to show receivables blow-up; covenant pressure; refinancing risk. Short thesis timeframe: 6-12 months. Bear case target $40 (-67%). Bull case (short gets squeezed): management raises equity at depressed price (dilution), some downside capped at $100. Borrow check: 25bps borrow rate, 5% utilization — easy borrow. Position size: 2% NAV. Hedge: long sector ETF at 30%."

## SECTION VII: CASE STUDY — MERGER ARB
Model merger arb pitch: "Microsoft-Activision deal (MSFT buying ATVI for $95 cash, announced January 2022). Current situation: deal pending at $85 spot (June 2023), $10 gross spread = 11.8% gross return. Key regulatory issues: (a) UK CMA initially blocked April 2023, Microsoft revised cloud gaming remedies August 2023, CMA approved October 2023; (b) FTC sued to block, lost federal court July 2023; (c) EU approved with cloud remedies May 2023; (d) China approved. Completion probability estimate: 85% post-CMA approval. Break case: deal falls through at $65 (-24% from current). Expected return: 0.85 × 11.8% + 0.15 × (-24%) = 6.4%. Annualized (3 months to expected close): ~25%. Position size: 4% NAV given high conviction post-CMA. Funding cost: long 4.5% financing drag. Actual result: deal closed October 2023, full $95 realized, arb made full spread. Lessons: regulatory risk dominates modern mega-deals; patience through regulatory uncertainty can capture substantial premium."

## SECTION VIII: CASE STUDY — MACRO TRADE
Model macro trade: "I am bearish 10-year Treasuries, targeting yield rising from 4.0% to 4.75% over next 3 months. Drivers: (1) Fiscal deficit at 6.5% GDP, Treasury issuance ramping to $2T annual, supply pressure; (2) Sticky services inflation with CPI ex-shelter stalling at 3.5%, Fed on hold; (3) Term premium re-emerging after decade of compression. Trade expression: short 10-year Treasury futures (TYZ5) at 110-16 (implied yield 4.02%), target 106-00 (yield 4.70%), stop 112-00 (yield 3.80%). P&L on $100M notional: target +$4.5M, stop -$1.5M. Duration hedge: pair with long 2-year (TU futures) to isolate 2s10s steepener. Alternative expression: 10-year swap receiver unwind / payer, or long TLT puts. Size: $100M notional, equivalent to ~$650k DV01. Risk budget: 15bps of fund VaR. Catalysts: Q3 refunding announcement (August), Jackson Hole speech (August), CPI prints. What would prove me wrong: material growth weakening, Fed dovish pivot, flight-to-quality bid."

## SECTION IX: CASE STUDY — CREDIT/DISTRESSED
Model distressed pitch: "I recommend the XYZ 7.5% 2028 senior unsecured notes trading at 65, representing 15% YTW and opportunity for recovery to 90+ over 18-24 months. Company situation: highly levered consumer business, 6.5x net leverage, EBITDA declining, debt maturing 2028 requires refinancing. Thesis: (1) Asset value protects bonds — DCF recovery analysis values enterprise at 4x current EBITDA = $2.1B, well above $1.4B total debt; (2) Consensual restructuring likely — sponsor wants to preserve equity via amend-and-extend, willing to pay bondholders par with extension; (3) Catalyst — refinancing window 18 months out will force action. Recovery analysis: liquidation 85c, going concern 92c, consensual 100c. Weighted recovery 91c, IRR from 65 = 45% over 18 months. Downsides: credit deterioration accelerates, covenant litigation drags out, senior secured takes hostile action. Position size: 3% NAV across multiple instruments — senior unsecured 65, subordinated 35 (to capture additional recovery upside), short equity as hedge."

## SECTION X: CASE STUDY — SECTOR DEEP DIVE
"Walk me through UK banking sector view." Example answer: "UK banking sector (FTSE 350 Banks) consists of Lloyds, NatWest (formerly RBS), Barclays, HSBC, Standard Chartered, and smaller specialty banks. Current dynamics: (1) NIM expansion from higher rates peaked 2023-2024 as deposit betas catch up; (2) Credit quality deteriorating modestly from cyclical tights; (3) Regulatory capital Basel 3.1 implementation impacts (phasing-in through 2030); (4) M&A speculation around smaller players (Virgin Money, Metro Bank, OSB). My bias: long Barclays (undervalued vs US peers, restructuring complete, 0.6x P/TBV) vs short Standard Chartered (EM exposure, weak RoE, 0.5x P/TBV) — both at depressed multiples but Barclays has clearer path to re-rating. Catalyst: Barclays investor update Q1, Basel 3.1 finalisation, buyback announcements. Risks: UK recession, political intervention, M&A disrupting relative value."

## SECTION XI: TECHNICAL VALUATION QUESTIONS
Technical valuation questions: (1) "Walk me through a DCF." — Project 5-10 year FCF, terminal value (Gordon growth or exit multiple), discount at WACC, sum to enterprise value, subtract net debt = equity value, divide by shares = per share; (2) "What's the WACC?" — Weight of debt × (1-tax) × cost of debt + weight of equity × cost of equity; cost of equity via CAPM = Rf + beta × ERP; (3) "Difference between EV and equity value?" — EV includes all providers of capital (equity, debt, minority interest, preferred) minus cash; (4) "Why use EV/EBITDA vs P/E?" — EV/EBITDA is capital structure neutral, ignores D&A differences, better for capital-intensive; (5) "What's the difference between levered and unlevered FCF?" — Unlevered before debt service, levered after; (6) "Explain accretion/dilution in M&A." — If P/E of acquirer > P/E of target (reciprocal earnings yield < financing cost), deal is dilutive; (7) "What's a good ROIC?" — Above WACC creates value; typically >10% is good for most industries.

## SECTION XII: PROBABILITY AND STATS QUESTIONS
Probability/stats questions for quant interviews: (1) "Central limit theorem." — Sum of n iid random variables approaches normal distribution as n→∞; (2) "Bayes' theorem." — P(A|B) = P(B|A)P(A)/P(B); used for updating beliefs with new information; (3) "Markov chain." — Memoryless stochastic process; transition matrix; stationary distribution; (4) "Brownian motion." — Continuous stochastic process with independent Gaussian increments; building block of Black-Scholes; (5) "Maximum likelihood estimation." — Parameter values maximizing likelihood function of observed data; (6) "OLS assumptions." — Linearity, independence, homoscedasticity, normality of errors; (7) "R² vs adjusted R²." — Adjusted penalizes adding variables; (8) "Type I vs Type II error." — Type I: reject true null (false positive); Type II: fail to reject false null (false negative).

## SECTION XIII: OPTIONS AND GREEKS QUESTIONS
Options questions: (1) "Explain Black-Scholes." — European option pricing model assuming log-normal stock prices, constant volatility and interest rates, no dividends (extension available); formula C = S×N(d1) - K×e^(-rt)×N(d2); (2) "What are the Greeks?" — Delta (∂C/∂S), Gamma (∂²C/∂S²), Vega (∂C/∂σ), Theta (∂C/∂t), Rho (∂C/∂r); (3) "ATM delta?" — Approximately 0.5; (4) "What is put-call parity?" — C - P = S - K×e^(-rt); enforces no-arbitrage between puts and calls; (5) "Explain vol smile." — Implied vol varies by strike; OTM puts typically have higher IV than ATM due to demand for downside protection; (6) "What's gamma scalping?" — Long gamma position profits from large moves in either direction; delta-hedge locks in gains; (7) "Explain vega exposure." — Long options = long vega (benefit from vol increase); short options = short vega; (8) "What's a delta-neutral portfolio?" — Net delta = 0; insensitive to small stock moves but has gamma/vega exposure.

## SECTION XIV: FIXED INCOME TECHNICAL QUESTIONS
Fixed income technical: (1) "What is duration?" — Price sensitivity to interest rates; Modified duration = Macaulay duration / (1+y); (2) "What is convexity?" — Second-order rate sensitivity; positive for most bonds, negative for callable bonds; (3) "Explain the yield curve." — Relationship between yield and maturity; normal upward sloping, inverted downward; (4) "What's a spread trade?" — Long one bond, short another; captures relative value without duration exposure; (5) "Swap spread = ?" — Swap rate minus corresponding Treasury yield; reflects credit/liquidity in swap market; (6) "Explain the basis trade." — Cash bond vs corresponding futures contract; captured via implied repo rate; (7) "What is OAS?" — Option-adjusted spread; spread after accounting for embedded options; (8) "Explain CDS." — Credit default swap; protection buyer pays premium, receives par minus recovery if default occurs; (9) "DV01?" — Dollar value of 1 basis point move in yield.

## SECTION XV: BEHAVIORAL QUESTIONS
Behavioral questions (STAR format expected — Situation, Task, Action, Result): (1) "Tell me about a difficult decision you made." — Pick genuine example, show decision framework; (2) "Describe a conflict with a colleague and how you handled it." — Professional maturity; (3) "Time you took initiative." — Leadership potential; (4) "Biggest failure and what you learned." — Intellectual honesty; (5) "Time you had to persuade someone with an unpopular view." — Communication; (6) "How do you handle pressure?" — Stress management. Principles: (a) Be specific with examples; (b) Show self-awareness; (c) Focus on what you did, not team; (d) Demonstrate learning; (e) Keep stories concise (2-3 minutes).

## SECTION XVI: FIRM-SPECIFIC PREPARATION
Firm-specific research essentials: (1) Founder background — read biographies, interviews, letters; (2) Strategy history — know key trades, wins, losses; (3) Recent news — AUM, team changes, fund launches; (4) Culture — corporate values, work style, famous stories; (5) Investment philosophy — how they view markets, process; (6) Recent portfolio positioning — from 13F filings if public, from interviews; (7) Competitor context — how they position vs peers. Specific firm knowledge examples: "Brevan Howard runs discretionary macro with multiple strategy pods under Alan Howard's centralized risk framework"; "Marshall Wace pioneered the TOPS fundamental analyst ranking system for systematic alpha capture"; "TCI is run by Chris Hohn with concentrated long/short equity + activist campaigns"; "Lansdowne historically had 20-30 longs held 2-3 years with deep fundamental research but closed LDMF in 2020".

## SECTION XVII: QUESTIONS TO ASK INTERVIEWERS
Strong questions to ask interviewers (shows you're engaged and thinking): (1) "How does the investment team debate ideas — is there structured challenge or collegial discussion?" (2) "What's the biggest lesson the firm has learned in the past 2-3 years?" (3) "How do you think about capacity for this strategy?" (4) "What's the career path for someone in this role over 5-7 years?" (5) "How does the firm manage drawdowns?" (6) "What separates successful people at this firm from those who don't work out?" (7) "What would you do differently if you were starting at the firm today?" (8) "How do you balance short-term P&L pressure with long-term thesis development?" Avoid: (a) Questions answerable from public sources; (b) Salary/benefits questions early; (c) Questions demonstrating lack of research; (d) Overly aggressive challenge.

## SECTION XVIII: RED FLAGS IN INTERVIEWS
Red flags interviewers watch for: (1) Lack of specific examples — generic answers suggest weak experience; (2) Poor market knowledge — suggests limited engagement; (3) Weak stock pitch — suggests limited investment thinking; (4) Evasiveness on failures — lack of self-awareness; (5) Negative attitudes toward prior employers — concerning professional behavior; (6) Lack of intellectual curiosity — shows up in reading, hobbies, side interests; (7) Poor analytical rigor under pressure; (8) Inability to handle pushback; (9) Unrealistic compensation expectations; (10) Failure to do firm-specific research. Best candidates demonstrate: (a) Deep market knowledge; (b) Structured thinking under pressure; (c) Humility about limits; (d) Genuine intellectual curiosity; (e) Grit and work ethic; (f) Professional maturity.

## SECTION XIX: NEGOTIATION DYNAMICS
Offer negotiation principles: (1) Have competing offers if possible — strongest negotiating position; (2) Know market rates — reference Sheffield Haworth, Glocap, Options Group compensation reports; (3) Focus on total compensation (base + bonus + equity + benefits) not just base; (4) Timing matters — negotiate before accepting, very hard to change post-acceptance; (5) Written offers are binding — verbal discussions less so; (6) Partnership equity — critical for long-term wealth building; (7) Sign-on bonus — negotiate for lost bonus from prior firm; (8) Vesting terms — ensure protection if firm situation changes. Avoid: (a) Nickel-and-diming on small amounts; (b) Threats you can't back up; (c) Deadline-based pressure that breaks relationships; (d) Aggressive posture that signals difficult personality.

## SECTION XX: FINAL PREPARATION CHECKLIST
Interview preparation checklist (6-8 weeks before): (1) Complete CV tailoring — quantified accomplishments, technical skills; (2) Prepare 2-3 polished stock pitches (1 long, 1 short, 1 non-equity if relevant) — 10-page memos with executive summary; (3) Read firm materials — founder letters, interviews, press, any published research; (4) Study industry/sector relevant to firm — deep understanding of 2-3 key themes; (5) Review fundamentals — valuation, accounting, financial modeling, corporate finance; (6) Practice brainteasers — 30-50 problems to build pattern recognition; (7) Review current markets — rates, FX, credit, equities, commodities; (8) Read recent "must-reads" — FT Alphaville, Grant's Interest Rate Observer, Bloomberg Opinion, recent HF investor letters; (9) Practice verbal delivery — time yourself on 2-3 minute and 5 minute responses; (10) Prepare questions for each interviewer. Sleep well, eat well, dress appropriately (Mayfair business casual for HFs, more formal for banks). Arrive 15 minutes early. Bring multiple copies of CV and any pitch materials. Send concise thank-you notes within 24 hours.

---

# SERIES EPILOGUE

Chapters 32-48 extend the G2G London hedge fund series with 17 hyper-dense cram sheets covering the complete institutional, strategic, operational, regulatory, and career landscape of the modern London hedge fund industry. Combined with Ch30 (Hedge Funds general) and Ch31 (Activist Hedge Funds), the series now comprises 19 interconnected chapters providing a single source of truth for elite interview preparation. The structure: Ch32-37 cover specific strategy deep-dives (macro, quant, multi-strat, long/short, credit, event-driven); Ch38 extends to EM and digital assets; Ch39-44 cover operational and regulatory infrastructure (PB, legal, regulation, LPs, risk, ops); Ch45 catalogues blowups; Ch46-47 cover launching/scaling and talent; Ch48 synthesizes interview preparation. Each chapter follows the same 20-section format established in earlier chapters, delivering London-focused, hyper-technical, quantitative, and narrative content with specific firm names, AUM figures, historical trades, formulas, and regulatory detail. The ethos throughout: absolute hardcore depth, assume elite readership, no length cap, no topic too complex to cover.
